## Hydroborate Solid Electrolytes for High-Voltage All-Solid-State Batteries

## THÈSE

présentée à la Faculté des sciences de l'Université de Genève pour obtenir le grade de Docteur ès sciences, mention chimie par

## Ryo ASAKURA

de

Gunma (Japan)

Thèse N o  5580

GENÈVE Atelier d'Impression ReproMail 2021

## Abstract

Lithium-ion batteries have become ubiquitous as electrochemical energy storage devices for portable electronics. They represent a key technology for the decarbonization of the transport and power sector by enabling electric vehicles and the integration of renewable energy into the electric grid. However, their physicochemical limits and safety concerns motivate research on next-generation all-solid-state batteries, which promise higher energy and power densities, and improved operational safety.

The  key  component  of  an  all-solid-state  battery  is  the  ion-conducting  solid electrolyte, for which several material classes are currently under investigation. Among them, hydroborates draw attention as an emerging material class with high Li + and Na + conductivity,  compatibility  with  alkali  metal  anodes,  and  facile all-solid-state cell fabrication by cold pressing. However, the extension of the cell voltage to 4 V remains a major challenge as factors limiting the thermodynamic and kinetic stability of hydroborate electrolytes have not yet been investigated and understood in detail.

The goal of this thesis is therefore to assess the electrochemical and thermal stability of hydroborate solid electrolytes and their compatibility with cathodes for the implementation into high-voltage all-solid-state batteries with a cell voltage &gt;4 V.

In  this  PhD  thesis,  a  robust  methodology  is  established  to  determine  the electrochemical  oxidative  stability  of  hydroborate  electrolytes  based  on  voltammetric methods,  employing  LiBH4  as  a  model  solid  electrolyte.  The  method  is  shown  to  be generally applicable to hydroborate solid electrolytes. By extending this method, certain hydroborate electrolytes are found to be capable of effectively passivating the interface with 4 V-class cathodes. Specifically, the in-situ passivation of the interface between the mixedanion closo -hydroborate solid electrolyte Na 4 (CB11H12)2(B12 H12) and the 4 V-class cathode Na3(VOPO4)2F is discussed in detail. Upon electrochemical polarization, the cathode/electrolyte interface forms a self-passivating interphase layer, enabling for the first time  long-term  cycling  of  a  4 V  hydroborate-based  all-solid-state  battery  at  room temperature  with  an excellent capacity and  energy  retention for &gt;800 cycles. In combination with a sodium metal anode, this cell records the highest average discharge cell voltage achieved so far with a single solid electrolyte and the highest specific energy on a cathode active material basis among all reported all-solid-state sodium batteries.

Besides  the  electrochemical  stability,  the  thermal  stability  of  the  electrolyte  in contact with the electrode materials is key for the operational safety of a battery, especially when the battery is fully charged. We demonstrate the high intrinsic thermal stability of closohydroborate solid electrolytes in contact with cathode active materials in the charged and discharged states, serving as a precondition to guarantee the safe operation of an allsolid-state battery technology over a wide temperature range and for fast charging.

The  results  of  this  thesis  demonstrate  that  hydroborates  represent  a  promising material  class  of  solid  electrolytes  for  enabling  a  competitive  all-solid-state  battery technology.

## Résumé

Les batteries lithium-ion sont devenues omniprésentes comme porteurs d'énergie électrochimiques pour  les  appareils  électroniques  portables.  Ces  batteries  permettent  également  d'alimenter  des véhicules électriques et d'intégrer l'énergie renouvelable dans les réseaux d'électricité. Ces batteries sont ainsi une technologie clé en vue de la décarbonisation des secteurs du trafic et de l'électricité. Cependant,  les  limitations  physico-chimiques  et  les  problèmes  liés  à  la  sécurité  motivent  des recherches sur une nouvelle génération de batteries entièrement solides, qui promettent des densités d'énergie et de puissance supérieures, ainsi qu'une sécurité opérationnelle accrue.

Le composant clé d'une batterie entièrement solide est l'électrolyte conducteur d'ions solide, pour lequel diverses classes de matériaux ont été proposées. Parmi celles-ci, les hydroborates attirent l'attention comme une classe de matériaux émergeante de par leur conductivité élevée pour Li +  et Na + , leur compatibilité avec des anodes de métaux alcalins et leur fabrication facile de cellules entièrement solides par compression à froid. Cependant, l'extension de la tension de la cellule à 4 V reste un défi majeur, étant donné que des facteurs tels que la stabilité thermodynamique et cinétique n'ont jusqu'à présent que peu été étudiés et compris en détail.

Le  but  de  cette  thèse  est  donc  de  déterminer  la  stabilité  électrochimique  et  thermique  des électrolytes solides d'hydroborate et leur compatibilité avec des cathodes afin de les implémenter dans une batterie entièrement solide à haut potentiel avec une tension de la cellule supérieure à 4 V.

Dans cette thèse, une méthodologie robuste pour déterminer la stabilité oxydative électrochimique d'électrolytes d'hydroborates basée sur des méthodes voltammétriques est établie en utilisant le composé LiBH4 comme conducteur ionique solide modèle. Cette méthode est cependant applicable en  général  aux  électrolytes  solides  d'hydroborates.  L'extension  de  cette  méthode  a  permis  de découvrir  que  certains  électrolytes  d'hydroborates  étaient  capables  de  passiver  efficacement l'interface avec des cathodes de la classe de 4 V. Plus spécifiquement, nous étudions la passivation in-situ de l'interface entre l'électrolyte solide di-anionique Na4(CB11H12)2(B12H12) et la cathode de la classe 4 V Na3(VOPO4)2F. Suite à une polarisation électrochimique, l'interface cathode/électrolyte forme une couche d'interphase auto-passivante, ce qui a permis pour la première fois de cycler une batterie de 4 V basée sur des hydroborates à température ambiante avec une excellente capacité et rétention  d'énergie  pour  plus  que  800 cycles.  En  combinaison  avec  une  anode  de  sodium métallique, cette cellule démontre la plus haute tension de décharge obtenue à ce jour avec un électrolyte solide, et la plus haute énergie spécifique sur la base du matériau actif de cathode parmi toutes les batteries au sodium entièrement solides reportées dans la littérature.

A part la stabilité électrochimique, c'est la stabilité thermique de l'électrolyte en contact avec les matériaux d'électrode qui est à la clé de la sécurité opérationnelle d'une batterie, en particulier lorsque la batterie est entièrement chargée. Nous démontrons la stabilité thermique intrinsèque très élevée des électrolytes solides d'hydroborates en contact avec les matériaux actifs de cathode dans leur état chargé ou déchargé, ce qui est une précondition pour assurer une opération sûre d'une technologie de batterie entièrement solide dans une large gamme de température mais qui est aussi une condition importante pour permettre une charge rapide.

Les résultats de cette thèse démontrent que les hydroborates représentent une classe d'électrolytes solides très prometteuse pour permettre d'obtenir une technologie de batterie entièrement solide compétitive.

## Zusammenfassung

Lithium-Ionen-Batterien  sind  als  elektrochemische  Energiespeicher  für  mobile  Anwendungen allgegenwärtig  und  ein  unverzichtbarer  Bestandteil  des  täglichen  Lebens.  Sie  ermöglichen  den Betrieb von Elektrofahrzeugen und die Integration erneuerbarer Energien in das Stromnetz. Sie sind somit eine Schlüsseltechnologie für die Dekarbonisierung des Verkehrs- und Stromsektors. Ihre physikalisch-chemischen Grenzen und Sicherheitsbedenken motivieren jedoch die Forschung an alternativen Batteriekonzepten. Dabei erhalten gerade Festkörperbatterien grosse Aufmerksamkeit, da sie höhere Energieund Leistungsdichten gepaart mit besserer Betriebssicherheit versprechen.

Die Schlüsselkomponente einer Festkörperbatterie ist der Festkörperelektrolyt. Verschiedene  Materialklassen  kommen  dafür  in  Betracht.  Unter  ihnen  steht  die  noch  recht unerforschte  Klasse  der  Hydroborate  hervor,  die  hoher  Li + und  Na + Leitfähigkeiten  mit  der Kompatibilität  zu  metallischen  Anoden  und  einfacher  Herstellung  von  Festkörperzellen  durch Kaltpressen ermöglichen. Allerdings bleibt eine hohe Zellspannung von 4 V, vergleichbar mit den kommerziellen  Lithium-Ionen-Batterien  eine  große  Herausforderung,  da  die  Faktoren,  die  die thermodynamische und kinetische Stabilität von Hydroborat-Elektrolyten begrenzen, noch nicht im Detail untersucht und verstanden wurden.

Das Ziel dieser Arbeit ist es daher, die elektrochemische und thermische Stabilität von Hydroborat-Festkörperelektrolyten und deren Kompatibilität mit Kathoden für die Implementierung in Hochvolt-Festkörperbatterien zu verstehen und eine Zellspannung von 4 V zu erreichen.

In  dieser  Dissertation  wird  zunächst  eine  robuste  Methodik  zur  Bestimmung  der elektrochemischen oxidativen Stabilität von Hydroborat-Elektrolyten auf Basis voltammetrischer Methoden etabliert,  wobei  zunächst  Lithiumborhydrid  (LiBH4)  als  Modell-Festkörperelektrolyt verwendet wird. Die Methode ist jedoch generell auf Hydroborat-Festkörperelektrolyte anwendbar. Die Erweiterung dieser Methode erlaubt, dass bestimmte Hydroborat-Elektrolyte in der  Lage  sind,  die  Grenzfläche  zu  Kathoden  der  4 V-Klasse  effektiv  zu  passivieren.  Konkret untersuchen  wir  die in-situ -Passivierung  der  Grenzfläche  zwischen  dem  Mischanion-ClosoHydroborat-Festkörperelektrolyten  Na4(CB11H12)2(B12H12)  und  der  4 V-Kathodenaktivmaterial Na3(VOPO4)2F. Nach der elektrochemischen Polarisierung bildet sich an der Kathoden/ElektrolytGrenzfläche eine passivierende Zwischenschicht, die es erstmals ermöglicht, eine 4 V-Hydroboratbasierte Festkörperbatterie bei Raumtemperatur mit einer exzellenten Kapazität und Energieerhaltung  für  mehr  als  800 Zyklen  langfristig  zu  betreiben.  In  Kombination  mit  einer Natrium-Metall-Anode verzeichnet diese Zelle die höchste durchschnittliche Entladungszellspannung, die bisher mit einem einzelnen Festelektrolyten erreicht wurde, sowie die die höchste spezifische Energie auf Basis des aktiven Kathodenmaterials unter allen berichteten Natrium-Festkörper Batterien.

Neben der elektrochemischen  Stabilität  ist  die  thermische  Stabilität  des  Elektrolyten  in Kontakt  mit  den  Elektrodenmaterialien  der  Schlüssel  für  die  Betriebssicherheit  einer  Batterie, insbesondere wenn die Batterie vollständig geladen ist. Wir zeigen die hohe intrinsische thermische Stabilität von Closo-Hydroborat-Festkörperelektrolyten in Kontakt mit unterschiedlichen Kathodenmaterialien im geladenen und entladenen Zustand, eine Voraussetzung, um den sicheren Betrieb einer Festkörperbatterietechnologie über einen weiten Temperaturbereich zu gewährleisten. Dies ist insbesondere wichtig für die Schnellladung.

Die Ergebnisse dieser Arbeit zeigen, dass Hydroborate eine vielversprechende Festelektrolytklasse  darstellen,  um  eine  wettbewerbsfähige  Festkörper-Batterietechnologie  zu ermöglichen.

## Chapter 1 Introduction

## 1.1 Background

Climate change caused by human economic activity is one of the major challenges of our time.  To  mitigate  this  change  and  to  limit  global  warming  within  &lt;2 °C  by  2050,  as proposed by the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change (IPCC), [1]  greenhouse gas (e.g. CO2) emissions have to be reduced continuously by 6 % every year. [2]  As shown in Fig. 1.1, CO2 emissions are divided into power, industry, transport, and buildings sectors, where the power and transport sectors account for the majority of emissions. Drastic and constant reduction of greenhouse gases in each sector requires the " Energiewende "  from finite  fossil  fuels  such  as  coal,  oil,  and  natural  gas  to  renewable  energy  sources.  In  the power sector, increasingly cost-competitive solar and wind power plants are forecasted to be  the  major  electricity  sources  by  2050. [2] This  increase  in  the  ratio  of  low-carbon, sustainable electricity supply also serves as a driving force for transition to e-mobility in the transport sector and further electrifications in other sectors.

Fig. 1.1  Annual net CO2 emissions in the economic transition scenario with the 2.0 °C, 1.75 °C, and 1.5 °C climate targets (top). Global electricity generation mix with the future projection from the New Energy Outlook (NEO) 2020 (bottom). [2]

Rechargeable  batteries  are  currently  used  mainly  as  power  sources  for  portable electronics (&lt;100 Wh) and as auxiliary power units for internal combustion engine (ICE) vehicles. They also play a crucial role in the energy transition, especially in the power and transport sectors. First, the intermittent nature of renewables such as solar and wind power cause frequency fluctuations in the electrical grid, which can more likely cause a blackout as a consequence of the further installation of photovoltaics and wind farms. Stationary electrochemical  energy  storage  using  rechargeable  batteries  (&gt;10 MW)  is  one  of  the solutions to the short-timescale frequency control because of its relatively fast response time, high round-trip efficiency, geographical flexibility, and facile scalability, which is advantageous to pumped-hydro or compressed-air energy storage. [3] Moreover, rechargeable batteries can close the peak gap economically between electricity demand and supply on an hourly and daily basis e.g. by daytime charging from photovoltaics and by evening-time  discharging  for  households. [4,5] For  instance,  larger  battery  systems  are coupled  to  large  solar  farms  (&gt;1 MWh),  while  smaller  battery  systems  (10 kWh)  are combined with residential photovoltaic systems. Energy storage at a longer timescale (e.g. month, season, year) could be achieved by other energy conversion technologies such as water splitting for the production of hydrogen or more generally by power-to-X for the production of synthetic fuels. [6] In the transport sector, rechargeable batteries enabled fully electric  vehicles  (EVs)  (50-100 kWh)  competitive  to  ICE  vehicles  in  terms  of  travel distance, enabling zero-emission vehicles, and the departure from fossil fuel consumption upon driving. This shift to e-mobility in the transport sector contributes to the reduction of CO2 emissions as long as electricity is generated by low-carbon energy sources such as renewables. [7] Rechargeable batteries also promote electrifications in other sectors, thereby becoming more and more important energy storage technologies for the ongoing energy transition.

Fig. 1.2  Specific energy (in Wh kg -1 ) and energy density (in Wh L -1 ) of rechargeable batteries (left). [8] Schematic of a lithium-ion battery cell upon discharging (right). [3]

As shown in Fig. 1.2, rechargeable batteries have evolved over the past decades in terms  of  both  specific  energy  (=  gravimetric  energy  density)  and  (volumetric)  energy density. The current state-of-the-art battery type in the market is the lithium-ion battery (LIB), which employ (i) lithium ions as charge carriers, (ii) an organic liquid electrolyte with lithium salts, and (iii) topotactic reactions of intercalation compounds as cathode and anode active materials (see Fig. 1.2). [3] The extended electrochemical stability window of carbonate-based  electrolytes  (compared  to  e.g.  aqueous  electrolytes)  and  larger  redox potential difference between layered transition metal oxide cathodes (e.g. LiCoO2) and carbonaceous anodes (e.g. graphite) than in previous battery types enabled powering 3 Vclass electronic circuits in one cell, resulting in the wide-spread deployment of LIBs in portable electronics. Their proof of concept was patented in 1985 and the first LIBs were introduced into the market by Sony in 1991. [9,10]  Furthermore, the recent growing demand for e-mobility served as the last push to award the Nobel Prize in Chemistry 2019 to John B. Goodenough, M. Stanley Whittingham, and Akira Yoshino for the development of LIBs. [11-13] As shown in Fig. 1.3, the LIB demand is predicted to grow by 10 times in the next 10 years, mainly driven by the growing EV market. Such a high demand and mass production  of  EVs  have  already  led  to  the  rapid  reduction  of  LIB  pack  price  from 1183 $ kWh -1 in  2010  to  137 $ kWh -1 in  2020.  This  trend  is  predicted  to  continue  to 61 $ kWh -1 in 2030, which makes EVs and other battery applications more and more costcompetitive.

Fig. 1.3  Forecasted annual demand (top) and pack price (bottom) of LIBs. [2]

In addition to cost reduction by mass production, the energy content stored in a LIB cell directly affects the weight, size, and performance of EVs and other mobile applications. Hence, increasing pack- and cell-level energy of LIBs has been attempted along with the continuous development of electrode active materials. The latest generation of LIBs marks the specific energy of 270 Wh kg -1  and the energy density of 770 Wh L -1 , [14] achieved by high nickel content in layered transition metal oxide cathodes and by silicon incorporation into graphite anodes. These values are approaching a physicochemical limit; therefore, a more  fundamental  breakthrough  in  a  battery  system  is  needed  to  aim  for  ambitious research  targets  of  &gt;400 Wh kg -1   and  &gt;1200 Wh L -1 . [15] Simultaneously,  high  rate capability is also required for fast charging, ideally within 10 min (= 6C in terms of C-rate). Realization of high power does not only require high ionic conductivity (&gt;1 mS cm -1 ) but also needs to overcome the concentration gradients of ion-conducting salts in the liquid electrolytes due to their low Li + transference number and the presence of mobile counter anions. Moreover, the organic liquid electrolytes limit the operating temperature range due to low ionic conductivity at low temperatures (e.g. &lt;0 °C) and thermal instability with a charged  cathode  at  high  temperatures  (e.g.  &gt;60 °C).  The  flammability  of  the  organic solvents in the electrolytes leaves safety concerns in the case of short circuit or external damage of a cell, which motivates research on new types of electrolytes with improved safety. Also, the scarcity and geographical maldistribution of lithium and cobalt resources drive the investigation of other cell chemistries, e.g. sodium ions as an alternative charge carrier. [16,17]

## 1.2 All-solid-state batteries

All-solid-state batteries, which replace liquid electrolytes with solid electrolytes, are one of the promising next-generation battery types with a relatively high technology readiness level at the component level. This battery type promises to overcome the above-mentioned limitations of LIBs in terms of energy, power, and safety.

Simple substitution with heavier inorganic solid electrolytes leads to a decrease in specific energy on a cell level (see Fig. 1.4); [14] however, the absence of leaking risks of liquid electrolytes enables a simpler cell-packaging design and hence higher energy density on  a  module  or  system  level.  Moreover,  all-solid-state  batteries  have  the  potential  to employ  lithium  or  sodium  metal  as  a  high-capacity  (3850 mAh g -1 or  1165 mAh g -1 , respectively) anode active material with a low redox potential ( -3.04 V or -2.71 V vs. the standard hydrogen electrode, respectively) by suppressing dendrite formation upon electrodeposition of lithium or sodium metal during charging. This can significantly increase both specific energy and energy density (see Fig. 1.4). [14] Also, inorganic solid electrolytes exhibit (i) a cation transference number of almost unity thanks to an immobile anion framework, and (ii) low charge-transfer resistance at the solid/solid interface thanks to the absence of de-solvation  processes  in  liquid  electrolytes, [18] given  a  good  contact  at  the  interface  is maintained. These features potentially enable higher specific power (= gravimetric power density)  and  (volumetric)  power  density  in  all-solid-state  batteries  compared  to  LIBs. Lastly, the intrinsic thermal stability of inorganic solid electrolytes can extend the operating

temperature range, especially at high temperatures (e.g. &gt;60 °C), and improve safety by inhibiting thermal runaway upon overcharging or mechanical failure of a cell.

Fig.1.4  Schematic of LIBs (center) and all-solid-state battery with (right) or without (left) a lithium metal  anode. [14] The  wvol and  w grav stand  for  (volumetric)  energy  density  and  specific  energy  (= gravimetric energy density), respectively.

Solid electrolytes are the key component that influences the performance of all-solidstate batteries. Material properties required for ideal solid electrolytes are summarized in the following: [19]

-  Ionic conductivity: comparable to or higher than that of liquid electrolytes, i.e. &gt;10 -3  S cm -1 at room temperature
-  Ion selectivity: only conducting charge carrier ions of interest (e.g. Li + , Na + ), i.e. cation transference number ≈ 1
-  Electronic conductivity: negligible electronic conduction to prevent self-discharge, i.e. ~0 S cm -1
-  Electrochemical stability (oxidation &amp; reduction): wide intrinsic stability window to enable a high cell voltage (without interface modifications) and various options for electrode active materials, especially lithium or sodium metal
-  Chemical stability: negligible  spontaneous  chemical  decompositions  in  air,  e.g. against oxygen and water molecules
-  Thermal stability: negligible thermal decomposition at least up to 300 °C
-  Compatibility  with  electrodes: stable  interface  formation  with  electrode  active materials and conductive additives at any operating potential and temperature
-  Mechanical properties: flexible size and shape control, and facile densifications for good contact with electrodes
-  Device integration: facile fabrication protocols of all-solid-state cells
-  Processing cost: low-cost raw materials and simple preparation for scaling up

To  fabricate  all-solid-state  batteries  with  such  ideal  solid  electrolytes,  electrode active materials also have to fulfill the following criteria for high energy and power:

-  Average redox potential: high (&gt;3.0 V vs Li + /Li or Na + /Na) for cathodes, low (ideally close to 0 V vs Li + /Li or Na + /Na) for anodes

-  Theoretical capacity: high (&gt;120 mAh g -1 ) for cathodes, ideally lithium or sodium metal (3850 mAh g -1  or 1165 mAh g -1 ) for anodes, directly affecting the maximum possible energy content of a cell [17,20]
-  Ionic conductivity: moderately high for high power applications
-  Electronic conductivity: moderately high; otherwise, additional improvement with conductive additives is necessary
-  Structural stability: negligible structural degradation after the initial "activation" in the first cycle (if necessary) for stable capacity retention
-  Volume  change: as  small  as  possible  to  maintain  stable  electrode/electrolyte interfaces, e.g. &lt;7.5 % for lithium-based systems [21]
-  Particle  size: well-balanced  with  the  size  of  solid  electrolytes  and  conductive additives  to  maximize  ionic  and  electronic  conduction  (and  hence  capacity utilization and rate capability) in the electrode composites [22,23]
-  Compatibility with electrolytes: negligible (electro)chemical reactions with solid electrolytes at any operating potential and temperature
-  Thermal stability: negligible decomposition up to 300 °C at any state of charge
-  Processing cost: low-cost raw materials and simple preparation for upscaling

In principle, electrode  active materials  developed with liquid electrolytes can be employed in  all-solid-state  batteries.  Although  most  of  the  criteria  for  electrode  active materials are identical to the ones with liquid electrolytes, all-solid-state batteries require a few additional conditions. For instance, minimal volume changes upon redox reactions are particularly beneficial for stable all-solid-state battery cycling by maintaining solid-solid interfaces between an electrolyte and electrodes. Also, fast cation diffusion inside electrode active materials by a short diffusion length and/or by high ionic conductivity can facilitate high rate capability and long-term cycling performance against contact loss (and prolonged diffusion pathways). These differences are mainly due to crack formation or contact loss at the solid-solid interfaces that directly lowers the capacity utilization of the accessible electrode active materials in the electrode composites. [24,25] This is in contrast to the ability of  liquid  electrolytes  to  wet  the  electrode  composites  and  to  form  new  solid-liquid interfaces.

Therefore, electrode-electrolyte interface stability, as well as ionic conductivity of solid electrolytes, is crucial for long-term all-solid-state battery cycling. As shown in Fig. 1.5, when a solid electrolyte is sandwiched between an anode and cathode active material and  cycled  beyond  the  thermodynamic  electrochemical  stability  window  of  the  solid electrolyte,  (electro)chemical  electrolyte  decomposition  occurs  at  the  interface  with interphase formation. [17,26,27] The formation and physical properties of this interphase is a decisive factor for the long-term electrochemical behavior (see Fig. 1.6). [28,29] If the formed interphase  has  higher  electronic  conductivity  than  the  solid  electrolyte,  electrolyte decomposition may continue until short-circuiting. If the interphase shows very low ionic and electronic  conductivity,  the  interphase  increases  cell  resistance  and  overpotentials, leading to severe capacity fading. Thus, sufficiently high ionic conductivity and negligible electronic  conductivity  are  required  for  the  interphase  to  passivate  the  interface  upon electrolyte  decomposition.  Provided  a  good  initial  contact,  this  means  that  a  stable

interface can be obtained by (i) cycling within the thermodynamic stability window with no  interphase  formation  or  (ii)  employing  a  designed  electrolyte  to  form  a  passivating interphase beyond the intrinsic stability window. Otherwise, the electrode materials need (iii)  an  artificial  protective  coating  to  kinetically  hinder  electrolyte  decomposition. [24,25] With lithium or sodium metal anodes, additional measures are needed to suppress the formation and propagation of dendrites. [28] Therefore,  solid  electrolytes with high ionic conductivity and a wide intrinsic electrochemical stability window generally offer a high degree of freedom for the selection of electrode active materials.

Fig. 1.5  Potential profile in an all-solid-state cell. VB and CB stand for valence band and conduction band, respectively. The variables μ and φ are the chemical potential of elemental lithium (in black) and the electric potential (in red), respectively. [17,26,27]

Fig. 1.6  Three types of interphase/interface between an electrode (lithium metal in this example) and an  inorganic  solid  electrolyte:  (a)  thermodynamically  stable  interface,  (b)  mixed  ionic/electronicconducting interphase (MCI), and (c) metastable, kinetically stabilized solid electrolyte interphase (SEI). (d -f) Expected time dependence of a resistance change in the respective interface/interphase type. [28,29]

Seeking for ideal solid electrolytes, several classes of inorganic materials have been studied, e.g. oxides, sulfides, halides, or hydroborates. Fig. 1.7 and 1.8 provide an overview of the ionic conductivity and intrinsic electrochemical stability windows of representative inorganic  solid  electrolytes. [17,30,31] Note  that  organic  polymer-based  electrolytes  are excluded  from  the  lists  due  to  their  low  ionic  conductivity,  relatively  low  cation transference  number,  and  limited  improvement  of  oxidative  and  thermal  stability compared to organic liquid electrolytes. However, their facile processability and potential dendrite suppression functionality can facilitate the use of lithium metal in LIBs.

Fig. 1.7  The room temperature ionic conductivity of representative (a) lithium-ion and (b) sodiumion conductors discovered over the past decades. [17]

Fig. 1.8  Calculated electrochemical stability windows of representative lithium-ion (top) and sodiumion (bottom) conductors. [30,31]

First,  oxides  have  been  studied  for  a  long  time  as  solid-state  Na + superionic conductors. This material class generally exhibits high thermal and chemical stability. The stability of β -alumina (Na2O · (8 -11) Al2O3) against sodium metal enabled the commercialization  of  molten-salt  Na-S  batteries  by  NGK  Insulators  at  an  operating temperature  of  ≥300 °C. [32] Its  superior  successor β "-alumina  (Na2O · (5 -7)Al2O3)  and NASICON (= Sodium ( Na ) S uper I onic CON ductor)-type Na1+3 x Zr2(Si x P1 -x O4)3  (NZSP) derivatives have recently shown room-temperature ionic conductivity above 10 -3  S cm -1 , motivating to lower the operating temperature. [33-35] As for lithium-ion conductors, lithium phosphorus oxynitride (LiPON) is well-known but its original applications were limited to thin-film batteries due to the low ionic conductivity of ~10 -6  S cm -1 . [19] Later on, bulk solid electrolytes such as NASICON-type Li1+ x Al x Ti2 -x (PO4)3 (LATP), LISICON (= Li thium S uper I onic CON ductor)-type Li2+2 x Zn1 -x GeO4, garnet-type Li7La3Zr2O12 (LLZO),  perovskite-type  Li 3+ x La(2/3) -x TiO3  (LLTO),  and  their  derivatives  have  reached ionic conductivity in the range between 10 -5  S cm -1 and 10 -3  S cm -1 at room temperature. [19,36] Although many of these Li + or Na + conductors show high electrochemical oxidative stability, they tend to show poor reductive stability and form mixed  ionic/electronic-conducting interphases (MCI)  that do not allow interface passivation (see Fig. 1.6). Therefore, only a few candidates such as LLZO derivatives and β "-alumina  are  intensively  studied  due  to  their  relatively  high  ionic  conductivity  and compatibility against lithium or sodium metal. [37-39] More importantly, the hard and brittle nature of oxide solid electrolytes induces large grain-boundary resistance that lowers total conductivity and requires sintering at &gt;900 °C for electrolyte preparation. This sintering process  makes  it  challenging  to  achieve  an  initial  good  electrode/electrolyte  contact without the incorporation of polymers that reduce thermal stability.

Second, sulfides (including thiophosphates) made  a breakthrough in solid electrolytes as glass-ceramic Li2 S-P2S5, thio-LISICON-type Li10GeP2S12 (LGPS), argyrodite-type Li 6 Pn S5 X ( Pn = P, Sb; X = Cl, Br, I), and their derivatives reached high room-temperature Li + conductivity of  &gt;10 -3  S cm -1 or  even  &gt;10 -2  S cm -1 that is competitive to or beyond the ionic conductivity of organic liquid electrolytes. [40-44] The analogy  to  lithium-ion  conductors  was  also  used  to  design  sulfide-based  sodium-ion conductors,  discovering  room-temperature  ionic  conductivity  of  &gt;10 -3  S cm -1 e.g.  in Na3P1x As x S4 ,  Na 3 SbS4,  Na11 Sn2PS12,  and  Na3 -xPn 1 -x W x S4 ( Pn =  P,  Sb). [33,34,45-48] These superionic conductors raised the feasibility of all-solid-state batteries and triggered further research activity because of their simple material preparation by mechanical ball milling and  their  ductility  enabling  a  good  initial  contact  with  electrodes  by  cold  pressing. However, the very narrow electrochemical  stability windows of sulfides (&lt;1 V) almost eliminate the possibility of a thermodynamically stable interface (see Fig. 1.6) and need interface  passivation  on  both  cathode  and  anode  sides.  Their  high  driving  forces  for electrolyte decomposition at electrode redox potentials also limit the use of conductive carbon in the electrode composites for high rate capability. [49] Moreover, apart from Na3P1x As x S4, Na3SbS4, and Na3 -x Sb1 -x W x S4 , [45,46,50] they are sensitive to air and moisture, releasing toxic H2S gas upon chemical decomposition.

Additionally, halides have been recently spotlighted since Panasonic reported that LiYCl6 and LiYBr6 exhibit higher ionic conductivity of &gt;10 -4  S cm -1 than stated in earlier

studies. [51,52] Typical halide solid electrolytes are made up of ternary phases: LiM -X or NaM -X ( M = transition metal, X = F, Cl, Br, I). Selecting M and X for face-centered-cubic ( fcc )-type  anion  sublattices  or  preparing  derivatives  with  aliovalent  substitution  of M is shown  to  enable  room-temperature  conductivity  of  &gt;10 -3  S cm -1 e.g. in Li3 InCl6, Li x ScCl3+ x , and Li3 -xM 1 -x Zr x Cl6 ( M = Y, Er, In, Yb) for X = Cl. [53-58] The electrochemical oxidative stability of halides is linked with the respective binary LiX or NaX systems (see Fig. 1.6); therefore, halides with smaller halogen atoms (= higher electronegativity) tend to be more stable at higher electrochemical potentials. [30] On the other hand, the reductive stability is determined by the selected M with MCI formation, limiting the potential for the direct use of lithium or sodium metal anodes. Although halides show high thermal stability and facile pelletization  by cold pressing, their  moisture  sensitivity (except Li 3InCl6 and Li3Y1 -x In x Cl6 [59,60] )  leads to irreversible chemical decomposition, similarly to sulfides. In addition to the reactivity in air, heat treatment processes in sealed ampoules needed for some of the highly conductive halides may challenge facile upscaling.

As described above, each material class has its own benefits and drawbacks, and none  of  them  meets  the  material  requirements  for  ideal  solid  electrolytes  so  far. Hydroborates emerged as a promising material class of Li + and Na + conductors that can overcome the aforementioned issues in other material classes. The details are discussed in the next section.

## 1.3 Hydroborate solid electrolytes

## 1.3.1  Discovery of superionic conductors

Hydroborates are comprised of cations (e.g. alkali metal and alkaline earth metal) and three-dimensional cluster anions with boron-hydrogen and boron-boron covalent bonds. Structures of the representative hydroborate anions are listed in Fig. 1.9. Hydroborate solid electrolytes draw growing attention because they combine material properties suitable for the implementation in all-solid-state batteries , including compatibility with lithium and/or sodium metal anodes, low gravimetric density (&lt;1.2 g cm -3 ; less than half of other material classes),  soft  mechanical  properties  enabling  cold  pressing,  high  thermal  and  chemical stability thanks to the covalent bonding in hydroborate anions, solution processability, and low toxicity. [61]

Fig. 1.9  Structures of (a) [BH 4 ] -, (b) [B 10H10] 2 -, (c) [B 12H12] 2 -, (d) [1-CB 9H10] -, and (e) [CB11H12] -anions. White and black balls represent hydrogen (H) and carbon (C) atoms, respectively. The rest of the balls in ocher, blue, and green represent boron (B) atoms.

Complex  hydrides  have  been  originally  studied  as  hydrogen  storage  materials, among which LiBH4 was first discovered to exhibit order-disorder phase transition upon microwave irradiation. [62,63] Matsuo et al. found superionic conduction (&gt;10 -3  S cm -1 ) in its hexagonal high-temperature phase above the phase-transition temperature of &gt;115 °C, [64] initiating a research field on hydroborates as solid ionic conductors. Fig. 1.10 summarizes the temperature-dependent ionic conductivity of representative hydroborate solid electrolytes.  LiBH 4 consists  of  a  relatively  small  [BH 4 ] -anion,  while  larger  but  more structurally stable, cage-like closo -hydroborate anions [B n H n ] 2 -( n = 10, 12) are also found to  show  order-disorder  phase  transition. [65,66] In  particular,  the  disordered  phases  of Na2B10H10  and  Na2B12H12  above  their  respective  phase-transition  temperatures  exhibit around  one  order-of-magnitude  higher  ionic  conductivity  than  that  of  LiBH4. [67,68] Furthermore, both lithium and sodium salts of closo -monocarbaborate anions [CB n -1H n ] -( n = 10, 12), which substitutes one boron (B) atom of closo -hydroborates by a carbon (C) atom,  possesses  even  higher  ionic  conductivity  in  their  high-temperature  phases. [69,70] Although the ideal cation/anion size ratio for fast ionic conduction may vary with different cation  radii, [71] the  general  trend  of  conductivity  enhancement  in  monovalent closo -monocarbaborates  originates partly from their lower anion charge, reducing the electrostatic interactions with cations compared to divalent closo -hydroborates.

Fig.  1.10    Temperature-dependent  ionic  conductivity  of  Li  hydroborates  (top) [72] and  of  Na hydroborates (bottom). [67,70,81,73-80]

Order-disorder phase transitions of hydroborates are confirmed experimentally by ion dynamics studies. [82-91] The easiest observable is the line width of NMR spectra. When hydrogen (H) atoms in hydroborates are involved in thermally activated jump motions, the line width of  1 H NMR Δ H decreases with increasing temperatures by averaging out dipoledipole interactions between nuclear spins within the same anion. On the other hand, for compounds  exhibiting  localized  motions  such  as  anion  reorientations,  interactions between nuclear spins from different anions remain partially even at elevated temperatures. Therefore,  the  temperature  dependence  of  the  line-width  narrowing  can  be  used  to distinguish between localized motions and thermally activated diffusion. The atomic jump rates τ -1 of anion reorientational motions can be further characterized by measuring the nuclear  spin -lattice  relaxation  rate R 1 at  around μ s-  to  ns-timescale. R 1 reaches  the maximum when τ -1 becomes nearly equal to the nuclear magnetic resonance frequency ω (in MHz), i.e. ωτ ≈ 1. In the limit of slow atomic motion ( ωτ ≫ 1), R 1 is proportional to ω -2 τ -1 , whereas, in the limit of fast motion ( ωτ ≪ 1), R 1 is proportional to τ and becomes frequency independent. Assuming that the temperature dependence of the jump rate τ -1 follows the Arrhenius law, the activation energy E a can be quantified in both limit regions.

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

Fig. 1.11  Temperature dependence of 1 H and 23 Na spin -lattice relaxation rates R 1 for  the  order -disorder phase transitions of Na2B12H12 and NaCB11H12. [82,85,92] The vertical bars show the respective phase-transition temperature.

Upon the phase transition  of  hydroborates,  the  1 H NMR line width Δ H  and 1 H spin -lattice relaxation rate R 1 H  (e.g. Na2B12H12 and NaCB11H12 shown in Fig. 1.11) drop discontinuously, corresponding to a soar in the anion reorientational jump rate τ -1 . [82,85,92]

This accompanies an abrupt change of the 23 Na spin -lattice relaxation rate R 1 Na from slowmotion  to  fast-motion  regions  with  a  decrease  in  the  activation  energy,  indicating  the acceleration of translational, long-range cation diffusion induced by fast anion reorientations.

These phenomena are also evidenced by quasi-elastic neutron scattering (QENS) experiments  that  probe  microscopic  relaxation  processes  at  the  much  shorter  pstimescale. [83,86-88,91,93,94] The  drop  in  the  elastic  neutron  scattering  intensity  at  the  phasetransition  temperature  indicates  that  anion  reorientational  jump  rates  (=  frequencies) increase by a few orders of magnitude from &lt;10 7 s -1 to &gt;10 10 s -1 . The ratio of elastic to total (= elastic + quasi-elastic) scattering intensities, i.e. so-called elastic incoherent structure factor (EISF), suggests that the anion reorientation mechanism shifts from a uniaxial to a multiaxial character above the phase-transition temperature. Although NMR and QENS measurements  characterize  rather  a  microscopic  relaxation  at  a  short  timescale,  these findings  are  in  line  with  electrochemical  impedance  spectroscopy,  which  measures macroscopic relaxation at a longer timescale (from MHz to Hz or mHz). In other words, fast anion reorientations in the disordered, high-temperature phases induce the dramatic enhancement of cation conduction, resulting in a leap in ionic conductivity upon orderdisorder phase transition (see Fig. 1.10). The phase-transition temperatures vary with the combination of cation and hydroborate anion species,  but the conductivity jump upon phase  transition  is  a  commonly  observed  feature  of  hydroborate  solid  electrolytes. [83] Complementary computational studies on the ion conduction mechanism of hydroborate solid electrolytes will be discussed in Chapter 2.

To  obtain  high  room-temperature  ionic  conductivity,  many  attempts  have  been made to stabilize the disordered, high-temperature phases of hydroborates. Reducing the crystallite size and possibly introducing H-atom deficiencies by ball milling are shown to increase ionic conductivity; [95,96]  however, this approach produces metastable compounds that can be vulnerable to the re-formation of low-temperature phases after thermal cycling. Nano-confinement in nanoporous hosts is also found to be effective for the conductivity improvement of e.g. LiBH4 and Li4(BH4)3I due to the suppression of a long-range order at the  nm-scale  thin  layer  of  hydroborates  on  the  pore  walls. [82,97-100] However, the  roomtemperature  Li + or  Na + conductivity  of  nano-confined  hydroborates  has  yet  to  exceed 10 -3  S cm -1 . [91,97,106,107,98-105] Chemical  modifications,  e.g.  partial  or  full  halogenation,  of hydroborate anions is also investigated, which so far results in an undesirable increase in phase-transition  temperatures, [108,109] apart  from  the  iodination  of M 2 B12 H12  ( M =  Li, Na). [110,111] Alternatively, cation mixing shows  a moderate  improvement  of  ionic conductivity, but raises the question of which cation in which fraction is conducted in the electrolytes due to the presence of multiple charge-carrier species. [112-115]

So far, anion mixing by ball milling and/or heat treatment is the most successful approach  to  the  high-temperature  phase  stabilization  of  hydroborates.  This  was  first attempted to stabilize the high-temperature phase of LiBH4 with halides Li X ( X = Cl, Br, I). [116,117] Among the anion mixtures, Li4(BH4)3I exhibits the highest ionic conductivity of &gt;10 -4  S cm -1 at  room temperature due to cell volume expansion by relatively large and polarizable I -ions. [116,117] Slightly higher room-temperature conductivity is also obtained by mixing LiBH4 with LiNH2 or P2S5. [118-120]  For Na analogues, a mixed anion Na2(BH4)(NH2)

exhibits  improved  room-temperature  conductivity  of  3  ×  10 -6  S cm -1 ,  which  is  by  four orders of magnitude higher than that of NaBH4. [73,74,90] Additionally, ternary anion mixing in [BH4] --based systems recently shows conductivity improvement. [121,122]

The  approach  of  binary  anion  systems  was  further  extended  to  mixing  two hydroborate anion species; for example, Na3(BH4)(B12H12) show the highest NaBH4-based room-temperature  conductivity  of  5  ×  10 -4  S cm -1 . [76] Mixing  two  types  of closo -hydroborates  and/or  monocarbaborates  is  even  more  effective  due  to  the  higher conductivity of their original high-temperature phases than [BH4] -.  In particular, mixed closo -hydroborate  anions  are  extensively  studied  and  found  to  be  Na + superionic conductors (&gt;10 -3  S cm -1 ) at room temperature, e.g. Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) and Na4(CB11H12)2(B12H12)  that  crystallize  in  the  disordered,  high-temperature  phases  of [B10H10] 2 -and [B12H12] 2 -, respectively. [123-126] Some of the closo -hydroborate anion mixtures can also be obtained by the reaction products from [BH4] -salts and neutral decaborane B10 H14. [127,128] Na closo -hydroborates and monocarbaborates tend to exhibit roughly oneorder-of-magnitude higher conductivity upon anion mixing than Li counterparts, [126,127,129] which can be attributed to the high polarizability and large ionic radius of Na + ions that are beneficial to cation conduction in the framework of the mixed closo -hydroborate anions. Among these anion mixtures, a combination of [1-CB9H10] -and [CB11H12] -is  shown to record the highest room-temperature conductivity in both lithium and sodium systems, i.e. Li(CB9H10)0.7(CB11H12)0.3  (=  0.7Li(CB9H10)-0.3Li(CB11H12))  and  Na2(CB9H10)(CB11H12) with 7 × 10 -3  S cm -1 and 7 × 10 -2  S cm -1 , respectively (see Fig. 1.10). [72,86,129] Notably, the latter  marks the  highest room-temperature ionic conductivity among ever-reported Na + solid  electrolytes,  followed  by  Na 2.9 Sb0.9 W0.1S4  with  4  ×  10 -2  S cm -1 . [34,48] It  is  worth mentioning that the mixed closo -hydroborate anions experience no distinct phase transition but  exhibit  non-Arrhenius  conductivity  behaviors  with  changes  in  activation  energies, including  glass-like  transition  and  correlated  ion  motion. [86,93,126] These  behaviors  are observed at relatively low temperatures where thermal energy is not sufficient to saturate (i) the background energy fluctuation, which can be attributed to short-range cation-cation and  cation-anion  correlation,  and  (ii)  anisotropic  anion  reorientations  around  specific symmetry axes of the two constituent anions. As for material preparation processes, the closo -hydroborate mixtures Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) can be prepared by crystallization out of an equimolar solution of Na2B12H12 and Na2B10H10 using a suitable solvent as well as by ball milling (and subsequent heat treatment). Facile wet-chemical synthesis of [B12H12] 2 -and [CB11H12] -salts and Na 4 (B12 H12)(B10 H10) is also reported with a cost-competitive reagent NaBH4, which leaves the potential for future upscaling and mass production. [130-135]

The quest for hydroborate solid electrolytes is further extended to other chargecarrier  ions.  Compared  to  Li + and  Na + analogues,  KCB11H12  exhibits  a  lower  phasetransition temperature but lower conductivity in both low- and high-temperature phases, which is explained as the steric hindrance of K + translational diffusion due to the large cation size. [87]  In contrast, NMR studies of KBH4 and M 2 B12 H12 ( M = K, Rb, Cs) show no phase transition up to 300 °C and decreasing activation energies of reorientational anion motions with increasing cation radius (and lattice parameters). [85,89,136,137] This indicates that hydroborates with larger cation sizes (i.e. Rb + , Cs + ) than K + may contribute to facilitating cation diffusion again due to the expansion of the disordered lattice. [138] For alkaline earth

metal cations, Mg hydroborate solid electrolytes, e.g. mixed anions with [BH 4 ] -, generally hold  relatively  low  room-temperature  ionic  conductivity  (up  to  10 -5  S cm -1 )  and  the difficulty  to  remove  solvents  due  to  strong  Coulombic  interaction  with  divalent  Mg 2+ ions. [139-141] This limits the use of closo compounds, e.g. Mg(CB11H12)2 to ion-conducting salts for organic liquid electrolytes or ionic liquids. [142-144]

## 1.3.2  Implementation in all-solid-state batteries

Hydroborates already exhibit sufficiently high ionic conductivity to be implemented into stably  working  all-solid-state  batteries.  In  parallel  to  the  first  discovery  of  superionic conduction in LiBH4, the implementation into a first all-solid-state battery was initiated with LiBH4-based solid electrolytes. [145] For anode and cathode active materials, lithium metal and TiS2 are typically employed, respectively, as was the case with the prototype cells of LIBs. [12] One clear distinction from LIBs is that hydroborate-based all-solid-state batteries  promise  a  more  realistic  use  of  alkali  metal  anodes  because  some  of  the hydroborate solid electrolytes are shown to be kinetically or thermodynamically stable in contact with alkali metal (e.g. lithium or sodium metal). [146,147] For instance, LiBH4 shows stable  100 h  galvanostatic  stripping/plating  in  a  symmetric  cell  with  lithium  metal  at 0.2 mA cm -2  and 0.1 mAh cm -2 at 120 °C, which is above the phase-transition temperature of LiBH4. [146] Also, alkali metal anodes function as a reservoir of charge-carrier cations, enabling the use of e.g. "Li-free" cathode active materials.

Taking these advantages, stable battery cycling in TiS2|LiBH4|Li cells was obtained with a capacity retention of &gt;85 % after 300 cycles at C/5 at 120 °C. [148] The operating temperature  can  be  reduced  by  anion  mixing  e.g.  Li 4(BH4)3 X ( X =  Cl,  Br,  I)  and Li3(BH4)(NH2)2 [119,120,122,149-151] or by nano-confinement. [98,106] Although the electrochemical oxidative stability of LiBH 4 was initially claimed to be &gt;5 V vs. Li + /Li, bulk-type all-solidstate  cells  with  a  LiBH 4 solid  electrolyte  exhibit  large  irreversible  capacity,  impedance growth,  and  quick  capacity  fading  with  a  Li 3 PO4-coated  LiCoO2  cathode. [145,152] This unclear  instability  at  high  electrochemical  potentials  directed  research  to  employ  a secondary  non-hydroborate  solid  electrolyte [98,150,153] or  electrode  active  materials  with relatively low redox potentials (≤2.7 V vs. Li + /Li) like TiS 2 .  Examples of the latter are Li4Ti5O12 [119,154] or alternatively elemental sulfur (S) to gain more capacity as compensation for low cell voltages (1672 mAh g -1  for S vs 239 mAh g -1 for TiS 2 ). [100,146,155-157]  However, the low electronic conductivity of sulfur requires a large fraction of conductive carbon in the electrode composite, reducing its specific energy and energy density significantly. The large volume changes upon (de)lithiation of sulfur cause significant capacity fading in the first few cycles in S|LiBH4|Li and S|Li4(BH4)3I|Li cells. [98,100,155,157] This is accompanied by constant electrolyte decomposition during long-term cycling.

Fig. 1.12  Performance of hydroborate-based all-solid-state sodium (left) and lithium (right) batteries. (a) Galvanostatic cycling in symmetric cells sandwiched with sodium or lithium metal. (b) Schematics of all-solid-state cells. (c) Galvanostatic charge -discharge curves. (d) Cycle dependence of discharge capacity and Coulombic efficiency. [61,72,158]

A similar approach was employed for preliminary studies on the implementation of closo -hydroborate  and  monocarbaborate  solid  electrolytes  into  all-solid-state  batteries. They also show compatibility with lithium metal, and all-solid-state cells were tested with a  TiS 2 cathode  for  a  relatively  short  term  (≤20 cycles). [70,96,159,160]   Among them, the best electrochemical performance was obtained with Li(CB9H10)0.7(CB11H12)0.3 (see Fig. 1.12),

which  exhibits  no  overpotential  increase  and  lithium  dendrite  formation  during  300 h galvanostatic stripping/plating in a lithium symmetric cell at 0.2 mA cm -2 and 0.1 mAh cm -2 at 25 °C. [72] The high ionic conductivity of this electrolyte and its tolerance at high current densities lead to high rate capability, e.g. &gt;70 % capacity remaining at high C-rates compared to low C-rates, i.e. at 10C vs C/10 with a TiS2 cathode and at 1C vs 0.03C with a sulfur cathode, respectively. [72,161] At an elevated temperature of 60 °C, the TiS2 cathode exhibits ~70 % capacity retention after 200 cycles at 1C charging/discharging.  On  the  other  hand,  the  sulfur  cathode  involves  a  large  capacity fading in the first cycle. But compared to the second cycle, the reversible capacity retains ~70 % after 100 cycles at 60 °C at 1C charging and 5C discharging, which corresponds to a current density of ~4 mA cm -2 . These results indirectly demonstrate the stability of the mixed-anion closo -monocarbaborates in the operating potential range; however, the limit of the electrochemical stability is not identified yet.

For  Na  hydroborates,  the  implementation  into  all-solid-state  batteries  started relatively recently using closo -hydroborates; nevertheless, stable cycling with a higher cell voltage  is  achieved  than  for  Li  hydroborates  (see  Fig.  1.12).  Duchêne  et  al.  employed Na4(B12H12)(B10H10)  in  a  sodium  symmetric  cell,  demonstrating  no  sodium  dendrite formation during &gt;280 h galvanostatic stripping/plating at 0.1 mA cm -2 and 1.2 mAh cm -2 at 60 °C. [79] The compatibility of this electrolyte with sodium metal is also indicated by X-ray tomography. [162] Preliminary electrochemical stability experiments with nA-scale currents also indicate the possibility of battery cycling up to ~3 V vs Na + /Na, [79] which matches the redox potential of NaCrO2 with a theoretical capacity of 120 mAh g -1 . Moreover, the solution processability of this electrolyte enables dramatic improvement of the electrode/electrolyte contact by electrolyte coating without sacrificing ionic conductivity.  Such  intimate  contact,  as  well  as  moderate  electronic  conductivity  and minimal  volume  changes  (&lt;0.3 %)  of  NaCrO2,  achieves  85 %  capacity  retention  after 250 cycles at C/5 in NaCrO2|Na4(B12H12)(B10H10)|Na cells at 60 °C. [158] The solution-based process is further applied to infiltrate the electrolyte into slurry-casted porous electrodes. This solution infiltration, the use of a Na-Sn anode, and applied external pressure (≥3 MPa) enable  67 %  capacity  remaining  at  5C  charging/discharging  (compared  to  C/10)  of NaCrO2 at 30 °C with a high mass loading of 7.7 mg cm -2 ,  corresponding to a current density of 4.5 mA cm -2 . [163]

A  similar  study  was  also  done  for  Na 4 (CB11H12)2(B12 H12),  which  reports  stable galvanostatic sodium stripping/plating for 500 h at 25.5 μ A cm -2 and  12.8 μ Ah cm -2 at room temperature and preliminary cycling experiments with NaCrO2. [164] However, the full potential of this electrolyte remains unknown.

## 1.4 Aim of this thesis

As discussed in the previous section, closo -hydroborate solid electrolytes display superionic conduction at room temperature and implementation in all-solid-state lithium and sodium batteries.  The  reported  average  cell  voltages  are  limited  up  to  3 V,  which  needs  to  be extended to aim for higher specific energy and energy density. However, the successful

integration  of  high-voltage  cathodes  is  limited  by  a  lack  of  understanding  of  the electrochemical oxidative stability of hydroborate solid electrolytes. Also, hydroborates are claimed to show high thermal stability, requiring experimental proofs in the context of allsolid-state  battery  applications.  Therefore,  the  aim  of  this  thesis  is  to  assess the electrochemical oxidative stability and  thermal  stability of closo -hydroborate solid electrolytes  and  to  implement  them  into  stably  working,  high-voltage  all-solid-state batteries. The assessment of electrochemical stability is also pivoted to open-cage, nido -hydroborates as a new candidate for solid electrolytes. [88,165,166]

This thesis consists of the following structure:

Chapter 2 discusses computational studies on the stability of hydroborates and their suitability as solid electrolytes in terms of the ion conduction mechanism, electrochemical stability, and mechanical stability.

Chapter 3 provides an overview of the experimental techniques used in this thesis.

In Chapter 4 ,  we  study  a  robust  methodology  to  determine  the  electrochemical oxidative stability of hydroborate solid electrolytes.

In Chapter 5 , we implement 4 V all-solid-state sodium batteries for the first time using  a  mixed-anion closo -hydroborate  solid  electrolyte  at  room  temperature,  which  is enabled by a self-passivating cathode/electrolyte interface.

In Chapter  6 ,  we  perform  a  systematic  study  on  thermal  and  electrochemical interface compatibility of closo -hydroborate solid electrolytes in cathode composites with layered transition metal oxides.

In Chapter 7 , we investigate a sodium salt of a nido -hydroborate as a cost-effective building block for Na + hydroborate solid electrolytes.

In Chapter 8 , we examine an analogous lithium salt of a nido -hydroborate for Li + hydroborate solid electrolytes with the implementation into a proof-of-concept all-solidstate lithium battery.

Finally, Chapter 9 concludes this thesis and provides perspectives on the further development of hydroborates and hydroborate-based all-solid-state batteries.
