## Chapter 2

## Computational Studies on closo -Hydroborates

## 2.1 Introduction &amp; motivation

Boron (B) is the smallest atom in Group 13 of the periodic table with the atomic number of 5 and has three valence electrons, one less than the neighboring carbon (C). B-B or B-H bonds in a molecule are often analogized to C-C or C-H covalent bonds, which represent well-known localized two-center two-electron (2c2e) bonds. The one-electron deficiency in a  B  atom  also  creates  multi-center  bonds  and  accounts  for  peculiar  and  intriguing properties of boron compounds.

Multi-center boron bonds, e.g. three-center two-electron (3c2e) B-B-B bonds, are represented in Fig. 2.1, where three atomic orbitals  form  one filled bonding molecular orbital  and  two  empty  antibonding  molecular  orbitals.  This  type  of  a  3c2e  bond  was initially proposed by Eberhardt et al. [1] for boron-hydrogen compounds, i.e. the two B-H-B bridging-bond configuration to explain the stability of diborane B 2 H6, which is the simple substitution  of  C  with  B  in  C 2 H6  but  with  two  electrons  less.  The  3c2e  bond  is  a  key structural  building  block  of  polyhedra  and  polyhedral  fragments  of  "electron-deficient" (compared to carbon compounds) boron cluster compounds, e.g. neutral hydroboranes and negatively charged  hydroborate  ions. [2]   Among  them,  an  icosahedral  hydroborate [B12H12] 2 -, together with the isoelectronic monocarbaborate [CB11H12] -and dicarbaborane C2B10H12, show unusual chemical and thermal stability, and low toxicity. [3,4] But why are some hydroborate clusters so stable?  The  origin  of  their  stability  and  the  prediction  of unknown stable hydroborates by computational studies are reviewed in Section 2.2.

Fig. 2.1  Graphic representation of a 2c2e B-B bond and 3c3e B-B-B bond. [5]

Due to their high stability, low toxicity, functionalizability, etc., hydroborates and heteroborates have been studied in many different fields of applications such as medicine, [69] hydrogen storage, [10-12] luminescence, [13] catalyst, [14] and polymers. [15] Currently, one of the potentially most impactful applications among them is as solid electrolytes for all-solidstate batteries, [4,10,16-20] because the ongoing electrifications of the mobility sector motivate research on next-generation rechargeable batteries with higher energy density and faster charging/discharging than current-generation Li-ion batteries. [21-24] But how can hydroborates  serve  as  fast  ion-conducting  solid  electrolytes?  To  answer  this  question, Section 2.3 reviews computational studies on closo -hydroborates and monocarbaborates in view of potential candidates for solid electrolytes and their ion conduction mechanism.

Furthermore, Section 2.4 features recent computational studies on other material properties of hydroborates that need to be considered for solid electrolyte applications, e.g. electrochemical stability and mechanical properties. This review offers a comprehensive overview of how computational studies have advanced the fundamental understanding of hydroborates and their practical applications for solid electrolytes in the ever-expanding field of all-solid-state batteries.

## 2.2 Origin and prediction of stable hydroborates

## 2.2.1 Multicenter bonding and stability of hydroborates

Hydroborate clusters form a wide variety of structures that follow so-called Wade-Mingos rules. [25] Williams proposed in 1971 that a cage-like closo n -vertex polyhedron as a parental structure  can  be  fragmented  into  a nido ( n -1)-vertex  polyhedron and an arachno ( n -2)vertex  polyhedron  (see  Fig.  2.2). [2] This  triggered  Wade's  discovery  that  all  of  these polyhedra have the common number ( n +1) of skeletal bonding molecular orbitals and of skeletal electron pairs, and hence 2 n +2 skeletal electrons. [26,27] In other words, when the number of cluster vertices n is kept constant, the number of skeletal orbitals and of skeletal electron pairs for closo ([B n H n ] 2 -), nido (B n H n +4 or [B n H n +3] -), and arachno (B n H n +6 ) clusters are n +1, n +2, and n +3, respectively. Mingos extended Wade's counting rule to the total number of electrons (i.e. 4 n +2 for closo , 4 n +4 for nido , and 4 n +6 for arachno ) by including 2 n exo -electrons corresponding to the B-H bonds. This extended approach was applied to "electron-precise" and "electron-rich" (= having the exact and more number of electrons required  for  bonding,  respectively)  organometallic  clusters  with  CO  ligands  as  well  as "electron-deficient"  boron  clusters  to  describe  that  transition  metal-CO  complexes  with excess electrons resulted in bond breaking. [28,29] These electron-counting rules provide a simple  but  powerful  framework  to  grasp  the  unifying  structural  relationship  between different types of clusters.

Fig. 2.2  The structural relationship between closo (left), nido (middle), and arachno (right) polyhedra in different cluster sizes n. [2,25]

Many attempts have been made to rationalize this empirical rule and the structural stability of hydroborate clusters. Earlier X-ray diffraction studies in the 1960s [30-34] indicated (i) that all closo clusters ([B n H n ] 2 -, 6 ≤ n ≤ 12) consist of triangle faces (named deltahedra ), and (ii) that except [B 11H11] 2 -( n = 11) having a degree 6 vertex, all of the closo clusters have exclusively degree 4 or 5 vertices, where the degree of a vertex is defined as the number of edges comprising the vertex (see Fig. 2.3). [5] In particular, [B 12H12] 2 -( n = 12) consists only of  degree 5 vertices. Initially, Lipscomb's topological models attempted to describe the chemical bonding of nido and arachno clusters based on the topology of the distribution of

the 3c2e B-B-B bonds and 2c2e B-B bonds. [35] In addition, Dixon et al. introduced Kekulétype localized bonding structures with the 3c2e B-B-B bonds and 2c2e B-B bonds to closo clusters,  in  analogy  to  alternating  C-C  single  and  C=C  double  bonds  to  explain  the structure  of  planar  benzenoid  hydrocarbons. [36] However,  O'Neill  and  Wade  showed topological restrictions of the localized bond schemes in their assumptions of 3c2e and 2c2e  bond  allocation. [37,38] Rather,  they  proposed  a  polyhedral  skeletal  electron  pair model. [27,38,39] In this model, each of the n -vertex boron atoms in a closo -hydroborate cluster contributes three (= 3 n in total) atomic orbitals for skeletal bonding, comprising one spz hybrid  radial  atomic  orbital  and  two p tangential  atomic  orbitals.  The n radial  atomic orbitals  hybridize  to  form  only  one  bonding  molecular  orbital,  while  the  2 n tangential atomic orbitals form n bonding molecular orbitals with n localized electron pairs, leading to the total n +1 of skeletal bonding molecular orbitals. However, this model assumes no mixing  between  radial  and  tangential  atomic  orbitals,  based  on  which  a -2  charge  is required for closo clusters to have a closed-shell electronic configuration.

Fig. 2.3  The structures of closo-hyhroborates, [BnHn] 2 -(6 ≤ n ≤ 12). Degree 5 vertices are indicated by a standard dot (·), degree 4 vertices by a black square ( ■ ), and a degree 6 vertex by an asterisk (*). [5]

As mentioned above, individual Kekulé structures consisting of alternating 3c2e and 2c-2e bonds cannot fully explain the special stability of hydroborates, as they failed to account for the special stability of benzenoid hydrocarbons. Instead, King and Rouvray

introduced graph-theoretical approaches to resonance stabilization derived from Hückel theory. [40] This  approach  allows  the  overlap  of  atomic  orbitals  and  hence  a  globally delocalized  polyhedron,  which  has  a  multicenter  core  bond  involving all of  the  vertex atoms. Delocalization is a result of a mismatch between the vertex degree of a polyhedron and the number of atomic orbitals from the vertex atoms, which leads to the aromatic stabilization depicted by the resonance integral β in the secular equation. [5] Using a similar graph-theoretical approach, Aihara clarified that all the deltahedral closo clusters [B n H n ] 2 -with n ≥  6  have  positive  resonance  energies  and  HOMO-LUMO  energy  separations, concluding  the  three-dimensional  aromaticity  of  hydroborates  for  the  first  time. [41] Moreover, the highest resonance energy of a [B12H12] 2 -icosahedron ( n = 12) among other closo -hydroborates would be attributed to the 12 equivalent vertices of degree 5 (see Fig. 2.3), indicating that any deviation from the vertices of degree 5 means defective vertices that lower the structural stability of the closo clusters. [5] Later on, the n +1 skeletal bonding molecular orbitals in closo clusters according to the Wade-Mingos rules are shown to be the sum of n -2 delocalized resonant 3c2e skeletal bonding molecular orbitals and three globally  delocalized  states. [42] The  deeply  bound,  globally  delocalized  states  have  to accommodate  two  extra  electrons  to  be  fully  occupied  for  a  closed-shell  electronic configuration, which accounts for the dianion nature of closo -hydroborates.

Jemmis and Schleyer further proposed the six interstitial electron rule to explain the three π-type bonding molecular orbitals in analogy to Hückel's 4 N π +2 π electron rule. [43,44] Also,  Gimarc  et  al.  attempted  to  extend  the  two-dimensional  Hückel  theory  to  threedimensional closo -hydroborates while retaining their molecular topology. [45,46] Moreover, Poater et al. recently established a connection between the Wade-Mingos 4 n +2 rules of three-dimensional aromaticity and Hückel's 4 N π +2 rule of π aromaticity by the electronic confined space analogy (ECSA) method. [47-49] Note that 4 n +2 stands for the total number of valence electrons in n -vertex clusters, whereas 4 N π +2 refers to the number of electrons corresponding to the filled electron subshells of atoms, thereby leaving N π no structural relation.  The  definition  of  these  electron  counting  rules  is  therefore  fundamentally different, but the (retro-)ECSA method proved that every closo -hydroborate finds its planar [ n ]annulene (= hydrocarbon) analogue that satisfies Hückel's rule.

## 2.2.2 Three-dimensional aromaticity of closo -hydroborates

The special stability of closo -hydroborates is derived from three-dimensional aromaticity. It is worth mentioning that this three-dimensional aromaticity is not identical to "spherical" aromaticity, proposed by Hirsch et al. with a 2( N +1) 2 counting rule. [50] Although Hirsch's rule is an extension of Hückel's 4 N π +2 π electron rule in planar two dimensions to spherical three dimensions, this transition is valid only when the number of electrons corresponds to the  filled  electron  shell  of  inert  gas,  e.g.  fullerene  C 60 . [51,52] Conversely,  "spherical" aromaticity remains aromatic when converted to planar systems, whereas the conservation of aromaticity upon conversion is not always possible in the case of "spatial" aromaticity that obeys Hückel's rule. Thus, all the closo -hydroborates can be categorized into "spatial" aromaticity, but Hirsch's rule applies only to [B 6 H6] 2 -and [B12H12] 2 -( n = 6, 12).

To quantify the degree of three-dimensional aromaticity, there are several energetic (resonance  and  aromatic  stabilization  energies),  magnetic  ( 1 H  NMR  chemical  shifts, magnetic  susceptibility  anisotropies,  and  nucleus-independent  chemical  shifts  (NICS)), and geometric (bond length equalization) criteria proposed. [53-55]   Among them, nucleusindependent chemical shifts at the center of polyhedra (NICS(0)), proposed by Schleyer et al.,  are known to be the most established method for a simple but efficient aromaticity probe. [54,56] NICS(0) is based on the ab initio calculation of the absolute magnetic shielding and  hence  ring  current  effects  in  the  geometric  center  of  the closo -hydroborate  cages. Negative  NICS(0)  values  correspond  to  aromaticity  (=  diatropic  ring  currents),  while positive values correspond to anti-aromaticity (= paratropic ring currents).

Fig. 2.4  Plots of NICS (in ppm) at the center of closo-hydroborates ([BnHn] 2 -) and the most stable closo-monocarbaborate ([CBn -1Hn] -)  and closo-monoazaborane (NBn -1Hn ) isomers (5 ≤ n ≤ 12). [57,58]

Fig.  2.4  shows  the  NICS(0)  values  of closo -hydroborates  [B n H n ] 2 -and  their isoelectronic closo -monocarbaborate  [CB n -1H n ] -and closo -monoazaborane  NB n -1H n in different cluster sizes n (5 ≤ n ≤ 12). [57,58] Overall, all of the NICS(0) values are negative, indicating three-dimensional delocalization and aromaticity. The general trend in the three types of closo clusters demonstrates that icosahedra ( n = 12) are the most aromatic, followed by  octahedra  ( n =  6)  and  bicapped  square  antiprisms  ( n =  10).  This  fits  the  earlier discussions on electronic structures. However, in the case of heteroboranes, the greater degree of electron localization at the more electronegative heteroatoms (C and N) than B atoms  decreases  the  aromaticity,  which  is  evident  especially  at n =  10.  Furthermore, although the heteroatom introduces many positional isomers in their closo clusters,  the most stable isomers do not always correspond to the most aromatic isomers in each cluster size  (see  Fig.  2.5). [54,57] For  example,  as  shown  in  Fig.  2.6,  the  most  stable  isomers  of [CB9H10] -and NB9H10 ( n = 10) are 1- isomers due to the topological charge stabilization of more electronegative C and N atoms. Nevertheless, their NICS(0) values are higher than those  of  the  less  stable  2-  isomers.  The  same  phenomena  apply  to  more  complex

dicarbaboranes CB n -2H n ; e.g. the most stable 1,10-C2B8H10 isomer ( n = 10) among seven positional isomers exhibits the highest NICS(0) value and the least aromaticity (see Fig. 2.5  and  2.7).  Therefore,  it  has  to  be  kept  in  mind  that  NICS(0)  values  alone  are  not sufficient to account for the stability of the closo clusters.

Fig.  2.5    Plots  of  NICS  (in  ppm)  at  the  center  of  all  positional  isomers  of  closo-monoazaborane (NBn -1Hn) (right) and closo-dicarbaborane (CBn -2Hn ) (left) (5 ≤ n ≤ 12). [54,57] The black square ( ■ ) shows the most stable isomer in each cluster size.

Fig. 2.6  Optimized geometries for [CB9H10] -(right) and NB9H10 (left) isomers (n = 10). [54,57]

Fig. 2.7  Optimized geometries for C2B8H10 isomers (n = 10). [54]

Another complementary way of assessing the relative stability of the closo clusters, also suggested by Schleyer et al., [54,57] is to calculate the reaction energies Δ H to form the most stable positional isomers in different cluster sizes n in the following fictional reactions:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

Fig. 2.8 (left) shows the cluster size ( n) dependence of the reaction energies Δ H of each cluster formation reaction. With increasing cluster size, the absolute value of Δ H tends to become larger, indicating higher stability. This trend is reconfirmed by the calculation of formation enthalpies Δ H 0 f of [B n H n ] 2 -(5 ≤ n ≤ 12), explaining why [B10H10] 2 -( n = 10) is experimentally  more  stable  than  [B 6 H6] 2 -( n =  6). [59] Also,  this  phenomenon  in  threedimensional clusters is very different from the two-dimensional Hückel aromatics such as annulenes and the polybenzenoid hydrocarbons, where  the total aromatic stabilization energies do not increase, and thus the average stability per C-H group decreases by size. [54] Additionally, the fluctuations from the lines in  Fig. 2.8 (right) become smaller in each cluster size as one B atom is replaced by a more electronegative C or N atom. However, the  total  stabilization  energies  also  become  smaller,  which  is  in  line  with  the  lower aromaticity described by NICS(0). But since these ab initio calculations are mostly done at a single cluster scale in the gas phase, the actual stability of the closo clusters may need to consider their periodic crystal structure and the presence of counter cations.

Fig. 2.8  Plots of the reaction energies Δ H (in kcal/mol) of closo-hydroborates [BnHn] 2 -and the most stable closo-monocarbaborate [CBn -1Hn] -and closo-monoazaborane NBn -1Hn isomers (5 ≤ n ≤ 12) (left). Plots of the cumulative addition energies Δ Hadd (in kcal/mol) of closo-hydroborates [BnHn] 2 -(5 ≤ n ≤ 17) (right). [54,57,60]

Fig. 2.9  Plot of NICS (in ppm) at the center of large closo-hydroborates [BnHn] 2 -(5 ≤ n ≤ 17) (right) and optimized geometry for [B17H17] 2 -(n = 17) (left). [60]

Schleyer et al.  also  applied  a  similar  method  to closo -hydroborates with a larger cluster size ( n &gt; 12) [60] by  revisiting  the  former study by Brown and Lipscomb [61] at  the B3LYP/6-31G* level of theory with the following fictional B-H addition reactions:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

The cluster size ( n) dependence of the cumulative addition energies Δ H add is shown in Fig. 2.8 (right). Similar to Δ H in Fig. 2.8 (left), the absolute value of Δ H add increases even at n &gt; 12, apart from the exceptional stability of [B 12H12] 2 -( n = 12). Among them, [B17H17] 2 -( n = 17)   seems  to  be  more  stable  than  [B 12H12] 2 -( n = 12),  which  is  supported  by  the corresponding lower NICS(0) value at n = 17 than at n = 12 (see Fig. 2.9). The relative stability  and  aromaticity  of  [B 17H17] 2 -compared  to  even  larger  hydroborates  are  also predicted  by  the  retro-ESCA  method,  chemical  bonding  analysis,  and  elongation  of [B12H12] 2 -with a five-membered B5H5 ring. [49,62,63] Unfortunately, experimental evidence for the  existence  of  the  [B 17H17] 2 -cluster  has  yet  to  be  found.  This  might  be  because  the exceptional  stability  of  [B 12H12] 2 -hinders  reaction  pathways  to  [B 17H17] 2 -with  a  stable intermediate  compound.  Nonetheless,  computational  studies  enable  the  prediction  of possible  stable  hydroborates  and  pre-screening  of  synthesis  feasibility.  Lipscomb  once suggested that ring closure or addition reactions of cis-conjuncto hydroborates such as B 14 H20 might be possible routes to the larger closo -hydroborates. [60,64]

## 2.2.3 Dimer and condensed hydroborates

Larger closo cluster cages are not the only form of hydroborates with large vertices. One of the  other  forms  is  a  dimer  of closo clusters.  According  to  Schleyer  et  al.  and  related experimental studies, the dimerization of [B12H12] 2 -( n = 12) to [B24H23] 3 -is suggested to occur  in  an  acidic  environment  or  at  high  external  potentials  in  multiple  steps:  (i) protonation  ([B 12H13] -),  (ii)  H 2 loss  ([B 12H11] -),  and  (iii)  subsequent  [B 12H11] -attack  to another [B12H12] 2 -to  form [B 24H23] 3 -. [65-67] The two B12 clusters in the B 24H23 3 -dimer are connected by one 3c2e B-H-B bridging bond. The B-H distance in the bridging bond is slightly extended (1.32 Å) compared to the rest of the B-H bonds (1.20 Å). Also, this B-HB bridging bond, which was computed to be linear by geometry optimization, is actually bent in the presence of counter cations and/or solvents, as shown in Fig. 2.10 (top). [68,69] A combined experimental and theoretical study even shows the formation of a [B36H34] 4 -trimer from [B12H11] -and [B24H23] 3 -upon the thermal protolysis of [H(H2O) m ] 2 [B12 H12] in acid. [70]

The dimers of [B10H10] 2 -( n = 10) have much more intriguing structural diversity. [71,72] Due to the lower stability of [B10H10] 2 -,  the  dimerization  of  [B 10H10] 2 -takes  place  more easily than that of [B 12H12] 2 -. After Lipscomb et al. confirmed cis -[B 20H18] 2 -formation from the  oxidation  of  [B 10H10] 2 -, [73,74] the  other  two  non-condensed  isomers  ( trans -  and iso -[B20H18] 2 -)  with  two  3c2e  B-H-B  bridging  bonds  have  been  found  (see  Fig.  2.10 (bottom)). [71,75-77] In  these  linked  dimers,  the  structure  of  each  B 10 closo cluster  remains unaltered.  They  can  be  further  reduced  and  oxidized  reversibly  from/to  [B 20H18] 4 -and

[B20H19] 3 -with one 2c2e B-B bridging bond between the apical and/or equatorial vertices of the two closo clusters. The redox reactions can also accompany the substitution of H atoms in the dimer with other functional groups, and some of the substituted derivatives can be used as a non-toxic selective target in boron neutron capture therapy for cancer treatment. [72] Moreover, this dimer has another condensed isomer ( fac -[B 20H18] 2 -), in which a closo cluster and a nido cluster share a triangle face, as shown in Fig. 2.10 (bottom). [78] This isomer exhibits the highest stability among other isomers, thanks to the incorporation of a stable icosahedral cluster.

The generalized electron counting of various types of hydroborates can be done by a  so-called mno rule,  proposed  by  Jemmis  et  al. [79] According  to  this  rule,  stable macropolyhedral systems including linked and condensed polyhedra should have m + n + o numbers of electron pairs, where m is the number of polyhedra, n is the number of vertices, and o is the number of single-vertex-sharing condensations. And Wade's n +1 electron pair rule is a special case of the mno rule at m = 1 and o = 0. As shown above, the fundamental understanding  of  hydroborates  and  their  structural  diversity  and  stability  have  been developed with the substantial help and advancement of theoretical calculations over the past decades.

Fig. 2.10  Optimized geometries for Li 3 B24 H23 (top)  and [B 20 H18 ] 2 -isomers (bottom). [65,71,78] White balls in Li 3 B24 H23 stand for Li + . Black and white balls in [B 20 H18 ] 2 -stand for B and B-H, respectively.

## 2.3 Ion conduction mechanism of hydroborates

Very recently, hydroborates, particularly closo -hydroborates including C-substituted derivatives, have drawn researchers' attention as promising solid electrolytes that combine liquid-like ionic conductivity, high chemical and electrochemical stability, low gravimetric density,  favorable  mechanical  properties,  and  low  toxicity. [4,19] Among  these  attractive material properties, this section focuses on the characteristics of superionic conduction of closo -hydroborates and their ion conduction mechanism.

The ionic conductivity σ of a solid is generally described by an activated hopping process of a charge-carrier ion from an occupied lattice site to a neighboring empty lattice site. [80] Although the energy trajectory varies with migration pathways, the energy barrier of  hopping  in  a  given  migration  direction  is  governed  by  the  Gibbs  free  energy  of  the

hopping process Δ G , which is deconvoluted into the migration enthalpy ( ≈ the activation energy E a in  superionic  conductors) [81] and  the  migration  entropy Δ S m .  Therefore,  the conductivity σ can be written as:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

In conventional hopping theory, [82] the prefactor σ 0 is

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

with a geometric factor z (including diffusion anisotropies and correlation factors; z ≤ 1), the density of charge carrier ions n ,  the charge of the ions Ze ,  the jump distance a 0 ,  the Boltzmann constant k B ,  and the jump frequency ν 0 ,  which is the product of the attempt frequency  and  the  jump  probability. [83] According  to  these  functions,  crystal  structures favorable  for  fast  ion  conduction  typically  contain  (i)  a  large  number  of  available crystallographic sites with high charge carrier density n , (ii) low activation barrier E a for jumps between adjacent sites, (iii) similar potential energies at the initial and final state of the jump to increase the probability of forward and backward jumps. For example, sulfide solid electrolytes with a body-centered cubic ( bcc ) structure are shown to have symmetric migration  pathways  with  the  lowest  energy  barrier  through  face-sharing  tetrahedraltetrahedral site hopping, as exemplified in Li 10 GeP2S12 (see Fig. 2.11). [84] To improve ionic conductivity, increasing the content of charge carriers by altering compositions is beneficial for a larger prefactor σ 0 . Or lowering the activation barriers E a e.g. by multi-ion concerted migration is  also  effective  to  broaden  the  diffusion  pathways  and  thereby  open  up  the structural bottlenecks for ion jumps in the crystal structure. [80,85] However, this is not always the case e.g. in Li 10 GeP2S12 when the isotropy of three-dimensional diffusion pathways is diminished by increasing the unit cell volume. [86] Moreover, increasing the polarizability of the anion framework (= "softening" a lattice) is proposed to lower the Debye frequencies, which correspond to a vibration mode across the saddle point of the migration energy landscape, and hence reduces the activation barrier. [84,86,87] However, a "softer" lattice also lowers the prefactor σ 0 by decreasing the migration entropy Δ S m (influenced by nearly all vibrational frequencies around the migration pathway), the attempt frequency, and hence the  jump  frequency ν 0 .  This  dilemma  shows  a  trade-off  between  the  prefactor σ 0 and activation energy E a for higher ionic conductivity σ to design the structure of superionic conductors. [80]

Fig. 2.11  Cation migration pathways (left) and calculated energy paths (right) in (a) bcc-, (b) fcc-, and (c) hcp-type anion lattices. [84]

In contrast, the ion conduction in closo -hydroborates works differently from that in sulfide and oxide solid electrolytes. First of all, superionic conduction in closo -hydroborates is  normally  achieved  by  an  order-disorder  phase  transition;  and  the  disordered,  hightemperature phases can be stabilized at room temperature by hydroborate anion mixing. [8892] Unlike highly conductive sulfides, [84] most of the high-temperature phases form a facecentered  cubic  ( fcc )  or  hexagonal  close-packed  ( hcp )  structure,  except  a bcc structure  of Na2B12H12 and Na-1-CB9H10. [20] Furthermore, decreasing the charge-carrier concentration by substitution of one B atom by C increases the ionic conductivity and reduces the phasetransition temperature, [93,94] which does not fit the classical hopping model. To explain the fundamental  difference  in  the  ion  conduction  mechanism,  Kweon  et  al.  proposed  a combination  of  three  types  of  frustrations  based  on ab  initio molecular  dynamics simulations (see Fig. 2.12). [95-97]

Fig.  2.12    Effects  of  structural,  chemical,  and  dynamical  frustration  on  the  potential  energy landscape. [96]

The first factor is structural frustration derived from lattice site preferences. [96] Since metal hydroborates are ionic salts, the preferred coordination environment and interstitial sites of cations can be predicted by the cation-to-anion radius ratio, according to the first Pauling's  rule  with  a  hard-sphere,  close-packed  approximation. [20,98] This  parameter basically governs the anion lattice structure, types, and probability of interstitial sites for cations and vacancies, which constructs the basic energy landscape of migration pathways with local minima at each interstitial site. Vacancies are formed by an intrinsic mismatch between stoichiometry and preferred interstitial sites. The cation conduction is aided by the  availability  (=  density)  and  accessibility  of  interstitial  sites  at  sufficiently  low  local minima; i.e. less preferred interstitial sites and cation ordering can naturally flatten the overall energy landscape. In particular, the high density of tetrahedral sites, e.g. in the bcc structure shown in Li10 GeP2S12, [84] combined with high vacancy concentrations to tolerate disordering, is likely to be beneficial for ion conduction in Li and Na closo -hydroborates. However, as mentioned above, the structure factor cannot solely account for exceptional ionic conductivity in the fcc or hcp structure.

The second factor is chemical frustration between the local anion geometry and crystal lattice symmetry. [96] In the case of [B 12 H12] 2 -anions and Li + or Na + cations, cations tend  to  be  bound  above  the  triad  center  of  B  atoms  by  short-range  cation -anion interactions,  which  creates  a  preferred  cation  distribution  based  on  the  icosahedral symmetry of the [B12H12] 2 -anions. However, this cation distribution is incompatible with the preferred symmetry of the occupied lattice interstitial sites. This incompatibility and hence intrinsic frustration create additional local minima in the energy landscape, thereby

facilitating cation migration. For example, trigonal and linear site distributions in the fcc lattice  of  Li 2 B12 H12  are  incompatible  with  the  possible  anion  orientations,  inducing symmetry frustration compared to the bcc lattice. This type of frustration is also observed in halide solid electrolytes. [99]

The  last  factor  is  dynamic  frustration  caused  by  anion  rotation. [96] Because  the frequency  range  of  vibrational,  librational,  and  rotational  (=  reorientational)  modes  of anions overlap significantly with the frequency range of cation-derived modes, the cations docked to anions can be carried and subsequently released by the rotation of hydroborate anions.  This  anion  rotation  dynamically  alters  the  nature  of  the  above-mentioned symmetry incompatibility and fluctuates the underlying potential energy landscape, which enhances long-range diffusion and local  mobility of  cations.  This  type  of  frustration  is prominent after the transition to the disordered phase, as reported by Lu and Ciucci and Sau et al. [100-102] Advanced molecular dynamics simulations show that the phase transition causes  significantly  high  anion  reorientational  motion  as  well  as  lattice  parameter expansion. As shown in Fig. 2.13, the reorientation of anions distributes cations to other interstitial  sites  with  relatively  low  occupancy,  creates  well-connected  cation  diffusion pathways, and lowers the energy barrier for cation hopping. In the case of the fcc structure such  as  Li 2 B12 H12  and  Na2B10H10,  the  occupancy  difference  between  tetrahedral  and octahedral sites becomes smaller upon the phase transition with cell volume expansion of the unchanged crystal structure, facilitating tetrahedral-octahedral-tetrahedral site hoping. [100-102] These observations fit well with the acceleration of anion reorientational jump rates and cation  diffusion  upon  order-disorder  phase  transitions  of  hydroborates shown in dynamical studies. [103-105]

Fig. 2.13  Li + distribution isosurfaces in a single unit cell (top) and Li + occupancy at respective Li sites with varied lattice parameters (bottom) for the low-temperature ( α ) and high-temperature ( β ) phases of Li 2B12H12. [101]

This concept can be extended to account for the enhanced ionic conduction and lower  phase-transition  temperature  of closo -monocarbaborates,  i.e.  C-substituted closo -hydroborates. [97,102] In the case of LiCB 11 H12, the anion lattice forms the fcc symmetry. The more positive exo -H atom in the C-H bond than in other B-H bonds induces a stronger local repulsion to a cation, locating the cations to the farthest triad from the C atom in the cage anion. This preferred orientation by local anion-cation Coulomb interaction introduces frustration  between  the  lattice  symmetry  and  the  internal  symmetry  of  the  anion. Moreover, the asymmetry in anions generated by the stronger repulsion produces a higher degree  of  disorder  in  the  system,  reducing  anion reorientational barrier  and  the corresponding phase-transition temperature. [102,106]  The asymmetric [CB11H12] -anions also increase  the  effective  anion  radius  by  rotation,  which  enhances  cell  volume  expansion upon phase transition, in contrast to symmetric [B 12H12] 2 -anions. [102] In addition, the half cation  occupancy  in  [CB 11H12] -and  [CB 9 H10] -compared to  in  [B 12H12] 2 -and  [B 10H10] 2 -produces  more  vacancies  and  more  uniform  cation  distributions  to  tetrahedral  and octahedral sites with almost no cation-cation correlation. These contribute to facilitated anion reorientational motion  in the disordered phases of closo -monocarbaborates, according to dynamical studies. [97,103] On the other hand, mixed hydroborate anions are known  to  exhibit  no  phase  transition.  Interestingly,  experimental  studies  on  their  ion

dynamics show that chemical frustration (e.g. short-range cation-cation and cation-anion correlation) in the intermediate temperature regime is involved in relatively high activation energy  before  dynamic  frustration  becomes  dominant  at  elevated  temperatures. [107] Although the effects of monovalency in closo -monocarbaborate anion and of anion mixing have yet to be fully investigated so far, the three types of frustration and additional factors mentioned above serve to flatten the energy landscape of cation migration, resulting in high ionic conductivity.

Divalent  cations  can  also  introduce  cation  vacancies;  however,  Lu  and  Ciucci demonstrated that the diffusion of divalent cations (Mg 2+ and Ca 2+ ) is much slower in a lattice of [B 12H12] 2 -anions than the monovalent ones. [106] Therefore, the partial substitution of the monovalent cations by divalent ones reduces ionic conductivity, which is in line with the  results  in  halide  solid  electrolytes. [108] Additionally,  apart  from  the  C-H  bond  in [CB11H12] -, the relative Mulliken charges of [B 12H12] 2 -and [CB11H12] -anions exhibit almost charge neutrality of exo -H atoms, [93] which mitigate anion-cation Coulomb interactions. Therefore,  partial  or  full  halogenation  of closo -hydroborates  lowers  ionic  conductivity because more negatively charged halogen atoms than H atoms trap cations more due to stronger Coulomb interactions. [109,110] This aligns hydroborate anions in the crystal structure and hinders anion reorientational motions, which is unfavorable for ion conduction. In summary, computational methods serve as a powerful tool to understand the dynamics of ion conduction in hydroborate solid electrolytes, providing design principles for fast ion conductors.

## 2.4 Material properties of hydroborate solid electrolytes

## 2.4.1 Intrinsic electrochemical stability &amp; Interface stability

In  addition  to  ionic  conductivity,  solid  electrolytes  need  to  meet  many  criteria  for successful  implementation  in  all-solid-state  batteries.  Among  them,  one  of  the  key parameters for stable battery cycling is the intrinsic electrochemical stability window of the solid electrolytes. In thermodynamic equilibrium, it can be evaluated by the construction of ground-potential phase diagrams under external potentials based on density functional theory (DFT) calculations. The consideration of external potentials was proposed by Zhu et al. [111,112] and Richards et al. [113,114] In this methodology, a stable phase can be predicted by computing the lower convex hull of the grand potential Φ (= the characteristic state function of the grand canonical ensemble) in a given chemical and composition space. In Eq. (2.7), μ M is the chemical potential of the alkali metal M , while nM ( c ) and E ( c ) represent the  number  of  the  alkali  metal  and  the  enthalpy  of  composition c ,  respectively.  Phase diagrams and E ( c ) are based on DFT-computed bulk energies of materials obtained from databases  such  as  the  Inorganic  Crystal  Structure  Database  (ICSD)  and  the  Materials Project. [115]

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

As shown above, the electrochemical potential μ M is  dependent  on  its  chemical potential of elemental M μ 0 M and applied external potential ϕ (referenced to M ). Therefore, by  computing  the  grand-potential  phase  diagrams  with ϕ varied,  the  electrochemical stability window of a phase of interest can be calculated as the range of potentials ϕ within which the specific phase is the most stable. Note that no kinetic effects are considered and that the compositional phase diagrams are constructed at the ground state (0 K and 0 atm); therefore,  there  would  be  small  changes  in  free  energy  at  ambient  temperature  and pressure.

One of the simplest examples is the calculated electrochemical stability windows of Li and Na binary compounds shown in Fig. 2.14 (top). It is worth noting that all of them are thermodynamically stable down to 0 V vs M + / M ( M = Li, Na) and basically govern the oxidative stability limit of more complex compounds. [113,114] For example, the oxidative stability  of  LiCl  (~4.2  V  vs  Li + /Li)  determines  the  oxidative  stability  limit  of  LiM -Cl ternary compounds. [116,117] Two of the exceptions are hydroborates and phosphates, whose oxidative stability is not limited by that of M H and M 2 O (or M 3 P), respectively, thanks to strongly bound polyanions. [113]

Lu and Ciucci, and Lacivita et al. applied this methodology to some of the metal hydroborates, e.g. LiBH4, Li2B12H12, NaBH4, and Na2B12H12. [106,113,114] Fig.  2.14 (bottom) shows the compositional phase diagram of Li-B-H and calculated electrochemical stability windows of the four kinds of hydroborates. For reductive stability, Li hydroborates are shown to be thermodynamically unstable in contact with Li metal (0 V vs Li + /Li), while Na equivalents are shown to be stable with Na metal (0 V vs Na + /Na = ~0.3 V vs Li + /Li). These estimations are confirmed experimentally by interphase formation from LiBH4 in contact with Li metal and by the absence of interphase formation from Na4 (B12 H12)(B10 H10) in contact with Na metal, respectively. [118,119] Furthermore, the stability of decomposition products also directly influence interface stability. At very low potentials (~0 V vs Li + /Li), Li hydroborates are calculated to be reduced to binary compounds such as LiH and LiB, which  can  serve  as  an  interface  passivating  layer. [106] In  fact,  the  above-mentioned interphase formed between LiBH4 and Li metal is shown to be stable over electrochemical Li  deposition/depletion  in  a  Li  symmetric  cell. [118] For  oxidative  stability,  [BH 4 ] -is calculated to be decomposed to [B12H12] 2 -above 2 V vs M + / M ( M = Li, Na), [106,113] which matches experimental observations and the thermal decomposition behavior (see Chapter 4). [120-124] The [B12H12] 2 -interphase formed by [BH4] -decomposition enables battery cycling with LiBH4 above its oxidative stability limit. [125] On the other hand, [B 12H12] 2 -is calculated to decompose to neutral B9H11 above 3.5 V vs M + / M ( M = Li, Na), [106,114] which is not far from experimental observation in Na2B12H12 above 3.45 V vs Na + /Na (see Chapter 5). However, this calculation result does not consider dimerization to [B24H23] 3 -(= two B 12 clusters connected by one 3c2e B-H-B bridging bond) [66-68] that was observed in a [B 12H12] 2 -solution. [69,70] Since the structural stability of the B 12 icosahedra prevents cage opening or change in the cluster size, dimerization is expected to occur also in the solid state. This mismatch  is  due  to  the  absence  of  information  on  dimers  in  the  available  databases,

indicating the incapability of stability calculations based on existing databases that may not list all possible decomposition products.

Fig. 2.14  Electrochemical stability windows of Li and Na binary compounds with oxidation products at the oxidative stability limit (top). [114] Compositional phase diagram of Li-B-H and electrochemical stability  windows of Li and Na hydroborates calculated from the grand-potential phase diagrams (bottom). [106]

Partial or full halogenation (F, Cl, Br) of closo -hydroborates is expected theoretically and  shown  experimentally  to  enhance  the  electrochemical  oxidative  stability. [126-128] However, as discussed above, halogenation generally tends to hinder the dynamic motion of the hydroborate anions and hence lowers the ionic conductivity by several orders of magnitudes; [109,110] therefore, a compromise has to be found between the ionic conductivity and oxidative  stability.  Alternatively,  halogenated  hydroborates  may  be  used  as  a  thin cathode protection layer, for which the ionic conductivity is less crucial than for bulk solid electrolytes.

The  stability  calculations  determined  from  the  phase  diagrams  were  further employed  to  evaluate  the  interfacial  reactivity  between  various  solid  electrolytes  and cathodes. [113,114,129] A heat map can be drawn by calculating the maximum driving force of inter-mixing reactions between a solid electrolyte material and a cathode active material. However,  as  is  the  case  with  electrochemical  stability  calculations,  the  reliability  of interface  stability  calculations  is  limited  by  the  physical  property  data  available  in  the databases. In other words, although the calculations are in principle correct, incomplete knowledge  of  the  various  reaction  intermediates  and  products  misleads  to  wrong conclusions. For example, one of the decomposition products from the interfacial reactions between Na2B12H12 and half-charged Na0.5CrO2 at ~3 V vs Na + /Na is calculated as NaBH4, even though NaBH4 is calculated to be unstable above 2 V vs Na + /Na. [114] Therefore, more experimental data on thermodynamic properties of reaction intermediates and products are required to improve the theoretical predictions for hydroborates, for which thermal and electrochemical decomposition pathways in the solid state and the effect of heteroatom(s) and of chemical modifications are yet underexplored.

## 2.4.2 Mechanical properties

Since the initial contact and stability of solid-solid interfaces are crucial for stable all-solidstate battery cycling, the mechanical properties of solid electrolyte materials have a large influence on device processing. One of the important parameters is the so-called Pugh's ratio  ( B / G ),  which  is  a  ratio  of  the  bulk  modulus  ( B )  and  the  shear  modulus  ( G ). [130] Materials  with B / G &gt;  1.75  are  classified  as  "ductile,"  while B / G &lt;  1.75  classified  as "brittle." Lu and Ciucci conducted ab initio calculations of bulk and shear modulus and Pugh's ratio for M BH4, M 2 B10H10, and M 2 B12 H12 ( M = Li, Na). They clarified that all of these hydroborates are likely ductile in nature ( B / G &gt; 1.75 or G / B &lt; 0.57) with B and G up  to  17 GPa  and  9 GPa,  respectively  (see  Fig.  2.15  (top)). [106] This  characteristic  is advantageous for facile pelletization by simple cold pressing compared to brittle oxide solid electrolytes which require sintering at high temperature, which is similar to sulfide solid electrolytes but with even lower bulk and shear modulus, and hence Young's modulus (see Fig. 2.15 (bottom)). [106,131] The lower values of "ductile" hydroborates than other classes of materials can be possibly due to the rotational flexibility of hydroborate anions. [106] In most cases, Li hydroborates exhibit higher bulk and shear modulus than Na hydroborates, which trend is in line with other material classes of solid electrolytes. [106] Moury et al. further discovered  the  phase  transitions  of  Na 2 B12 H12  at  high  pressure  with  a  decrease  in  bulk modulus,  for  which  the  experimental  and  computational  values  agree  well. [132] This suggests that pelletizing conditions influence the mechanical properties and their hysteresis as well as the relative density of ductile solid electrolyte pellets.

Fig.  2.15    Calculated  shear  and  bulk  moduli  and Pugh's  ratio  of  Li/Na  metal  and  Li/Na hydroborates (top) and plot of the calculated shear modulus (G) vs bulk modulus (B) for different classes of superionic solid electrolytes (bottom). [106,133] Dashed lines represent iso-Young's modulus lines in GPa.

The shear modulus of a solid electrolyte is also proposed to be one of the indicators for dendrite formation, which is required to be more than double the shear modulus of the respective alkali metal to hinder dendrite formation. [134] This corresponds to around 8 GPa and 7 GPa for Li and Na metal at room temperature, respectively. Not all the shear moduli of  hydroborates  exceed  these  criteria  according  to  Fig.  2.15  (top); [106] however,  the experimental validations of the criteria have to be carried out carefully because the surface conditions  of  solid  electrolytes  as  well  as  temperature  and  applied  external  pressure severely  affect  the  dendrite  formation. [135-139] Moreover,  superionic  hydroborate  solid electrolytes typically comprise mixed hydroborate anions. Thus, the effect of anion mixing on mechanical properties also needs to be taken into account in future studies.

## 2.5  Conclusion

Computational  studies  have  elucidated  the  fundamental  characteristics  of  delocalized chemical  bonds  and  the  unusual  stability  of  hydroborates  over  the  history  of  boron chemistry research. Also, they have recently facilitated the understanding of hydroborates as fast ion-conducting solid electrolytes for all-solid-state batteries. The current research topics  of  hydroborate  solid  electrolytes  focus  on closo -  and nido -hydroborates, closo -monocarbaborates, and their halogenated derivatives with 10 or 12 vertices ( n = 10, 12). Material  properties  requested  for  solid  electrolytes,  e.g.  electrochemical  stability  and mechanical  stability,  are  computationally  predicted  for  some  of  these  hydroborates, enabling direct comparison to other material classes of solid electrolytes.

Calculations  of  large  hydroborates  can  be  quite  time-consuming  due  to  many constituent atoms per anion and per unit cell. Particularly in the disordered, frustrated phases of closo -hydroborates and monocarbaborates, partial occupancies of cations require a large supercell, thereby further increasing  the  computational  costs.  The  heavy computational  costs  limit  the size  and  accuracy  of  simulations;  therefore,  higher computational power and better modeling/calculation methods are awaited for further developments. Also, modeling is based on many assumptions, which have to be carefully made with a comprehensive understanding of real phenomena observed in experiments. Otherwise,  calculation  results  of  fictional  compounds/reactions  may  deliver  no  valid insight.  Furthermore,  experimental  data  on  the  physical/thermodynamic  properties  of hydroborates are still lacking. Expanding the current database or creating a new database specialized for cluster compounds would improve the quality of computational studies of hydroborates.

## Chapter 3 Experimental Methods

## 3.1 Material preparation

Electrolyte and electrode materials are mainly prepared in the solid state, especially via a mechanical milling process. Experimental details and other preparation processes done by our collaborators such as the wet-chemical synthesis of nido -hydroborates can be found in the experimental section of each paper in Chapter 4-8.

Ball milling is a grinding technique in which a sample to be ground is confined in a rotating or shaking grinding vial with balls. The material of the vial and balls is chosen to be harder than the sample: typically hardened steel, tungsten carbide, and yttria-stabilized zirconia.  The  sample  is  ground  and  homogenized  due  to  the  impact  of  the  balls.  Ball milling, or more specifically high-energy ball milling, can also be used as a solvent-free, mechanochemical synthesis technique. Milling of the educts leads to grain size reduction and the increase in the contact area, both enhancing the reaction kinetics. Mechanically induced heat increases the local temperature inside the milling vial, facilitating chemical reactions.

In this thesis, especially in Chapter 5 and 6, high-energy ball milling (often with subsequent heat treatment) was used mainly for anion mixing of two types of hydroborates to stabilize a highly conducting, high-temperature phase, e.g. Na4 (CB11H12)2(B12H12) and Na4(B12H12)(B10H10).  Because  the  crystallite  size  reduction  helps  to  enhance  the  ionic conductivity  of  hydroborates,  individual closo -hydroborates  Na2B10H10,  Na2B12 H12,  and NaCB11H12, were also ball-milled to increase their conductivity and hence the sensitivity to electrochemical stability measurements. Additionally, ball milling was employed for the solid-state synthesis of cathode active materials in order to mix reagents and/or to reduce crystallite size before/after the calcination in a furnace.

For the anion mixing, the dissolution of the hydroborate components in a solvent and subsequent heat treatment can yield comparable ionic conductivity to the ball-milled samples in some  of the hydroborate combinations, e.g. Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) and Li2 (CB9H10)(CB11H12) as discussed in Chapter  4.  This  characteristic  of  the  mixed-anion hydroborates  enables  intimate  contact  between  a  solid  electrolyte  and  electrode  active material by electrolyte solution impregnation and heat treatment. Such electrolyte coating on a cathode active material was applied to prepare cathode composites for all-solid-state batteries as presented in Chapter 5 and 6.

## 3.2 Material characterization

## 3.2.1 X-ray diffraction

X-ray diffraction (XRD) is a non-destructive technique to gain structural information of (typically) crystalline materials. A crystalline lattice acts like an optical grid for X-rays (and neutrons), causing constructive interference in well-defined directions (Bragg reflections). The  diffraction  pattern,  i.e.  the  angular  distribution  of  the  Bragg  reflections  and  their relative intensities reflects the symmetry and position of atoms within the crystalline lattice. Therefore, X-ray diffraction can be used as a fingerprinting technique to identify known phases, where crystallographic information is already available. Moreover, its structural

details can be extracted by Rietveld refinement. For unknown phases, the crystal structure needs to be resolved prior to the refinement.

In this thesis, XRD was used extensively to characterize the phase purity and crystal structure of synthesized electrolyte and electrode materials, e.g. for Na 4 (CB11H12)2(B12 H12) and Na3(VOPO4)2F in Chapter 5 and for Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) and NaTMO2 (TM = Cr, Mn, Fe) in Chapter 6. In the latter, the thermal decomposition behaviors and reaction products of the cathode composites were also investigated by temperature-dependent synchrotron X-ray diffraction, benefitting from the short measurement time and high-quality data. In the case of mixed-anion hydroborates, a stabilized high-temperature phase through anion mixing  can  be  identified  by  comparing  their  crystal  structures  with  those  of  the  hightemperature phases of the component anions, e.g. high-temperature phase stabilization of Na2B10H10 in Na4(B12H12)(B10 H10) or that of Na 2 B12 H12 in Na4(CB11H12)2(B12H12). Moreover, from the characteristic Bragg reflections, the crystal structures of new mixed anions with nido -hydroborates were solved and refined in Chapter 7 and 8.

## 3.2.2 Thermal analysis

Differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) is a thermoanalytical technique to determine the heat capacity as a  function of temperature and thereby  to identify phase transitions or thermal reactions. DSC measures the difference in energy input between a sample and reference material in the identical temperature program. In DSC curves, phase transitions or thermal reactions, for example, are expressed as exothermic/endothermic events due to the release/accumulation of the latent heat or reaction heat, respectively. In the case of open  crucibles,  DSC  often  accompanies  thermogravimetry  (TG)  to  measure  a  weight change between a sample and reference material, derived from e.g. thermal reactions or absorption/desorption. DSC-TG can also be combined with mass spectrometry (MS) to detect  the  mass-to-charge  ratio  ( m / z )  of  charged  fragment  species  emitted  by  thermal reactions or desorption as a function of temperature.

In this thesis, DSC was used to confirm the absence of phase transition in a mixedanion  hydrobortate  Na4(CB11H12)2(B12H12)  in  Chapter  5.  Combined  DSC-TG-MS  was employed to characterize the onset and progression of thermal decomposition in another mixed-anion hydrobortate Na4(B12H12)(B10H10), NaTMO2 (TM = Cr, Mn, Fe), and their cathode composites in Chapter 6. In this study, DSC signals were calibrated with reference materials to quantify the heat flow in the unit of W g -1 . For MS, a constant set of fragment species was applied to detect, among which only the selected ones with signal changes were  shown  to  discuss  possible  thermal  decomposition  pathways.  The  obtained  onset temperatures were compared with the  previous data  using a sulfide  and  selenide  solid electrolyte to demonstrate the relatively low reactivity of the hydrobortate solid electrolyte.

## 3.2.3 Scanning electron microscopy

Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) is an imaging technique to produce nm- or μ m-scale images  of  a  sample  with  a  focused  beam  of  electrons.  SEM  images  are  obtained  by

detecting e.g. secondary electrons emitted from sample atoms upon the interaction with an electron beam. When the energy of the electron beam is high enough to remove an inner shell  electron  from  the  sample,  characteristic  X-rays  are  emitted  upon  recombination, reflecting the element-specific energy difference between the electronic shells. Analysis of the energy of the emitted X-rays enables the elemental analysis of the sample by energydispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDX).

In this thesis, SEM was used to visualize the particle size and morphology of asprepared Na4(CB11H12)2(B12H12) and Na3(VOPO4)2F, and SEM-EDX was used to map the constituent  atoms  in  a  4 V-class  cathode  composite  in  Chapter  5.  Also,  cross-sectional SEM  was  employed  with  a  transfer  module  to  assess  morphological  changes  at  the cathode/electrolyte interface before and after all-solid-state battery cycling without sample exposure to air in Chapter 5.

## 3.3 Electrochemical characterization

## 3.3.1 Voltammetric methods

Voltammetric methods are a standard, dynamic electrochemical technique, in which a current  response  of  a  cell  is  measured  in  two-  or  three-electrode  configuration  while dynamically changing the applied potentials between a working and reference electrode. In linear sweep voltammetry, the potential is swept linearly with time, i.e. at a constant scan  rate  (in  mV s -1 classically  applied  in  a  cell  with  a  liquid  electrolyte).  Cyclic voltammetry sweeps potentials at a constant scan rate repeatedly with alternating scan directions between the upper and lower cutoff potentials. Electrochemical oxidation (= anodic) currents are normally defined as positive values, whereas reduction (= cathodic) currents defined as negative. Dynamic response of the anodic and cathodic currents to potential  sweeping  helps  to  identify  the  potentials,  pathways,  and  reversibility  of electrochemical redox reactions at the working electrode. Note that because linear sweep or cyclic voltammetry sweeps potentials regardless of the completion of electrochemical reactions at each redox potential, they are more likely to skip or overlook electrochemical reactions with slow kinetics or sequential reactions with a rate-limiting step, compared to the following galvanostatic methods.

In  this  thesis,  voltammetric  techniques  were  extensively  used  to  study  the electrochemical  oxidative  stability  of  hydroborate  solid  electrolytes  in  a  two-electrode configuration. In Chapter 4, 7, and 8, linear sweep voltammetry was used to determine the onset potential of electrochemical oxidation of the solid electrolytes, combining a low scan rate  of  10 μ V s -1 ,  electrolyte/carbon  composites,  and  linear  fitting  of  background  and anodic  currents.  The  same  approach  was  employed  for  ball-milled  samples  of  the individual hydroborate components (Na2B10H10, Na2B12H12, and NaCB11H12) to distinguish the contributions of the more or less stable hydroborate anion components to the mixedanion solid electrolytes Na 4 (CB11H12)2(B12 H12) and Na4(B12H12)(B10 H10) in Chapter 5 and 6, respectively.  Furthermore,  this  method  was  extended  to  cyclic  voltammograms  with  a stepwise increase in the upper cutoff cell voltage to characterize the reversibility of the electrolyte decomposition reactions in different voltage ranges in Chapter 5.

## 3.3.2 Galvanostatic methods

Galvanostatic methods are another standard electrochemical technique with chronopotentiometry, in which a constant current is applied to the working electrode and a potential response from an electrochemical cell is measured with respect to the reference electrode  potential  in  two-  or  three-electrode  configuration.  In  the  two-electrode  setup where  a reference electrode material also functions as a counter electrode, the measurement continues until the cell voltage reaches a defined cutoff value. For cycling experiments,  the  same  constant  current  with  alternating  current  directions  is  typically applied repeatedly between the upper and lower cutoff voltages. In the field of battery research,  applied  constant  currents  are  typically  defined  as  C-rates,  where n C  takes 1/ n hours to fully charge/discharge a cell. 1C (in mA g -1 ) is often based on the theoretical capacity (in mAh g -1 ) of an electrode active material or its practical capacity with a given cutoff voltage. In galvanostatic experiments, a tested cell exhibits overpotentials η , which is the sum of ohmic overpotential η ohm (derived from e.g. resistance of bulk electrolyte and other  cell  components),  charge-transfer  overpotential η ct (derived  from  e.g.  reaction kinetics or interfacial resistance or interphase formation), and concentration overpotential η cont (derived from e.g. concentration gradients when charge transfer processes occur faster than ion diffusion). Keeping η to low values is key for stable cycling and high rate capability of all-solid-state batteries.

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

In  this  thesis,  galvanostatic  measurements  were  widely  used  to  characterize  the electrochemical  performance  of  solid  electrolytes  and  electrodes  in  all-solid-state  cells. First, the galvanostatic measurements were used as a complementary method to confirm electrochemical  oxidation  of  solid  electrolytes  in  the  electrolyte/carbon  composites  in Chapter 4 and 6. Second, symmetric cells, sandwiching hydroborate solid electrolytes with sodium or lithium metal, were cycled galvanostatically to assess the compatibility of the electrolytes with alkali metal anodes and the absence of dendrite growth with an applied current and capacity in Chapter 5, 7, and 8. Finally, all-solid-state battery performance employing cathode active materials in the cathode composite and alkali metal anodes was tested by galvanostatic cycling with a given mass loading and C-rate in Chapter 5 and 8. Cycling performance with a high mass loading and C-rates was also examined for high areal capacity and rate capability, respectively. Also, the evolution of overpotentials during long-term galvanostatic cycling was associated with the capacity and energy retention of all-solid-state cells.

## 3.3.3 Electrochemical impedance  spectroscopy

Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) is an electrochemical technique to measure a frequency-dependent response of a cell to oscillating voltages or currents. The former, potentiostatic mode is more common, where sinusoidal voltage oscillation is applied to a cell with a fixed amplitude (in mV for a linear response) in the frequency range from MHz

to  mHz.  Considering  the  phase  difference  between  the  voltage  oscillation  and  current response, impedance Z is defined as a complex analogue of resistance from Ohm's law, comprised of the real part Z ' and imaginary part Z ":

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

The obtained frequency-dependent impedance data are often shown in a Nyquist plot ( Z " vs Z '), which can be fitted by an equivalent circuit based on the understanding or interpretation of a measurement system. This method is a powerful technique because EIS can  separately  characterize  different  contributions  to  resistive  and  capacitive  (and sometimes  inductive)  impedance  in  a  complex  system.  For  instance,  an  interface impedance can be expressed as a semi-circle in a Nyquist plot, using an RC parallel circuit with resistance R , capacitance C , and characteristic frequency ω = 1/ RC .

Moreover,  when  EIS  is  performed  for  a  solid  electrolyte  in  cylindrical  pellet geometry that is  sandwiched between ion-blocking electrodes in  two-  or  four-electrode configuration, ionic conductivity (or total conductivity in the presence of grain boundary) of the electrolyte σ can be calculated from pellet area A , and pellet thickness l , and bulk resistance R (estimated from Z ') by definition:

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

In this thesis, EIS was mainly used to characterize the interface behaviors in allsolid-state  cells.  In  Chapter  5,  the  interphase  formation  by  electrochemical  electrolyte oxidation and its cycle and potential dependence at a cathode/electrolyte interface were characterized by combined EIS and galvanostatic cycling with a stepwise increase in the upper cutoff  voltage  at  every  five  cycles.  In  Chapter  6,  such  interphase  formation  was observed with a sequential increase in the upper cutoff voltage during the first charging. In both cases, EIS measurements were performed at a steady state reached either by an opencircuit  step  or  by  a  potentiostatic  step  to  reduce  capacitive  noises.  Additionally,  the compatibility  of  a nido -hydroborate-based  electrolyte  with  a  lithium  metal  anode  was analyzed  by  time-dependent  cell  impedance  growth  in  Chapter  8.  The  temperature dependence  of  the  ionic  conductivity  of  mix-anion  hydroborate  solid  electrolytes  was measured by EIS in Chapter 5, 7, and 8.

## Chapter 4 Electrochemical Oxidative Stability of Hydroborate Solid Electrolytes

To achieve high operating cell voltages in all-solid-state batteries, knowing the intrinsic electrochemical stability of solid electrolytes, especially at high electrochemical potentials, is a primary step to design a stable electrode/electrolyte interface. For hydroborate solid electrolytes, previous experimental studies claimed electrochemical stability windows of &gt;5 V;  however,  this  contradicts  first-principles  calculations  predicting  lower  values. Furthermore, no stable cycling of bulk-type hydroborate-based all-solid-state batteries with high-voltage cathodes has been reported.

To  clarify  this  discrepancy  in  this  study,  we  establish  a  robust  experimental methodology  based  on  linear  sweep  voltammetry  to  determine  the  electrochemical oxidative  stability  of  hydroborate  solid  electrolytes.  Using  LiBH 4 as  a  model  solid electrolyte, we employ solid electrolyte/carbon composites as a working electrode and a low  scan  rate  of  10 μ V s -1 to  accelerate  kinetics  and  to  improve  the  sensitivity  to decomposition. This method enhances the decomposition currents for 10 3 times (from nA to μ A scale) and becomes robust with respect to the current collector material and the ionic conductivity of the electrolyte. By optimizing the type and ratio of conductive carbon in the composite, the oxidative stability of LiBH 4 is determined to be 2.0 V vs Li + /Li with a linear fitting of background and decomposition currents in Pt/LiBH4-carbon/LiBH4/Li cells. The measured oxidative stability limit is in good agreement with the value predicted by first-principles calculations, revealing overestimation in previous reports. The irreversible electrochemical decomposition outside the oxidative stability limit is supported by galvanostatic cycling in the same cell configuration. We also confirm the applicability of this method to selected, highly conductive hydroborate solid electrolytes, emphasizing the  need  to  measure  the  accurate  oxidative  stability  of  solid  electrolytes  for  a  proper interface  design  strategy  for  all-solid-state  batteries.  In  the  context  of  research  on hydroborate  solid  electrolytes,  this  study  introduces  a  more  accurate  electrochemical characterization tool. The established method is extensively employed to determine the electrochemical  oxidative  stability  of  several  kinds  of  mixed-anion  hydroborate  solid electrolytes and their component anions in the later chapters.

## Electrochemical Oxidative Stability of Hydroborate-Based SolidState Electrolytes

Ryo Asakura, † , ‡ Le ́ o Duche ̂ ne, † , ‡ Ruben-Simon Ku ̈ hnel, † Arndt Remhof, * , † Hans Hagemann, ‡ †
