## Chapter 6

Thermal and Electrochemical Interface Compatibility of a closo -Hydroborate Solid Electrolyte in Cathode Composites

The extension of cell voltage and cathode-based specific energy of all-solid-state sodium batteries is demonstrated in Chapter 6, whereas improved operational safety is another major advantage of all-solid-state batteries to lithium-ion batteries. Ensuring operational safety  requires  thermal  stability  of  solid  electrolytes  and  interface  compatibility  with electrodes at high electrochemical potentials or temperatures, which has been studied only partially for hydroborates in the context of hydrogen storage materials.

As a first systematic study on the compatibility of hydroborate solid electrolytes, therefore,  we  investigate  a  combination  of  a  mixed-anion closo -hydroborate  solid electrolyte  Na 4 (B12 H12)(B10 H10)  and  3 V-class,  layered  sodium  transition  metal  oxide cathodes NaTMO2 (TM = Cr, Mn, Fe). The solid electrolyte itself exhibits high intrinsic electrochemical oxidative stability and thermal stability, and its oxidative stability is found to be limited by the less stable [B 10H10] 2 -. In the composites of the solid electrolyte with conductive carbon and as-prepared cathode active materials (= in the discharged state), NaCrO2 shows higher thermal stability up to 525 °C than NaMnO2 and NaFeO2, according to DSC-TG-MS results. Next, upon the initial charging (= desodiation) of the NaCrO2 cathode, significant cell impedance growth is observed above the onset of the more stable [B12H12] 2 -decomposition by the sequential constant-current and constant-voltage measurements and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy with a stepwise increase in the  upper  cutoff  voltage.  This  supports  the  decomposition  mechanism  of  mixed hydroborate anions proposed in Chapter 5, and rationalizes the stable 3 V battery cycling with Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) up to 3.25 V vs Na + /Na in the previous study from our group. Furthermore, the electrolyte remains intact in the cathode composite upon the internal thermal decomposition of Na0.5CrO2 (= in the charged state) at ~250 °C with a minimal heat generation, demonstrating the potential for highly safe, hydroborate-based all-solidstate batteries with a wide operating temperature range. Considering the compatibility with alkali  metal  anodes,  this  includes  the  possible  implementation  of  hydroborate  solid electrolytes  into  high-temperature  molten-salt  batteries.  Furthermore,  the  experimental compatibility  of  the closo -hydroborates  with  3 V-class  cathodes  is  revealed  to  exceed previous  thermodynamic  calculations,  surpassing  that  of  sulfide  and  selenide  solid electrolytes (Na 3 PS4 and Na3PSe4). Thus, we highlight the room to improve the validity of theoretical studies hand in hand with solid experimental evidence and understanding of hydroborates.  Therefore,  this  study  contributes  to  demonstrating  both  the  possible operational  safety  of  hydroborate-based  all-solid-state  batteries  and  the  potential  for improved computational studies on hydroborates.

̂

In this study, I performed most of the material preparation and characterization, and  all  thermal  and  electrochemical  experiments.  Dr.  Léo  Duche ne  helped  to  prepare Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) and NaCrO2. Dr. Seyedhosein Payandeh helped with synchrotron Xray diffraction measurements and the calibration of the DSC signals. Dr. Daniel Rentsch conducted solid-state NMR measurements. I designed the experiments, performed the data analysis, and wrote the manuscript with input from all co-authors.

This chapter is not published yet.

Thermal  and  electrochemical  interface  compatibility of a  hydroborate  solid  electrolyte  with  3 V-class cathodes for all-solid-state sodium batteries

Ryo Asakura, 1,2 Léo Duchêne, 1 Seyedhosein Payandeh, 1 Daniel Rentsch, 3 Hans Hagemann, 2 Corsin Battaglia 1 , and Arndt Remhof 1†

1 Laboratory Materials for Energy Conversion, Empa, Swiss Federal Laboratories for Materials Science and Technology, 8600 Dübendorf, Switzerland

2 Département de Chimie-Physique, Université de Genève, 1211 Geneva 4, Switzerland

3 Laboratory for Functional Polymers, Empa, Swiss Federal Laboratories for Materials Science and Technology, 8600 Dübendorf, Switzerland

† e-mail: arndt.remhof@empa.ch

## Keywords

all-solid-state battery, solid electrolyte, hydroborate, interface compatibility, thermal stability

## Abstract

Thermal stability of solid electrolytes and their compatibility with battery electrodes are key factors to ensure stable cycling and high operational safety of all-solid-state batteries. Here we study the compatibility of a hydroborate solid electrolyte Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) with 3 V-class cathode active materials: NaCrO2, NaMnO2, and NaFeO2. Among these layered sodium transition metal oxide cathodes, NaCrO2 shows the highest thermal compatibility in contact with the

hydroborate solid electrolyte up to 525 °C in the discharged state. Furthermore, the electrolyte remains intact upon the internal thermal decomposition of the charged, i.e. desodiated, cathode (Na0.5CrO2) above 250 °C, demonstrating the potential for highly safe hydroborate-based allsolid-state batteries with a wide operating temperature range. The experimentally determined onset temperatures of thermal decomposition of Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) in contact with 3 V-class cathodes surpass those of sulfide and selenide solid electrolytes, exceeding previous thermodynamic calculations. Our results also highlight the need to identify relevant decomposition pathways of hydroborates to enable more valid theoretical predictions.

## Introduction

All-solid-state batteries have increasingly drawn researchers' attention due to their potentially high energy and power density, and improved operational safety. 1,2 Ionic conductivity of some of the solid electrolytes (SEs) already surpasses that of flammable organic liquid electrolytes employed in state-of-the-art lithium-ion batteries, 3-5 accelerating studies on the implementation of SEs in all-solid-state batteries. In addition, the increasing demand and price for raw materials of lithium-ion batteries are motivating a shift to sodium-ion batteries as a cost-effective and sustainable alternative. 6,7 Sodium-ion batteries can also be developed into next-generation allsolid-state sodium batteries by replacing the organic liquid electrolyte with solid superionic sodium conductors. 8,9

Hydroborates are one of the most promising classes of materials among solid superionic conductors. They combine a wide electrochemical stability window, low gravimetric density (&lt;1.2 g cm -3 ), soft mechanical properties that enable cold pressing, solution processability, and low toxicity. 10-16 The superionic conduction of hydroborates was originally found in the hightemperature  phase  of  LiBH4. 17 Subsequently,  room-temperature  liquid-like  ionic  (Li + ,  Na + ) conductivity (≥1 mS cm -1 ) was discovered in hydroborate solid electrolytes with mixed anions

of more stable, cage-like closo -hydroborate [B n H n ] 2-, closo -monocarbaborate [CB n -1H n ] -, and open-cage nido -hydroborate [B n H n +3] -. 18,19,28,20-27 Closo -hydroborate SEs have been successfully  implemented in high-voltage all-solid-state sodium batteries, demonstrating stable 3 V and 4 V all-solid-state battery cycling at C/5 with a high capacity retention of 85 % after 250 cycles and 78 % after 800 cycles, respectively. 29-32 These results are achieved by the compatibility of the closo -hydroborate SEs with a sodium metal anode and a proper choice of cathode active materials based on the electrochemical oxidative stability limit of the SEs. 33,34 So far, computational and/or experimental studies on the (electro-)chemical compatibility between hydroborate SEs and cathode active materials at high potentials or at elevated temperatures are scarce. 3538 Furthermore, the thermal stability of hydroborates has been discussed mainly in the context of  applications for hydrogen storage materials. 39-44 The  combined understanding of electrochemical and thermal stability of hydroborate SEs, and the compatibility with cathode active materials are missing to ensure stable cycling and high operational safety of hydroborate-based all-solid-state batteries.

Here we present a systematic study on the electrochemical and thermal stability of a closo -hydroborate SE, Na4(B12H12)(B10H10), and its compatibility in 3 V-class cathode composites. By combining potential-dependent electrochemical characterization and temperature-dependent material characterization, we provide experimental evidence for the compatibility of the closo -hydroborate SE with layered sodium transition metal oxide cathodes NaTMO2 (TM = Cr, Mn, Fe) in both the discharged and charged states. By comparing the experimentally determined onset temperatures with the theoretically predicted chemical reactivity between SEs and cathode active materials, 38 the validity of thermodynamic calculations on hydroborates using the current databases 45-47 is also discussed.

## Experimental Section

## Material preparation

Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) was prepared by ball milling of Na2B12H12 (KatChem) and Na2B10H10 (KatChem) in a 1:1 molar ratio in argon for 3 × 15 min in a 1:10 sample-to-ball weight ratio using a Spex 8000M shaker mill, followed by heat treatment at 250 °C for 12 h under vacuum (10 -3 mbar). 20,29 Na2B12H12 was used as purchased, while Na2B10H10 was predried at 160 °C for 6 h under vacuum. For electrochemical stability measurements, Na2B12H12 was ball-milled for 3 × 15 min in a 1:30 sample-to-ball weight ratio using a Spex 8000M shaker mill, and heattreated at 300 °C for 12 h under vacuum to enhance its ionic conductivity. 23,30  Na2B10H10 was ball-milled likewise without subsequent heat treatment. Conductive carbon Super C65 (Imerys Graphite &amp; Carbon) was dried at 150 °C for 24 h under vacuum prior to use.

Three kinds of layered sodium transition metal oxides NaTMO2 (TM = Cr, Mn, Fe) were prepared  by  solid-state  synthesis. 48-50 For  NaCrO2,  Cr2O3  (&gt;99 %,  Fluka)  and  Na2CO3 (&gt;99.95 %, Alfa Aesar) were mixed for 2 × 15 min in a 1:30 sample-to-ball weight ratio using a  Spex 8000M shaker mill, and calcined for 5 h under argon at 900 °C at a heating rate of 3.3 °C min -1 . For NaMnO2, Mn2O3 (99 %, Sigma-Aldrich) and Na2CO3 (&gt;99.95 %, Alfa Aesar) were mixed for 2 × 15 min in a 1:30 sample-to-ball weight ratio using a Spex 8000M shaker mill, and calcined for 9 h in air at 700 °C at a heating rate of 10 °C min -1 . For NaFeO2, Fe3O4 (97 %, Sigma-Aldrich) and Na2O2 (97%, Sigma-Aldrich) were mixed for 15 min with a mortar and pestle and calcined for 12 h in air at 630 °C at a heating rate of 10 °C min -1 . During the cooling step immediately after calcination, NaMnO2 and NaFeO2 powders were transferred to an argon-filled glovebox (MBraun, O2 and H2O &lt;0.1 ppm) to avoid chemical reactions with moisture.

Na0.5CrO2 was obtained by chemical desodiation in an argon-filled glovebox to enable direct comparison to the previous report. 36 As-prepared NaCrO2 was dispersed in an anhydrous

acetonitrile (99.8 %, Sigma-Aldrich) solution of iodine (I2, 99.8 %, Alfa Aesar) in the weight ratio of NaCrO2 : I2 = 1 : 5, and stirred at 1200 rpm for 4 days in a sealed vial wrapped with aluminium  foil  to  inhibit  light  exposure.  The  solution  involving  the  chemical  oxidation  of NaCrO2 by iodine (see the chemical reaction below) was centrifuged, and the Na0.5CrO2 residue was washed with anhydrous acetonitrile until no iodine and iodide were visually present. The washed powder was dried at 25 °C for 18 h under vacuum.

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

For material characterization, cathode composites were prepared by mixing NaTMO2 (TM = Cr, Mn, Fe) or Na0.5CrO2, Na4(B12H12)(B10H10), and Super C65 in a 70:20:10 weight ratio for 15 min with a mortar and pestle. All chemicals were stored and handled in an argonfilled glovebox.

## Material characterization

Powder X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns were collected with Cu-K α radiation ( λ = 1.5418 Å) in transmission mode, using an Empyrean diffractometer and an X'Celerator detector (Malvern Panalytical) or using an X'Pert diffractometer and a PIXcel detector (Malvern Panalytical), respectively. A focusing mirror was employed to remove K β radiation. Temperature-dependent synchrotron XRD patterns were collected in transmission mode with a wavelength of 0.7095 Å at a heating rate of 20 °C min -1 , using a Mythen detector at the MS-X04SA beamline of the Swiss Light Source (SLS) at the Paul Scherrer Institute in Villigen, Switzerland. 51,52 Samples were filled and sealed in borosilicate or quartz glass capillaries under argon atmosphere. Phase identifications of XRD patterns were performed by the HighScore software. Rietveld refinement was  carried  out  with  the  TOPAS  software. 53,54 Combined  differential  scanning  calorimetry (DSC), thermogravimetry (TG), and mass spectrometry (MS) were conducted using punctured aluminium pans under helium flow at a heating/cooling rate of 5 °C min -1 , using an STA 449

F3 Jupiter and a QMS 403 C Aëolos (NETZSCH). Total heat generation was calculated by the integral of the exothermic DSC signals compared to the one around the onset temperature as a baseline. Magic-angle spinning (MAS) solid-state NMR measurements were performed using an Avance 400 NMR Spectrometer (Bruker) with a 4 mm cross-polarization (CP)-MAS probe. 11 B and 23 Na NMR spectra were recorded as single-pulse experiments at 128.4 MHz (typically 512 scans) applying MAS rates of 13 kHz, π/12 pulse lengths of 1.5  s with 50 kHz SPINAL64 proton decoupling during acquisition. The data were processed with a line broadening of 10 Hz, and the background resonance of the probe was subtracted from the spectra with the reference data recorded under the same measurement conditions.

## Cell assembly

SE/carbon composites (SE = Na2B12H12, Na2B10H10, Na4(B12H12)(B10H10)) were prepared for electrochemical stability measurements by mixing each SE and Super C65 with a mortar and pestle for 15 min in a 75:25 weight ratio. 34 For electrochemical measurements with cathode active materials, NaTMO2 (TM = Cr, Mn, Fe)/Na4(B12H12)(B10H10)/carbon cathode composites in a 70:20:10 total weight ratio were prepared by solution impregnation to ensure an intimate contact. 29 Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) was dissolved in anhydrous isopropanol  (99.9%, VWR) and NaTMO2 was dispersed in the solution by ultrasonication for a few min. The mixture was dried under vacuum and heat-treated at 180 °C for 4 h to obtain SE-coated NaTMO2 (NaTMO2:Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) = 70:10 in weight). SE-coated NaTMO2 was mixed with the remaining 10 wt % of Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) and 10 wt % of Super C65 for 15 min with a mortar and pestle.

For electrochemical stability measurements of SEs, 5 mg of SE/carbon composite as a working electrode and 80 mg of SE were pressed uniaxially at 150 MPa (for Na2B12H12 and Na2B10H10) or 900 MPa (for Na4(B12H12)(B10H10)) into a stacked 12 mm-diameter pellet. A platinum  disk  (99.997 %,  0.25 mm  in  thickness,  Alfa  Aesar)  and  aluminum  foil  (&gt;99.3 %,

15 μm in thickness, MTI) with 12 mm diameter were attached as current collectors to the working electrode side of the pellet (= the SE/carbon composite side). 34 A surface-scratched sodium metal foil with 10 mm diameter was rolled out from a sodium metal cube (99.9 %, SigmaAldrich), and attached on the other side of the pellet as a reference/counter electrode. For the NaTMO2|Na4(B12H12)(B10H10)|Na  all-solid-state  cells,  2 mg  of  the  cathode  composite  and 80 mg of Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) were pressed together uniaxially at 900 MPa into a pellet with 12 mm diameter and ~500 μm thickness. An aluminum foil and a sodium metal foil were attached to the side of the working and reference/counter electrode, respectively. All components were assembled in a two-electrode configuration in a Swagelok-type cell and fixed by a spring with a maximum cell pressure of 0.2 MPa.

## Electrochemical characterization

All electrochemical measurements were carried out using a VMP3 or a VSP multichannel potentiostat (BioLogic). For electrochemical oxidative stability measurements, linear sweep voltammetry was performed in Pt/SE-carbon/SE/Na cells from 2.0 V to 5.0 V vs. Na + /Na at a scan rate of 10 μV s -1 at  an  elevated temperature of 60 °C for enhanced ionic conductivity. Galvanostatic charge-discharge measurements of the Na4(B12H12)(B10H10)/carbon composite were conducted between 2.0 V and 5.0 V vs Na + /Na at a constant current density of 7.8 μA cm -2 . The specific irreversible capacity was calculated based on the weight of Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) in the Na4(B12H12)(B10H10)/carbon composite (= 0.75·5 mg = 3.75 mg).

For assessment of the NaTMO2|Na4(B12H12)(B10H10)|Na all-solid-state cells, galvanostatic charge-discharge measurements were performed at C/20 (1C = 120 mA g -1 for NaCrO2, 150 mA g -1 for NaMnO2, 100 mA g -1 for NaFeO2) between 2.00 V and 3.25 V (for Cr and Mn) or 3.40 V (for Fe) vs. Na + /Na after a 12 h rest at 60 °C. For galvanostatic and potentiostatic measurements, the upper cut-off voltage was increased stepwise by 0.1 V from 2.8 V to 3.9 V vs. Na + /Na. Upon reaching each upper cut-off voltage step with a constant current of C/20, a

constant voltage step was applied until the current decays &lt;10 % of the applied current. The constant voltage step at each upper cut-off voltage is necessary to complete electrochemical reactions prior to electrochemical impedance spectroscopy that was conducted to probe electrochemical  responses  with  different  time  constants  in  a  frequency  range  from  100 kHz  to 5 mHz with a 10 mV amplitude.

## Results and Discussion

## Electrochemical and thermal stability of hydroborate solid electrolyte

We first assess the stability of hydroborate solid electrolytes (SEs) at high potentials. Linear sweep  voltammetry  was  conducted  to  assess  the  electrochemical  oxidative  stability  of  the mixed-anion SE Na4(B12H12)(B10H10), and its constituent anions, Na2B12H12 and Na2B10H10 (see Fig. 1a and S1a). 30,34 The sensitivity to electrochemical oxidation is enhanced at a low scan rate of 10  V s -1 and by using a SE-carbon composite as a working electrode to increase the contact area between electrode and electrolyte. 30,34 By linear fitting of capacitive background and anodic  decomposition  currents,  the  onset  potential  of  electrochemical  oxidation  of  Na2B10H10 (3.15 V vs Na + /Na) is confirmed to be lower than that of Na2B12H12 (3.45 V vs Na + /Na). 30,34 This is in line with the relative stability of closo -hydroborate cluster series, exhibiting the highest stability of the icosahedral [B12H12] 2cluster among [B n H n ] 2-( n = 5-12). 44,55-57  A minor contribution to the oxidation currents was observed at ~2.9 V vs Na + /Na for all the samples, as commonly attributed to the oxidation of solvent residues contained as impurities in closo -hydroborates and their derivatives. 30,34 In a previous report, we tentatively attributed the major decomposition peak of Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) at ~3.25 V vs Na + /Na to the [B12H12] 2oxidation, 34 but in this work, this peak is shown to be derived from the [B10H10] 2oxidation. This means that the electrochemical oxidative stability of the equimolar anion mixture Na4(B12H12)(B10H10)

is  limited  by  the  two  types  of  the  component  hydroborate  anions,  especially  by  less  stable [B10H10] 2rather than [B12H12] 2-.

Fig. 1 (a) Linear sweep voltammograms of Na2B12H12 (dark green), 30 Na2B10H10 (light green), and Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) (blue) 34 at a scan rate of 10 μV s -1 at 60 °C and (b) initial galvanostatic (= constant-current) charge-discharge curves of Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) at a current density of 7.8 μA cm -2 in Pt/SE-carbon/SE/Na cells (SE:carbon = 75:25 in weight) at 60 °C. The specific capacity was calculated based on the weight of Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) in the composite. (c) DSC and MS data of Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) at a heating rate of 5 K min -1 . (d) 11 B MAS solidstate NMR spectra of Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) at 25 °C and after heating to 300 °C and 600 °C for &gt;1 h under vacuum.

In the same cell configuration as voltammetric experiments, galvanostatic cycling was performed at 60 °C for the Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) SE at a constant current density of 7.8 μA cm -2 to reach the total SE decomposition capacity obtained during the linear sweep voltammetry after around 100 h (~C/100). As shown in Fig. 1b, SE oxidation during the first charge initiates a plateau at ~3.25 V vs Na + /Na, followed by a gradual voltage increase to &gt;4 V vs Na + /Na. This

is  in  agreement  with  voltammetry  results,  supporting  the  initial  [B10H10] 2oxidation  from 3.25 V vs Na + /Na and the subsequent [B12H12] 2oxidation above 3.40 V vs Na + /Na. During the first discharge, almost no capacity is recorded due to the irreversible oxidative decomposition reactions, as exemplified in other hydroborate SEs. 30,34,58 Possible electrochemical oxidative decomposition products include dimers of closo -hydroborates with a B-H-B bridging bond. 59,60 The influence of irreversible SE decomposition on all-solid-state battery cycling with 3 V-class cathode active materials will be discussed below.

Second, the thermal stability of the Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) SE was evaluated by differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) and mass spectrometry (MS) (see Fig. 1c). An endothermic peak between 100 °C and 200 °C corresponds to the release of residual H2O evidenced by mass spectra  ( m / z =  18),  which  is  inevitable  due  to  the  hygroscopic  nature  of  vacuum-dried Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) and the short air exposure of the DSC pan just before the MS measurement. However, the sample was kept dry before the air exposure and this dehydration is known to be reversible for alkali metal closo -hydroborates. 39 Therefore, residual H2O is observed only for MS measurements of SE-containing samples, and the H2O release would not affect the thermal decomposition of Na4(B12H12)(B10H10). After the first endothermic peak, no further heat changes were detected in the DSC data up to 500 °C. The 11 B and  23 Na MAS solid-state NMR spectra and XRD patterns at 25 °C and 300 °C shown in Fig. 1d and S1b, c are identical to those of previously reported Na4(B12H12)(B10H10), 20 thereby reconfirming the structural stability of Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) at least up to 300 °C. A slight H2 release ( m / z =  2)  associated with an endothermic DSC signal was observed above 500 °C; moreover, heating to 600 °C leads to an almost complete loss of Bragg reflections in the XRD patterns. The  11 B MAS NMR spectrum of the sample heated to 600 °C shows the resonance broadening of [B12H12] 2at -16 ppm and the resonance loss of [B10H10] 2at  -2 ppm and -30 ppm. This implies dehydrogenation and amorphization of Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) upon its thermal decomposition, as reported for closo -[B12H12] 2salts. 39-41 Overall,  this  hydroborate  SE  exhibits  intrinsic  thermal  stability  up  to

500 °C, which is advantageous compared to standard organic liquid electrolytes and polymerbased electrolytes that typically decompose far below 300 °C. 61-66

## Compatibility between 3 V-class cathodes and hydroborate solid electrolyte

The thermal stability of three kinds of layered sodium transition metal oxides NaTMO2 (TM = Cr, Mn, Fe) as cathode active materials and their respective cathode composites (NaTMO2:Na4(B12H12)(B10H10):carbon = 70:20:10 in weight) were evaluated by DSC-TG-MS and by XRD at selected temperatures (see Fig. 2). The DSC data of the three kinds of NaTMO2 show nearly zero heat emission/absorption with no exothermic/endothermic events, indicating that they are stable up to 600 °C (which is expected as they were calcined at ≥630 °C). Consequently, the following analysis is focused on their respective cathode composites incorporating the Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) SE and conductive carbon (see Fig. S2 for its XRD pattern). XRD patterns shown in Fig. 2c, f, i indicate that all three kinds of the cathode composites remain unchanged at least up to 300 °C. Above 300 °C, deviations are observed with respect to the transition metal species in the layered oxides.

Fig. 2 DSC data of (a) NaCrO2, (d) NaMnO2, (g) NaFeO2 (green), and their cathode composites (NaTMO2:Na4(B12H12)(B10H10):carbon = 70:20:10 in weight) (red) recorded at heating rates of  5 °C min -1 .  MS  (left  axis)  and  TG  data  (right  axis)  of  the  cathode  composites  with  (b) NaCrO2,  (e)  NaMnO2,  and  (h)  NaFeO2.  XRD  patterns  of  the  cathode  composites  with  (c) NaCrO2, (f) NaMnO2, and (i) NaFeO2 at 25 °C and after heating at 300 °C and 600 °C for &gt;1 h under vacuum, respectively. The broad reflection at around 22 ° in some of the spectra is attributed to Super C65 carbon black.

Firstly,  the  NaCrO2  composite  exhibits  a  sharp  exothermic  peak  at  a  heat  flow  of 5.0 W g -1 with an onset temperature of 525 °C, accompanied by a pronounced H2 release and a ~1 % weight loss as a consequence of Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) decomposition (see Fig. 2a, b). Although  the  main  phase  in  the  XRD  pattern  at  600 °C  is  still  NaCrO2  (see  Fig.  2c),  the Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) reflection at 15 ° is lost and reflections attributed to e.g. Cr7C3 and NaBO2 appear, according to phase identification by HighScore. This indicates that the presence of the hydroborate SE and conductive carbon triggers the thermal decomposition reactions of NaCrO2.

Secondly, the NaMnO2 composite involves a few exothermic peaks with comparable maximum heat flow (4.9 W g -1 )  to  the  NaCrO2  composite,  but  lower  onset  temperature  of 421 °C, accompanied by corresponding H2 releases and minor weight loss starting at higher temperatures above 450 °C (see Fig. 2d, e). This can also be attributed to accelerated thermal decomposition reactions of NaMnO2 in the presence of Na4(B12H12)(B10H10). The XRD pattern at 600 °C in Fig. 2f shows that the main phase shifts from NaMnO2 to e.g. MnO and Mn7C3 with NaBO2 as a minor phase, according to phase identification by HighScore. This implies that reduction reactions are induced by the hydroborate SE and conductive carbon upon the decomposition of NaMnO2.

Finally, the NaFeO2 composite causes exothermic peaks at  an even lower heat flow (&lt;1.7 W g -1 )  and onset temperature of 393 °C with an H2 release and a gradual weight loss starting  from  higher  temperatures  over  500 °C  (see  Fig.  2g,  h).  It  is  noteworthy  that  this NaFeO2:Na4(B12H12)(B10H10):carbon composite exhibits higher thermal stability than a NaFeO2 mixture with a polytetrafluoroethylene (PTFE) binder and carbon black ( T onset ~ 340 °C) that is further destabilized in the presence of an organic liquid electrolyte. 63 Compared to the other two composites, broad exothermic peaks and corresponding H2 release were observed above the onset temperature (see Fig. 2h). In the XRD pattern at 600 °C, the main phase was identified

as metallic iron with remaining NaFeO2 and NaBO2 as minor phases (see Fig. 2i), suggesting the reduction of Fe 3+ in NaFeO2 to Fe 0 by Na4(B12H12)(B10H10).

In summary, we found that the thermal decomposition pathways induced by the hydroborate SE vary with the nature of the transition metal species and the inherent structural stability of their sodium layered oxides. 36,37,67,68 Also, the onset temperatures of thermal decomposition suggest that the layered sodium transition metal oxides NaTMO2 (TM = Cr, Mn, Fe) exhibit increasing thermal reactivity with the hydroborate SE in the order of Cr &lt; Mn &lt; Fe. This order coincides  with  that  reported  for  the  sulfide  and  selenide  SE,  Na3PS4  and  Na3PSe4. 36 Since NaCrO2 in its cathode composite appeared to be the most thermally stable among the three transition metal species, further electrochemical and thermal characterizations will focus on the NaCrO2 composite.

Fig. 3 (a) Galvanostatic and potentiostatic curves during the first charge of a NaCrO2|Na4(B12H12)(B10H10)|Na all-solid-state cell at 60 °C. The upper cut-off voltage was increased stepwise by 0.1 V from 2.8 V to 3.9 V vs. Na + /Na. For each upper cut-off voltage step, a C-rate of C/20 (1C = 120 mA g -1 ) was applied, followed by a constant voltage step until the current decayed to &lt;10% of the applied current. (b, c) Impedance spectra recorded after the constant voltage step at each upper cut-off voltage. Offsets in the y-axis were applied for improved visibility. The inset in (c) shows the impedance spectra over the full frequency range at the upper cut-off voltage above 3.7 V vs. Na + /Na.

To investigate the electrochemical compatibility of the Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) SE with the NaCrO2 cathode composite in relation to the SE oxidative stability, galvanostatic and potentiostatic charging with electrochemical impedance spectroscopy was conducted with a stepwise increase in the upper cut-off voltage from 2.8 V to 3.9 V vs. Na + /Na in NaCrO2|Na4(B12H12)(B10H10)|Na all-solid-state cells at 60 °C. As shown in Fig. 3a, only galvanostatic processes at C/20 deliver capacity at low voltages, whereas potentiostatic processes start contributing to capacity from 3.5 V vs. Na + /Na, indicating sluggish kinetics of electrochemical reactions at higher voltages. Although the electrochemical oxidation of Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) overlaps with the desodiation of NaCrO2 above 3.25 V vs Na + /Na (see Fig. 1b), the influence of the electrochemical SE decomposition can be identified from the corresponding impedance spectra in Fig. 3b, c, S3. Up to 3.2 V vs Na + /Na, the cell impedance

decreases to as low as 13 Ω due to a low interfacial impedance expressed as a semi-circle. 32 However, the interfacial impedance starts to grow significantly from 3.3 V vs Na + /Na, increasing the total cell impedance by three orders of magnitude at 3.7 V vs Na + /Na. NaCrO2 undergoes reversible electrochemical (de)sodiation up to 3.7 V vs Na + /Na (≈ Na0.4CrO2) while maintaining its layered structure with enhanced Na + diffusion in the desodiated state; 69-71 therefore, the desodiated cathode active material Na x CrO2 (0.4 &lt; x &lt; 1) would not contribute to the significant impedance growth. 32 Thus, the dramatic increase in the interfacial impedance at high voltages can be attributed to the irreversible SE decomposition and the formation of a resistive interphase.  This  behavior  was  also  observed  beyond  the  oxidative  stability  limit  of  another closo -hydroborate SE, Na4(CB11H12)2(B12H12), 30 implying  analogous  dimer  formations  from closo -hydroborate anions (initially [B10H10] 2and subsequently [B12H12] 2) upon electrochemical oxidation of Na4(B12H12)(B10H10). 59,60 This result fits well with previous reports and Fig. S4a, b, rationalizing that setting the upper cut-off voltage below the oxidative stability limit (e.g. 3.25 V vs. Na + /Na with Na4(B12H12)(B10H10)) enables stable all-solid-state battery cycling of cathode active materials with closo -hydroborate SEs. 29-32

On the other hand, when cells are cycled beyond the oxidative stability limit of SEs to take advantage of higher redox potentials of cathode active materials (e.g. 3.40 V vs. Na + /Na for NaFeO2 in Fig. S4c), cells exhibit a large irreversible capacity during the first discharge and faster capacity decay in later cycles. 29,30 The decay starting from the first cycle could be induced both by the electrochemically formed resistive interphase and by inherent structural degradation and volume changes of the cathode active materials. Depending on the combination of the oxidative stability limit of SEs and redox potentials of cathode active materials, a protection layer against electrochemical SE decomposition would therefore also be required for the cathode composite in  the  case  of  hydroborates  to  extend  their  operating  potential  ranges.  Although chemical species and inherent chemical/structural stability of cathode active materials may have

a strong influence on the overall thermal stability, the cathode protection layer could also attenuate their thermal reactivity with hydroborate SEs. 36

## Compatibility between desodiated cathode and hydroborate solid electrolyte

We have so far investigated fully sodiated NaCrO2 (in a discharged state) and its initial electrochemical  charging  step.  In  the  following,  the  thermal  stability  of  desodiated  NaCrO2  (in  a charged state) and its cathode composite is discussed. The desodiated sample was obtained by chemical desodiation 36 with its XRD pattern refined as Na0.5CrO2 (see Fig. S6a), corresponding to electrochemical desodiation up to 3.4 V vs. Na + /Na at C/50. 69  The DSC-TG-MS measurements were performed for Na0.5CrO2 and the corresponding cathode composite, as shown in Fig. 4a, b.

Fig. 4 (a) DSC  data of Na0.5CrO2 (green) and its Na0.5CrO2 cathode composite (Na0.5CrO2:Na4(B12H12)(B10H10):carbon = 70:20:10 in weight) (red) recorded at a heating rate of 5 °C min -1 . The orange circle highlights the onset of thermal reactions of Na0.5CrO2. (b) MS (left axis) and TG (right axis) data of the same composite. (c) XRD patterns of the  Na0.5CrO2 cathode composite at 25 °C and after heating at 300 °C and 600 °C for &gt;1 h under vacuum, respectively. The broad reflection at around 22 ° in some of the spectra is attributed to Super C65 carbon black. (d) Temperature-dependent synchrotron XRD patterns of the cathode composite recorded at a heating rate of 20 °C min -1 . Each symbol corresponds to the phase observed in (c).

In the Na0.5CrO2 cathode composite, an endothermic peak was observed by DSC between 100 °C and 200 °C (see Fig. 4a), accompanied by an H2O release by MS (see Fig. 4b). This is attributed to dehydration of the hydroborate SE, as discussed in Fig. 1c, which is not present in Na0.5CrO2 without the SE (see Fig. S6e, f). More importantly, the DSC signals of Na0.5CrO2  and  its  cathode  composite  both  show  exothermic  peaks  at  a  very  low  heat  flow (&lt;0.3 W g -1 ) and onset temperatures of 240-250 °C (see Fig. 4a), indicating that the initial ther-

mal deposition of Na0.5CrO2 is independent of the presence of the hydroborate SE. The corresponding TG-MS signals shown in Fig. 4b and S6e, f indicate a two-step decomposition process associated with (i) an exothermic CO2 release ( m / z = 44) between 300 °C and 500 °C and (ii) an endothermic H2O release above 500 °C with the absence of H2 signals, which also occurs irrespective of the SE. These results evidence that O2 releases take place continuously over the course of thermal decomposition of Na0.5CrO2 above 300 °C, possibly accompanying thermal reactions of the released O2 with a residual solvent for chemical desodiation of NaCrO2 and additional dehydrogenation of Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) above 500 °C in the composite. According to the XRD patterns shown in Fig. 4c, the thermal decomposition products of the Na0.5CrO2 composite are a mixture of NaCrO2 and CrO2 at 300 °C and NaCrO2 and Cr2O3 at 600 °C, respectively. The observation of thermal reactions at 300 °C and the absence of NaBO2 formation at 600 °C in the Na0.5CrO2 cathode composite propose different thermal decomposition pathways from the fully sodiated NaCrO2 composite.

To investigate the thermal decomposition mechanisms in more detail, temperature-dependent synchrotron XRD measurements were performed for the NaCrO2 composite (see Fig. S5), Na0.5CrO2 (see Fig. S6b-d), and the Na0.5CrO2 composite (see Fig. 4d, S7). For the NaCrO2 composite,  neither  an  additional  peak  evolution  nor  a  significant  intensity  decrease  below 520 °C is observed (see Fig. S5), which is in agreement with the DSC-TG-MS results shown in Fig. 2a-c. Moreover, the reflection of Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) at 7 ° remains above 500 °C in Fig. S5, indicating the high thermal stability of the SE and its compatibility with NaCrO2.

The temperature-dependent synchrotron XRD patterns of Na0.5CrO2 (see Fig. S6b-d) shows a similar thermal decomposition behavior to the electrochemically desodiated sample in liquid-electrolyte cells. 70,72,73 The thermal decomposition mechanism of Na0.5CrO2 is suggested to involve the following steps: (i) order-disorder phase transition from the monoclinic (P′3) to rhombohedral (P3) lattices below 200 °C (e.g. loss of reflections at 11 ° and 12 °), (ii) phase

segregation of P3 Na0.5CrO2 into (a) Na-rich P3 Na x CrO2 and (b) Na-free P3 CrO2δ with an O2 release above ~250 °C (e.g. appearance of a reflection at 9 °), (iii a) re-sodiation of Na-rich P3 Na x CrO2 to O3 NaCrO2 above 400-500 °C (e.g. reflection shift from 7 ° to 8 °), and (iii b) decomposition of Na-free P3 CrO2δ into Cr2O3 with repeated O2 release above 500 °C (e.g. reappearance of a reflection at 12 °). The onset of exothermic peaks at ~250 °C in DSC signals (see Fig. 4a) corresponds to the beginning of the phase segregation (ii), irrespective of whether the hydroborate SE is present or not.

The presence of the hydroborate SE in the Na0.5CrO2 composite seems to enhance the steps of phase segregation (ii) and re-sodiation (iii a), especially between 400 °C and 500 °C, resulting in a larger exothermic DSC signal than Na0.5CrO2 only (see Fig. 4a, d); however, the magnitude of the heat flow and the general decomposition pathways did not change significantly. In particular, due to the reactions (iii a) and (iii b), the same decomposition products of NaCrO2 and Cr2O3 were identified at 600 °C both in Na0.5CrO2 and its cathode composite. After the temperature-dependent synchrotron XRD measurements up to 600 °C, a color change from black to green was observed for both samples (see the images of capillaries in Fig. S6d, S7b), which also supports the identical thermal decomposition products of NaCrO2 and Cr2O3 in both cases and the possible carbon consumption in the Na0.5CrO2 composite. These results prove that Na0.5CrO2 can in principle be handled safely with modest thermal decomposition even in the composite with the hydroborate SE and conductive carbon.

Fig. 5 (a) Onset temperature of the reactions of NaCrO2, NaMnO2, NaFeO2, or Na0.5CrO2 with Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) (red), Na3PS4  (black), 36 or Na3PSe4 (grey). 36 (b) Maximum heat flow (left axis) and total heat generation (right axis) of the cathode composite with the hydroborate SE.

To  compare  the  thermal  reactivity  of  NaTMO2  (TM  =  Cr,  Mn,  Fe)  and  Na0.5CrO2  with Na4(B12H12)(B10H10), the onset temperature, maximum heat flow, and total heat generation obtained from DSC results are summarized in Fig. 5. As mentioned in the previous section, the onset temperature decreases in the order of Cr &gt; Mn &gt; Fe (and the maximum heat flow in Cr ≈ Mn &gt; Fe). The total heat generation up to 600 °C shows the reverse order of Cr &lt; Mn &lt; Fe, probably due to broad exothermic peaks starting from lower onset temperatures. These observations reflect the inherent structural stability of NaTMO2 related to the transition metal species, 67-69 which is also consistent with the reactivity reported for a sulfide and selenide SE. 36 The maximum  heat flow records the highest value (5.0 W g -1 ) with NaCrO2 and Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) at 525 °C, which is  comparable to  the  decomposition  of  Li0.47CoO2  at ~300 °C  in  oxide-based  all-solid-state  lithium  cells. 74 As  is  the  case  for  liquid-electrolyte cells, 72 Na0.5CrO2 provides the lowest maximum heat flow and total heat generation in Fig. 5b, indicating the attractive compatibility between Na x CrO2 (0.5 ≤ x ≤ 1) and the hydroborate SE. This is in stark contrast to layered lithium transition metal oxides or LiFePO4, which generate a higher heat flow in their delithiated states upon thermal decomposition with electrolytes. 72,75

Fig. 5a also illustrates the onset temperatures of thermal decomposition for various SEs in layered transition metal oxide cathode composites. We observe the following thermal stability in decreasing order: Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) &gt; Na3PS4 &gt; Na3PSe4, which correlates with their experimentally determined oxidative stability limits of 3.25 V, 2.50 V, and 2.33 V vs. Na + /Na, respectively. 36 Note that the TG profile of a mixture of NaCrO2 and Na3PS4 in the previous report exhibits a weight loss of ~1 % between 300 °C and 500 °C, 36 leaving the possibility of a lower actual onset temperature than the reported value of 500 °C for NaCrO2 and Na3PS4. The onset temperatures of Na0.5CrO2 (~3.4 V vs. Na + /Na) in composites with Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) and Na3PS4 are almost the same (240-250 °C), which is mainly due to the bulk internal segregation reaction of Na0.5CrO2, as discussed above. The less stable Na3PSe4 shows a lower onset of  220 °C  due  to  the  facilitated  oxidation  to  metallic  selenium. 36 The  thermal  stability  of Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) against the internal reaction of Na0.5CrO2 can be attributed to a low driving force of the inter-mixing reactions in the cathode composite, thanks to the high intrinsic electrochemical/thermal stability of the hydroborate SE as well as to a possible interface self-passivating functionality. 30,36 In fact, when the electrochemical oxidative stability limit of a hydroborate SE is much lower than the redox potential of a cathode active material in its charged state (e.g. ~2.0 V vs. Li + /Li for LiBH4 &amp; ~2.5 V vs. Li + /Li for TiS2), a thermal "relaxation" reaction can occur at an elevated temperature (i.e. lithiation of TiS2 at 120 °C in this example), as detected in the bulk by XRD. 34,76,77 This type of thermally activated sodiation of Na0.5CrO2 (corresponding to 3.0-3.4 V vs. Na + /Na) was not observed with Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) (stable up to ~3.25 V vs. Na + /Na) below the onset temperature of the internal segregation of Na0.5CrO2 ( T onset ~ 250 °C). This thermal compatibility, together with even higher ionic conductivity at &gt;100 °C, 78 implies the feasibility of high-temperature battery operation with e.g. molten sodium.

The relatively high compatibility of Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) with cathode active materials also questions the validity of the conclusions from previous thermodynamic calculations on

closo -hydroborates. The maximum driving forces for the reactions of NaTMO2 (TM = Cr, Mn, Fe) or Na0.5CrO2 with Na2B12H12 were calculated to be higher than those with Na3PS4 and with more reactive Na3PSe4 (except for a NaCrO2/Na3PSe4 combination). 79 This is in contradiction with the experimental results discussed above, because Na2B12H12 would exhibit higher thermal stability than Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) due to the absence of less stable [B10H10] 2. Moreover, despite the well-known higher thermodynamic stability of [B12H12] 2compared to [BH4] at high potentials or temperatures, 76,80-82 the calculated maximum driving forces with Na2B12H12 were reported to be higher than with NaBH4 for all listed cathode active materials. 79 This is thermodynamically  invalid,  considering  possible  higher  reaction  energies  with  [BH4] -than  with [B12H12] 2-. 81 Also, the calculated decomposition products for the reactions between Na2B12H12 and NaTMO2 (TM = Cr, Mn, Fe) or Na0.5CrO2 include less stable NaBH4. However, NaBH4 formation from Na2B12H12 is unlikely because the SE oxidation (e.g. [BH4] - [B12H12] 2-) typically takes place with simultaneous reduction of cathode active materials upon thermal decomposition. 36,76 The main reason for this discrepancy can be attributed to the lack of thermodynamic data of possible stable phases of decomposition products (e.g. dimers formed by oxidation  of closo -hydroborates 59,60 or nido -hydroborates 26 )  in  databases  that  are  used  for calculations. 45-47 Experimental identification of the exact electrochemical/thermal decomposition pathways and products is often challenging, especially for hydroborates due to low crystallinity and minimal change in boron oxidation states. 30 Also, further surface-sensitive characterization  remains  to  be  done  to  probe  electrochemical/thermal  reactions  at  the  interface. However, thermodynamic calculations based on solid experimental evidence and understanding of hydroborates would facilitate new discoveries in this field and allow a fair comparison with other classes of SE materials.

## Conclusion

We have elucidated the intrinsic electrochemical oxidative stability and thermal stability of Na4(B12H12)(B10H10) (&gt;3.2 V vs Na + /Na and &gt;500 °C) as a closo -hydroborate SE and its compatibility with 3 V-class cathode active materials for all-solid-state batteries. The high intrinsic stability of the hydroborate SE enables its compatibility with layered sodium transition metal oxide cathodes up to 525 °C in the discharged state, while in the charged state an internal segregation reaction occurs above ~250 °C with only minimal heat generation, irrespective of the presence of the SE. These results support the high operational reliability of hydroborate-based all-solid-state batteries with a wide operating temperature range and highlight the potential of closo -hydroborate SEs for high-temperature molten-salt battery applications. Moreover, contrary to previously reported thermodynamic calculations, the experimental compatibility between the hydroborate SE and 3 V-class cathodes is revealed to exceed that of sulfide and selenide SEs, which requires future validation of theoretical studies based on solid experimental evidence and understanding for further in-depth studies on hydroborates.

## Chapter 7 -Hydroborate-Based

## Na nido Solid Electrolyte

Nido -hydroborates  ([B n H n +3 ] -)  are  a  class  of  hydroborates  that  feature  open  nest-like structures.  Similar  to closo -monocarbaborates  ([CB n -1H n ] -),  where  one  boron  atom  is substituted  by  a  carbon  atom  in closo -hydroborates  ([B n H n ] 2 -), nido -hydroborates  are monovalent and thereby promise enhanced ionic conductivity as compared the divalent closo -hydroborates. Therefore, nido -hydroborates can also be used as a building block for mixed-anion hydroborate solid electrolytes.

To investigate this approach in this study, we synthesize a sodium salt of a nido -hydroborate  [B11H14] -from  a  cost-competitive  reagent  NaBH4  through  scalable  wetchemical  processes,  and  examine  their  capabilities  as  solid  electrolytes.  The  phase transition of the synthesized, solvent-free NaB 11 H14 occurs at around 60 °C, which is lower than  that  of  Na 2 B12 H12.  Moreover,  the  anion  mixing  of  the  Na nido -hydroborate  with Na2B12H12 records high room-temperature ionic conductivity of 4 × 10 -3  S cm -1 in the bcc anion  lattice.  In  return  to  the  lower  phase-transition  temperatures  and  higher  ionic conductivity,  however,  NaB11H14  undergoes  thermal  decomposition  at  a  low  onset temperature  at  120 °C  and  its  mixed  anion Na5(B11H14)(B12H12)2 exhibits  a  narrow electrochemical stability window of 0.15-2.60 V vs Na + /Na, due to the instability of the open-cage structure. Nevertheless, the reductive stability of the mixed anion close to 0 V vs Na + /Na enables stable galvanostatic cycling of a sodium symmetric cell for &gt;350 h at 50 μ A cm -2 and  50 μ Ah cm -2 at  60 °C  with  a  minimal  overpotential  increase.  This indicates the potential use of nido -hydroborate-based solid electrolytes on the anode side of an  all-solid-state  cell.  Additionally,  because  [B 11H14] -salts  are  one  of  the  reaction intermediates for the [CB11H12] -synthesis, they can also serve as a new building block for the cost-effective synthesis of hydroborate solid electrolytes.

In this study, I mainly contributed to the design of the electrochemical experiments such as the electrochemical stability measurements and galvanostatic cycling experiments. Dr.  Seyedhosein Payandeh prepared most of the materials and performed most of the structural characterization and electrochemical experiments. Petroula Avramidou helped with material synthesis. Dr. Daniel Rentsch conducted NMR measurements and analyzed 2D  NMR  data.  Dr.  Zbigniew Ł odziana  performed  density  functional  theory  (DFT) calculations.  Prof.  Radovan Č erný  contributed  to  the  crystal  structure  solution.  Dr. Seyedhosein Payandeh designed the experiments, performed the data analysis, and wrote the manuscript with input from all co-authors.

This chapter is published as S. Payandeh, R. Asakura, P. Avramidou, D. Rentsch, Z. Ł odziana, R. Č erný, A. Remhof, C. Battaglia, Chem. Mater. 2020 , 32 , 1101-1110.

pubs.acs.org/cm

Article

## Nido -Borate/ Closo -Borate Mixed-Anion Electrolytes for All-SolidState Batteries

SeyedHosein Payandeh, Ryo Asakura, Petroula Avramidou, Daniel Rentsch, Zbigniew Ł odziana, Radovan C ̌ erny ́ , Arndt Remhof, * and Corsin Battaglia

Cite This: Chem. Mater. 2020, 32, 1101 -1110

## ACCESS

Metrics &amp; More

Article Recommendations

ABSTRACT: Closo -borates and carbacloso -borates recently emerged as a promising class of solid electrolytes for all-solidstate batteries but are too expensive. Here, we introduce NaB 11H14 nido -borates as a new building block for solid electrolytes that can be synthesized via a cost-e /uniFB00 ective low-temperature method using cheap NaBH4 as a chemical feedstock. We employ two-dimensional (2D) nuclear magnetic resonance techniques to analyze the structure of this new building block. High ionic conductivity of up to 4 mS/cm at 20 ° C is achieved by combining nido -NaB11H14 with closo -Na2B12H12 into cubic unit cells. Importantly, Na2B12H12 can also be synthesized cost-e /uniFB00 ectively from NaBH4. Crystal structures are solved and re /uniFB01 ned by synchrotron X-ray di /uniFB00 raction and density functional theory. Cyclic voltammetry reveals electrochemical

*

Supporting Information

stability from 0.15 to 2.6 V vs Na/Na + . Galvanostatic cycling in symmetrical cells with sodium metal electrodes shows only a small overpotential increase from 2 to 2.5 mV after 400 cycles at 50 μ A/cm 2 . We also discuss how our results can be translated to render lithium solid electrolytes economically viable.

## 1. INTRODUCTION

All-solid-state batteries (ASSBs) are considered as nextgeneration battery technology promising improved safety and higher energy and power density than those of today ' s lithiumion batteries employing /uniFB02 ammable liquid electrolytes. The key component of an all-solid-state battery is a solid electrolyte, which is required to exhibit a high ionic conductivity of ∼ 1 mS/cm at room temperature, a low activation energy to enable battery operation over a large temperature range, chemical and electrochemical compatibility with the anode and cathode, high thermal stability, and appropriate mechanical properties. 1,2

Prominent candidate solid electrolytes include oxides, sul /uniFB01 des, and polymers. 3 -6 Oxide electrolytes are brittle ceramics, and therefore, it is challenging to compensate volume variations of the anode and/or cathode during cycling without crack formation. Sul /uniFB01 des are softer but su /uniFB00 er from a narrow electrochemical stability window and tend to release toxic hydrogen sul /uniFB01 de gas when exposed to humidity. 7,8 Polymer electrolytes have already been commercialized in allsolid-state batteries but are not yet compatible with 4 V class cathode materials, resulting in an energy density inferior to that of today ' s liquid-electrolyte lithium-ion batteries. 4 Recently, hydroborate electrolytes have emerged as a promising yet underexplored class of solid electrolytes exhibiting a high ionic conductivity of &gt;1 mS/cm, compatibility with lithium and

sodium metal anodes, and ductile mechanical properties that enabled integration into an all-solid-state battery, demonstrating an excellent capacity retention of 85% after 250 cycles at C/5. 9 -18

Currently, one of the major challenges toward an economically viable hydroborate all-solid-state battery technology is the cost of the building blocks for hydroborate electrolytes. High ionic conductivity is achieved by integrating di /uniFB00 erent closed-cage closo -borate or carbacloso -borate building blocks (see Figure 1a -c) into the electrolyte ' s crystal lattice. 9,12 -14 With 120 000 € /kg for closoNa2B10H10 and 254 000 € /kg for carbacloso -NaCB11H12 and similar prices for their lithium analogues, 19 these salts are too expensive for battery applications. Even though prices may drop with new synthesis routes, 20 alternatives are sought.

Recently NaB11H14 was investigated as a solid electrolyte. 21 Here, we explore the synthesis, structure, and electrochemical properties of this material in more detail; moreover, we introduce open-cage nido -borates (see Figure 1d) as a building block for solid electrolytes. Nido -NaB11H14 is synthesized from

Figure 1. Atomic structures of (a) B12H12 2 -, (b) CB11H12 -, (c) B10H10 2 -, and (d) B11H14 -polyanions. Boron is denoted by green and blue, carbon by brown, and hydrogen by white balls. (e) 11 B{ 1 H} NMR spectra of the synthesized samples in DMSOd 6 solutions.

NaBH4 ( ∼ 20 $/kg 22 ) using a cost-e /uniFB00 ective low-temperature method. In particular, the synthesis avoids the use of expensive, toxic, and explosive decaborane (B 10 H14) typically employed in the synthesis of closoNa2B10H10 and carbacloso -borates. 19,23,24 Combining monovalent nido -B11H14 -anions and divalent closoB12H12 2 -building blocks into a cubic lattice, we show that a high sodium-ion conductivity of up to 4 mS/cm 2 can be achieved at room temperature. As closoNa2B12H12 can also be synthesized cost-e /uniFB00 ectively from NaBH4, 25,26 our results show that hydroborate electrolytes can be made economically viable for all-solid-state batteries.

## 2. EXPERIMENTAL SECTION

NaB11H14 can be synthesized by reacting NaBH4 and B10H14 in dioxane (C4H8O2), resulting in the formation of NaB11H14(C4H8O2) n as a solid product (method 1, 27 eq 1, see the Supporting Information (SI) for details).

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

NaB11H14 can be extracted by dissolving the product in water and removing the coordinated solvents by heat treatment. 27 To avoid the use of B10 H14, an alternative synthetic approach was employed reacting NaBH4 and 1-promopentane (C5H11Br) in diglyme (method 2, eq 2, Table 1, see the SI for details). 28

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

Both reactants and the solvent are cheap and readily available, allowing cost-e /uniFB00 ective upscaling of the synthesis. Diglyme strongly coordinates with the Na ion and makes the precipitation of the NaB11H14.(diglyme) n complex impossible without decomposition. To overcome this challenge, sodium ions are substituted with Me3NH + , allowing the precipitation of Me3NHB11H14 and removing the coordinating diglyme (Figure S1, eq 3).

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

Finally, the reaction of Me3NHB11H14 with NaOH or NaHCO3 in water or NaH in THF leads to the isolation of NaB11H14 (Figure S2, eq 4).

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

The purity of NaB11H14 and the molecular structure of the B 11H14 -anion were analyzed by nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR). To reach an ionic conductivity of &gt;1 mS/cm, nidoNaB11H14 was mixed with closoNa2B12H12 in a ball mill at molar ratios of 2:1, 1:1, and 1:2 (Table 1). The crystal structures of the resulting materials were analyzed by synchrotron powder X-ray di /uniFB00 raction (PXRD) 29,30 and solved ab initio with direct space modeling using global optimization methods implemented into Fox. 30 Crystal structures were further optimized by density functional theory as implemented in the Vienna ab initio simulation package (VASP). Phase transitions and decomposition reactions were studied by combined thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) and di /uniFB00 erential scanning calorimetry (DSC). Ionic conductivity and electrochemical stability window were

## 3. RESULTS AND DISCUSSION

The purity of NaB11H14 was veri /uniFB01 ed by 11 B solution-state NMR in DMSOd 6 shown in Figure 1e. The reaction of NaBH4 with B10H14 (method 1) often results in Na2B12H12 impurities being present in the reaction products, while the reaction scheme with C5H11Br (method 2) results in NaB11H14 with a high purity with no indication of Na2B12H12 impurity. In this method, excess use of Me 3 NHB11H14 is crucial for cationic exchange to avoid oxidation of the B 11H14 -cage in eq 4. For this purpose, using NaH in THF is more promising than the aqueous solution since NaH is not soluble and cannot oxidize the cage in the presence of excess Me3NHB11H14 (Figure S2).

The resonance at δ 11 B of -14.2 ppm with the lowest relative signal intensity is assigned to boron 1 at the bottom of the cage, and in the 11 B -11 B-correlated NMR spectrum (Figure 2a), the cross signal at -14.2/ -16.8 ppm leads to the assignment of the boron 2 sites in the middle of the B 11H14 -cage and subsequently site 3 is assigned via the cross signal at δ 11 B -16.0/ -16.8 ppm. From the correlations observed in the 1 H -11 B HSQC spectrum (Figure 2b), the four types of hydrogens in NaB11H14 can be assigned as follows: the single boron at site 1 ( δ 11 B = -14.2 ppm) shows correlation to a proton at site A at 1.8 ppm, the /uniFB01 ve protons (B) at 1.2 ppm correlate to the boron atoms at site 2 in the middle of the cage and /uniFB01 nally two di /uniFB00 erent hydrogen species with δ 1 H at 1.6 and

To con /uniFB01 rm the atomic arrangement of the B11H14 -anion building block, we performed 1 H -1 H-, 1 H -11 B-, and 11 B -11 Bcorrelated 2D NMR of NaB11H14 in DMSOd 6 solution (Figure 2a -c). The data allow assigning chemical shifts to the individual boron and hydrogen atoms in the cage by spin -spin couplings between adjacent nuclei and disclose atomic connectivity in the polyhedral framework.

-3.6 ppm are assigned to sites C (5 protons) and D (3 protons) at the top of the cage, respectively. All hydrogens show a 3 J coupling with their adjacent neighbors (such as A ↔ B, B ↔ C, B ↔ D, C ↔ D black arrows), although the B ↔ D cross peaks might also originate from a 2 J coupling. Note that the homonuclear correlations (Figure 2a,c) were both recorded with simultaneous decoupling of 1 H or 11 B during the whole pulse sequences, respectively, since due to the relatively large 1 J( 1 H, 11 B) coupling in the order of 130 Hz the polarizations were not properly transferred without decoupling.

The three hydrogen atoms at site D appear at a very low frequency and the observation of only one resonance suggests only one type of hydrogen for this position. However, two di /uniFB00 erent bonding con /uniFB01 gurations are described in the literature for hydrogen atoms on the open side of the cage such as three bridging hydrogen atoms (B -H -B) or two bridging atoms (B -H -B), and one endohydrogen (B -H) atoms. 31 Analysis of the structure and the charge density distribution on the B11H14 -anion by density functional theory in vacuum con /uniFB01 rms that the con /uniFB01 guration with two bridging H atoms and one endo-H atom is by 30 meV more stable than the con /uniFB01 guration with three bridging H atoms. Moreover, calculations of the crystalline state stability did not yield a structure with three bridging H atoms. Therefore, we applied the con /uniFB01 guration with two bridging H atoms and one endo-H for solving the crystal structures. This con /uniFB01 guration was also employed previously for the determination of the crystal structure of [Ph3PH][B11H14]. 32

To verify that B11H14 -and B12H12 2 -remain intact during ball milling, we employed solid-state 1 H, 11 B, and 23 Na MAS NMR, shown in Figure 2d -f, and solution-state 11 B{ 1 H} NMR (Figure S3, DMSO solutions). The spectra from mixed-anion compounds Na x +2 y (B11H14) x (B12H12) y with x : y anion ratios of 2:1, 1:1, and 1:2 are shown along with the spectra from the NaB11H14 and Na2B12H12 precursors. The resonances of the parent compounds can be observed in the 1 H and 11 B NMR

spectra of the mixtures at the same chemical shifts, con /uniFB01 rming that both B11H14 -and B12H12 2 -anions are still intact in the mixtures (Figures 2d,e and S3). In comparison to the spectrum of the Na2B12H12 precursor, the corresponding MAS NMR resonances in samples containing NaB11H14 are much narrower, indicative of fast anion dynamics (Figure 2d,e). The 23 Na NMR spectrum of Na2B12H12 (Figure S4a) shows a manifold of spinning side bands typical for solids, whereas for the mixed samples, only the central transitions are observed. The central transition of Na2B12H12 shows a line shape typical for quadrupolar interactions (experimental and simulated shapes shown in Figure S4b). Two signals are observed for the 23 Na MAS NMR spectrum of NaB11H14, and since no side bands are observed for this spectrum, the two resonances are assigned to di /uniFB00 erent phases of NaB 11H14 present in this sample (Figure 2f). The 23 Na NMR spectra of all mixed compounds show almost identical chemical shifts and line width, suggesting that the sodium environment in the mixtures is averaged between Na2B12H12 and NaB11H14. This averaging has already been observed in the previous work. 13

We now analyze the crystal structures of NaB 11H14 and the mixed-anion compounds Na x +2 y (B11H14) x (B12H12) y with x : y ratios of 2:1, 1:1, and 1:2. PXRD data are shown for all four compounds in Figure 3a. Analysis of the PXRD data of NaB11H14 as a function of temperature (shown in Figure 4b) reveals the presence of two polymorphs, tentatively labeled as α and β , which undergo a phase transition to a common hightemperature γ phase. The α phase is the dominant phase at room temperature and its structure was solved in an orthorhombic unit cell with space group Pna 21 (no. 33) with

Figure 3. (a) PXRD of NaB11H14 and Na x +2 y (B11H14) x (B12H12) y . ● , ⧫ , and Δ symbols mark the Bragg re /uniFB02 ections of the room-temperature α and β phases and the high-temperature γ phase of NaB11H14, respectively ( λ Cu = 1.5406 Å). (b -d) Crystal structures of NaB11H14 and Na x +2 y (B11H14) x (B12H12) y compounds. In NaB11H14 at room temperature, sodium atoms are located in octahedral and tetrahedral positions represented by red and yellow balls, respectively. The location of sodium atoms in the ordered phase at -110 ° C is represented by purple balls.

hexagonal-closed packing (hcp) of B 11H14 -anions (Figure 3b). Interestingly, a satisfactory /uniFB01 t of the powder re /uniFB02 ections was obtained only after taking into account dynamic sodium disorder by introducing three types of Na atoms in the structure and setting their partial occupancy both in octahedral and tetrahedral sites (Figures 3b, S5, and S6). Two of the three Na atoms are located in tetrahedral sites (T), one close to the center and another one close to the triangular face, and comprise ∼ 90% of the Na atoms in the structure (yellow Na atoms, Figure 3b). However, the structural model requires a small occupancy of sodium atoms in octahedral sites (O, ∼ 10%, red Na atoms, Figure 3b). These sites are both faceand edge-shared with T sites, suggesting the T → O → T di /uniFB00 usion path for Na atoms (Figure 3b).

Density functional theory optimization of the structure indicates that Na cations are located in very shallow energy wells, and displacements of 0.3 Å in each crystallographic direction are related to an energy increase of ∼ 5 meV/formula unit and explain the somewhat larger vibrational ellipsoids for sodium. In all calculated structures, the shortest Na -H distance is greater than 2.25 Å.

In the optimized structure, the B11H14 -anion is oriented with the open side of the B 11 cage away from the Na atoms. Analysis of the charge density of the anion by electron localization functions shows that the charge distribution at the open apex is /uniFB02 atter and larger charge gradients are present on the opposite or side vertexes. This results in a strong preference for Na to be located outside of the open vertex (Figure S7). The energy di /uniFB00 erence is as large as 145 meV in favor of Na + located at the vertex opposite to the open side, while Na + directly at the open side vertex is highly unstable (&gt;800 meV). The charge density distribution was further analyzed via the Bader method; see Figure S8. In fact, this distribution strongly depends on the mutual cation -anion position, with a large split of the charge for Na near the apex opposite to the open side of the B 11 cage.

To decrease the dynamic sodium disorder in the structure, additional data were recorded at -110 ° C. Changes in the relative intensity of the (011), (020), (111), and (200) Bragg re /uniFB02 ections (Figure S9a) suggest the ordering of the Na cations at this temperature. Similar behavior was previously observed for Na4(B10H10)(B12H12). 33 Re /uniFB01 ning the di /uniFB00 raction pattern at -110 ° C shows that Na atoms are ordered and occupy only the tetrahedral site (purple Na atoms in Figure 3b; Figure S10). This allows de /uniFB01 ning the structure based on one type of Na (purple atoms in the tetrahedral site) with wurtzite as aristotype. Unit cell parameters and atomic positions are summarized in Table 2.

Indexing of the β phase was hampered by the low intensity of Bragg re /uniFB02 ections and peak overlapping with other phases. Based on combined PXRD, NMR, and IR measurements, we conclude that this polymorph is metastable and observed when NaB11H14 is heat-treated at 100 ° C for 2 h. β -NaB11H14 disappears after longer heat treatment and only α and γ phases are observed in the PXRD pattern of the sample annealed for 16 h at 100 ° C (Figure S14a). β -NaB11H14 was also not observed in the previous work where samples were annealed for 16 h at 100 ° C. 21 For the high-temperature γ phase, a cubic unit cell with space group I 4 ̅ 3 d was suggested in previous studies. 21 However, this space group and unit cell did not /uniFB01 t our data and further attempts to /uniFB01 nd a unit cell failed.

PXRD data of the mixed-anion Na x +2 y (B11H14) x (B12H12) y compounds with x : y ratios of 2:1, 1:1, and 1:2 are shown in
