Figure 3a. For Na4(B11H14)2(B12H12) and Na3(B11H14)(B12 H12), a phase with I -centered cubic cell forms (Figure 3c). For Na5(B11H14)(B12H12)2, a primitive cubic cell (Figure 3d) is identi /uniFB01 ed due to the extra Bragg re /uniFB02 ection at 2 θ = 25 ° (indicated by the arrow in Figure 3a), which is systematically extinct in the I -cell.

Because B11H14 -is monovalent and B12H12 2 -is divalent, the sodium content in the structure increases with y to maintain charge balance. Taking into account that the two hightemperature phases of Na2B12H12 consist of a bcc arrangement of orientationally disordered B12H12 2 -anions with primitive and I -centered space groups ( Pm 3 ̅ n and Im 3 ̅ m ), 34 these structural models were used to solve the structures of mixedanion Na x +2 y (B11H14) x (B12H12) y compounds (Figures S11 -S 1 3 ) . 3 4 The r e s u l t i n g I m 3 ̅ m s t r u c t u r e f o r Na4(B11H14)2(B12H12) and Na3(B11H14)(B12H12) is fully disordered (Figure 3c). For Na5(B11H14)(B12H12)2, the Na content in the structure increases and a Pm 3 ̅ n structure with a partial disorder and occupancy is stabilized (Figure 3d). The reduced disorder in this structure is expected due to the higher Na content. The Pm 3 ̅ n structure was already observed as an intermediate phase for Na2B12H12, where the increase in temperature increases the disorder and Im 3 ̅ m stabilizes as the /uniFB01 nal high-temperature phase. 34 The same behavior is observed for Na5 (B11 H14)(B12H12)2, which undergoes a phase transition from Pm 3 ̅ n to Im 3 ̅ m phase at T = 254 ° C by increasing the dynamic disorder in the structure (Figure S16a,b). Similar phases were also observed in the mixed-anion Na5(CB11H12)(B12H12)2 as a function of temperature. 14

In addition to the observed phases for NaB 11H14 and mixedanion Na x +2 y (B11H14) x (B12H12) y compounds in this study, an fcc phase was observed in ref 21 for one of the commercial product batches. The formation of a cubic fcc phase could be related to the presence of impurities; nevertheless, our observation of bcc structures for mixed Na2B12H12/NaB11H14 phases suggests that the fcc phase reported in ref 21 has a di /uniFB00 erent origin of impurity.

Unit cell parameters determined by Rietveld re /uniFB01 nements are summarized in Table 2, and the atomic positions in the new structures are presented in Tables S1 -S5.

To investigate the phase transitions in these compounds, we performed DSC and temperature-dependent PXRD experiments (Figure 4). The DSC data of NaB11H14 reveal two endothermic events with onset temperatures of ∼ 60 and 80 ° C, which correspond to the /uniFB01 rst-order polymorphic transitions of α - and β -NaB11H14 to γ -NaB11H11, respectively (Figure 4a). Cooling the sample back to 20 ° C shows an exothermic event in the range of 50 -30 ° C associated with the formation of only α phase. This corresponds to the reappearance of the Bragg re /uniFB02 ections of α -NaB11H14 in the temperature-dependent PXRD data (Figure 4b) and indicates that the β phase is metastable. The metastable character of the β phase also explains the fading of the endothermic peak at 80 ° C, apparent from the second heating cycle also shown in Figure 4a, which corresponds to the phase transition of β to γ phase. As discussed above, annealing the sample at 100 ° C for a longer time (16 h instead of 2 h) vanished the β phase and no endothermic peak was observed at ∼ 80 ° C (Figure S15b). This is also in agreement with the previous study on NaB 11 H14, where long heat treatment was performed (16 h at 100 ° C) and only the phase transition from α to γ was observed. Additional infrared spectroscopy and di /uniFB00 erential scanning calorimetry measurements coupled with mass spectrometry con /uniFB01 rmed that the β phase has the same stoichiometry (NaB11H14) and is not a solvent phase (Figure S15a,b).

The observed phase-transition temperatures in NaB 11H14 are lower than the ones observed for Na2B10H10 and NaCB11H12 (transitions at 100 and 107 ° C, respectively) and are comparable to the phase-transition temperature of NaCB9H10 at ∼ 50 ° C. 11,35,36

Figure 4a also shows DSC measurements of the mixed-anion Na x +2 y (B11H14) x (B12H12) y . Interestingly, the phase transitions observed for NaB11H14 are fully suppressed. Suppression of the phase transition upon anion mixing was already observed for other mixed-anion compounds. 9,12 -14,33,37 Further heating of the mixed-anion compounds Na4(B11H14)2(B12H12) and Na3(B11H14)(B12H12) beyond 200 ° C leads to decomposition without any phase transition (as monitored by PXRD shown in Figures S17 and S18). In contrast, heating sample Na5(B11H14)(B12H12)2 increases the disorder in the structure and a phase transition from Pm 3 ̅ n to Im 3 ̅ n phase is observed at 254 ° C, as already discussed above (Figure S16). 14,34 The formation of α -Na2B12H12 ( P 21/n) 38 at the decomposition temperature for the mixed-anion compounds (Figures S16 -S18) indicates that Na x + y (B11H14) x (B12H12) y segregates into its parent compounds with an immediate decomposition of NaB11H14.

The TGA measurements of NaB11H14 reveal a ∼ 2% weight loss at ∼ 190 ° C (Figure S19). However, the heat treatment of NaB11H14 for 2 h at di /uniFB00 erent temperatures shows that NaB11H14 decomposition and formation of Na2B12H12 and other B phase containing species already start at 120 ° C (Figure S20b). Interestingly, the decomposition products seem

Figure 4. (a) Di /uniFB00 erential scanning calorimetry ( Δ T / Δ t = 5 ° C/min). (b) Temperature-dependent synchrotron radiation powder X-ray di /uniFB00 raction data of NaB11H14 synthesized by method 2. ( Δ T / Δ t = 5 ° C/min, λ = 0.7095 Å). Symbols: ● α -NaB11H14, ⧫ β -NaB11H14, and Δ γ -NaB11H14.

to stabilize the γ phase of NaB11H14 at room temperature (Figure S20a).

The temperature dependence of sodium-ion conductivity of NaB11H14 and Na x +2 y (B11H14) x (B12H12) y mixtures determined by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy is presented in Figure 5. The relative densities of the pellets and particle morphologies of the samples are provided in Table S6 and Figure S21, respectively. Pure NaB 11H14 shows a conductivity of 8 × 10 -5 S/cm at room temperature, which is higher than ∼ 2 × 10 -6 S/cm observed for NaB11H14 in the previous study. 21 We attribute this to the higher γ -phase content in this sample. The apparent activation energy is 0.75 eV at room temperature but reduces to 0.27 eV at ∼ 70 ° C due to the

Figure 5. Arrhenius plot of the sodium-ion conductivity of NaB 11H14 and Na x +2 y (B11H14) x (B12H12) y with x : y ratios of 2:1, 1:1, and 1:2.

phase transition from α and β to the γ phase. The commercial NaB11H14 with an even higher amount of γ phase (Figure S2) shows a conductivity of 3.8 × 10 -4 S/cm at room temperature. In this sample, the activation energy is 0.53 eV at room temperature and changes to 0.2 eV at temperatures above the phase transition.

I n t e r e s t i n g l y , a l l t h r e e mi x e d - a n i o n Na x +2 y (B11H14) x (B12H12) y compounds show a signi /uniFB01 cantly higher ionic conductivity than that of NaB11H14, reaching values of 3 -4 mS/cm at room temperature. Although no phase transition occurs in these samples, the activation energy changes from 0.5 eV in low temperatures to 0.3 eV at ∼ 40 ° C. Similar non-Arrhenius behavior has been observed previously for Na4(B10H10)(B12H12) and Na2(CB11H12)(CB9H10) 9,13 and is explained by the transition from a regime with correlated cationic motion at low temperatures to a regime of noncorrelated cationic di /uniFB00 usion at elevated temperatures. 33 At low temperatures, the highest conductivity is observed for Na4(B11H14)2(B12H12), which contains the highest B11H14 -content and consequently the lowest Na + content in the structure. Similar results are also observed for the Na2 -x (CB11H12) x (B12H12)1 -x mixture, for which the highest conductivity is also observed for the lowest Na + content. 14 At high temperatures, the conductivity values of the three compositions converge, possibly due to the fast dynamics of the hydroborate cages, which compensates the Na content in the structure.

The room-temperature conductivity values of 3 -4 mS/cm determined for these mixed-anion Na x +2 y (B11H14) x (B12H12) y compounds is lower than ∼ 70 mS/cm observed for Na2(CB9H10)(CB11H12). 9 However, NaCB9H10 is expensive and challenging to synthesize, which hinder the practical application of t h i s material. 1 9 , 2 3 Conversely, Na x +2 y (B11H14) x (B12H12) y can be synthesized cost-e /uniFB00 ectively with conductivities comparable to those of the best sodium-ion conductors known so far including Naβ ″ -alumina ce-

ramics, 39,40 NASICON-type electrolytes, 41 Na11Sn2PS12, 42 Na3PSe4, 43 Na3BH4B12H12, 37 Na4(B10H10)(B12H12), 13 and Na2 -x (CB11H12) x (B12H12)1 -x 14 with conductivities in the range of 0.5 -3 mS/cm at room temperature.

The electrochemical stability of the mixed-anion Na x +2 y (B11H14) x (B12H12) y electrolytes was assessed for Na5(B11H14)(B12H12)2 by means of linear sweep voltammetry, as shown in Figure 6 (results for NaB11H14 are provided in

Figure 6. Linear sweep voltammetry of (a) Na/Na5(B11H14)(B12H12)2/Al cell from 1.3 to -0.1 V vs Na/Na + and (b) Na/ Na5(B11H14)(B12H12)2/Na5(B11H14)(B12H12)2+C/Pt cell from 2.0 to 4.0 V vs Na/Na + with a scan rate of 0.05 mV/s at 60 ° C. The linear /uniFB01 tted lines are shown by black dashed lines. (c) Galvanostatic cycling of a symmetric Na/Na5(B11H14)(B12H12)2/Na cell at 60 ° C with current densities of 25 μ A/cm 2 for the /uniFB01 rst 24 h and 50 μ A/cm 2 for the rest of measurement (1 h at each direction).

Figure S22). To determine the reductive stability limit, an Al working electrode was swept versus a Na metal electrode, acting as a counter and a reference electrode, starting from the open-circuit voltage of ∼ 1.3 to -0.1 V vs Na/Na + . The Al electrode was chosen as it does not alloy with sodium, so the measured current can be attributed to the reduction of Na5(B11H14)(B12H12)2. Figures 6a and S22a display a clear current onset at ∼ 0.15 V, which we attribute to the electrolytic reduction. The small current at higher voltages is attributed to capacitive current contributions. The decomposition currents remain in the nA range until Na plating occurs at ∼ 0 V. As we show below, these currents do not hinder stable stripping/ plating for at least 520 h, as shown in Figures 6c and S24.

The oxidative stability limit of Na5(B11H14)(B12H12)2 was determined following the methodology described in ref 44, by sweeping the Pt/C working electrode against a Na metal counter electrode from 2.0 to 4.0 V. Both samples show a clear current onset at ∼ 2.6 V attributed to electrolyte oxidation (Figures 6b and S22b). Current densities are higher by a factor of 100 compared to those displayed in Figures 6a and S17a, due to the use of high-surface-area carbon to increase the electrode/electrolyte interface and enhance the sensitivity to oxidation currents. As carbon gets sodiated at low potentials, this approach was not employed for the determination of the reductive stability. Analyzing the current density during subsequent cycles shows that the onset of oxidation currents shifts to ≥ 3.5 V, implying the formation of a passivating or blocking layer after /uniFB01 rst oxidation (Figure 6b, dashed line, and Figure S23).

The lower stability of open-cage nido -borates compared to closed -cage closo -borates is attributed to the delocalized hydrogen atoms on the open side of the cage. Upon oxidation, these hydrogen atoms are more prone to form H2 and oxidize the cage further to divalent anions such as B 11H13 2 -or dimers. The formation of the B20H18 2 -dimer was observed after the oxidation of B10H10 2 -. 45,46 Therefore, the possibility of extending the electrochemical stability beyond 2.6 V depends on the decomposition products formed at the interphase and should be investigated in more detail in the future.

Galvanostatic measurements were performed to investigate the long-term stability of electrolyte versus Na metal. Sodium metal plating and stripping from Na5(B11H14)(B12H12)2 were studied by galvanostatic cycling of symmetric Na/ Na5(B11H14)(B12H12)2/Na cell. Figure 6c reveals the excellent cycling stability for 400 h at a current density of 50 μ A/cm 2 , switching the current direction every 1 h. Careful inspection of Figure 6c reveals nevertheless a small increase of the overpotential from 2 to 2.5 mV after 400 h, which we attribute to slow electrolyte reduction at the Na/Na5(B11H14)(B12H12)2 interface, consistent with the results in Figure 6a. Additionally, continuing the sodium stripping and plating at room temperature showed a stable over potentials up to 520 h (Figure S24).

## 4. CONCLUSIONS

In summary, we investigated nido -borates as a cost-e /uniFB00 ective building block for solid electrolytes for all-solid-state batteries. Electrolytes combining nido -borate with closo -borate anions achieve a high sodium-ion conductivity of 3 -4 mS/cm. This value is higher than that for electrolytes based purely on doubly charged closo -borate anions but comparable to that of electrolytes combining closo -borates with singly charged carbacloso -borates. The electrochemical stability window of opencage nido -borates is narrower than that for closed-cage closo -borates due to the delocalized hydrogen atoms on the open side of the cage and ranges from 0.15 to 2.6 V vs Na/Na + . A protective interlayer may be necessary to stabilize the interface between nido -borate-based electrolytes and a sodium metal anode. 47,48 Nevertheless, we were able to demonstrate plating and stripping of sodium metal over an extended period of time with only a small increase in the overpotential. Analogously protective coatings may enable stable cycling of cathode materials beyond 3 V vs Na/Na + , 49 while cathode materials

such as sulfur with a theoretical capacity of 1675 mAh/g lie within the stability window. 50

Carbacloso -borates, which o /uniFB00 er a wider electrochemical stability window, 14 can be synthesized from nido -borate precursors by closing the cage with a carbon atom. 51,52 Other closo -borates such as SnB11H11 2 -and PB11H11 -were also synthesized based on the reaction of nido -B11H14 -with SnCl2 and PCl3. 53,54 Therefore, we believe that the availability of a cost-e /uniFB00 ective synthesis route for nido -borates represents a promising strategy to develop next-generation hydroborate electrolytes, o /uniFB00 ering simultaneously high ionic conductivity and an extended electrochemical stability window. Methods discussed and demonstrated here for sodium are also applicable to the corresponding lithium analogues, either by direct synthesis or by cation exchange. Cycling of a lithium -sulfur battery based on a mixed-anion lithium carbacloso -borate electrolyte was recently demonstrated, demonstrating the technological potential of this class of electrolytes when the cost is no longer an obstacle. 12

## SUPPORTING INFORMATION

## Nido -Borate/ Closo -Borate Mixed-Anion Electrolytes for All-Solid-State Batteries

SeyedHosein Payandeh a , Ryo Asakura a , Petroula Avramidou a , Daniel Rentsch a , Zbigniew Łodziana b , Radovan Černý c , Arndt Remhof a , Corsin Battaglia a a Empa, Swiss Federal Laboratories for Materials Science and Technology, 8600 Dübendorf, Switzerland.

b Polish Academy of Sciences, Institute of Nuclear Physics, ul, Radzikowskiego 152, 31-342, Kraków, Poland.

c Laboratory of Crystallography, Department of Quantum Matter Physics, University of Geneva, Quai Ernest-Ansermet 24, 1211, Geneva, Switzerland

## Corresponding Author

*Email: arndt.remhof@empa.ch

## Chemicals

Sodium borohydride (NaBH4, 99.0%), 1-bromopentane (C5H11Br, 98.0%), diglyme (C6H14O3, anhydrous 99.5%), 1,4-dioxane (C4H8O2, anhydrous 99.9%), sodium (99.9%), indium foil with thickness of 0.1 mm (≥ 99.995%), NaH (90%) and tetrahydrofuran anhydrous (99.9%) were purchased  from  Sigma-Aldrich.  Decaborane  (B10H14  98%)  was  obtained  from  Alfa  Aesar Chemicals. Dimethyl sulfoxide-d6 was bought from ARMAR Chemicals and C65 from Imerys Graphite &amp; Carbon Company. For comparison, NaB11H14 (95%) and Na2B12H12 (98%) were also purchased from Katchem. Preparation and manipulation of the chemicals and samples were performed in an argon-filled glovebox ( p (H2O and O2) &lt; 1 ppm).

## Sample preparation

NaB11H14  was  synthesized  using  two  solvent-based  methods  as  described  below.  The  first method is selected based on a known synthetic path between NaBH4 and B10H14. Although this process contains B10H14 as an unfavorable reactant, it was carried out to compare the products obtained with other synthesis methods. 1 NaBH4 and B10H14 were mixed in the molar ratio of 1:1 and 30 ml of 1,4-dioxane was added to  1 g of the mixture. All reactions described below were carried out under argon flow. The suspension was stirred overnight at 90  C and the dioxane complex of NaB11H14 precipitated as a yellow powder. Dioxane free samples were obtained by dissolving the powder in Milli-Q water and drying it first in rotary evaporator and then under vacuum at T  100 °C for 2 h.

Alternatively, NaBH4 was reacted with 1-bromopentane (C5H11Br) in diglyme. 2 5 g of NaBH4 (132 mmol) was transferred to a reaction flask with a ceramic filter and the vessel was sealed in the glovebox. Then 100 ml of anhydrous diglyme was added under Argon flow outside the glovebox and suspension was stirred at 105  C for 30 minutes (in a reflux setup). Subsequently, 22-24 ml (177 - 194 mmol) of C5H11Br, (47-61% mol excess, equation 2) were added slowly with an injection speed of 10 ml/h using a syringe pump. The excess of C5H11Br was used to completely consume NaBH4. When the injection was completed, a yellow powder was formed,

the temperature was increased to 110  C and the suspension was stirred additionally for 1 h. After cooling the suspension to RT and filtration of undissolved NaBr as by-product, the solution of NaB11H14 in digylme was collected and most of the solvent was removed using a rotary evaporator at 80 °C forming a viscous oily product.

The oily product was dissolved in 100 ml of the basic water (NaOH, pH  8), heated to 60  C and the undissolved powder was filtered yielding a clear solution of NaB11H14 in water. After cooling to RT, a solution of trimethylammonium chloride ((CH3)3NHCl, 4.3 g, 45 mmol, 270 % excess, in 20 ml Milli-Q water, equation 3) was added slowly which immediately precipitates Me3NHB11H14. The precipitate was filtered and recrystallized in water to yield a white powder which then was dried under vacuum at T = 100  C, for 2 h.

Finally, excess of Me3NHB11H14 was treated with NaOH or NaHCO3 in Milli-Q water or NaH in THF at 40  C to form NaB11H14. After the reaction in water, unreacted Me3NHB11H14 (insoluble) was filtered and the solution of NaB11H14 was dried first in a rotary evaporator and second under schlenk line at 80-100  C for 2 h. In the case of using THF, first, remaining unreacted NaH (insoluble) was filtered then the THF solvent was removed by rotary evaporator. In the last step, the powder was dissolved in water and the same procedure was followed as above to remove the unreacted Me3NHB11H14.

Commercial NaB11H14 was heat-treated at T =  100  C for 2 h under vacuum using Schlenk technique to remove traces of solvents prior to further investigations.

Powder mixtures of NaB11H14 (x) and Na2B12H12 (y) were ball milled (BM) in three molar ratio of 2:1, 1:1 and 1:2 (Nax+2y(B11H14)x(B12H12)y) for 15 minutes in 5 repetitions using stainless steel vials and 5 mm stainless steel balls with balls to sample ratio of 6:1. All of the samples are listed in Tab. 1 (the main part of the manuscript).

## Powder X-ray diffraction

Laboratory powder X-ray diffraction (PXRD) data were collected on PANalytical X'Pert powder diffractometer with a PIXcel1D detector. Measurements were performed in the 2θ range of 5-90° using transmission geometry and with a focusing mirror to remove K  .  Temperature dependent synchrotron radiation powder X-ray diffraction pattern (SR-PXRD) of the samples were collected at two beamlines: The Swiss-Norwegian Beam Line (SNBL, BM01) at the European Synchrotron Radiation Facility (ESRF), Grenoble, France, and the X04SA beamline at the Swiss Light Source (SLS), Villigen, Switzerland. At SNBL, a Dectris 2 M Pilatus detector was used, with the wavelength of 0.6868 Å. 3  At X04SA, Pilatus and Mythen detectors were used, with wavelengths of 0.7088 Å and 0.7095 Å respectively. 4 The samples were packed in boron silicate capillaries (o.d. 0.5 -0.7 mm) and sealed with glue.

## Structure solution and refinement

The crystal structure of  -NaB11H14 (RT phase) solved ab initio ,  with direct space modeling using global optimization methods implemented in the Fox program. 5   The PXRD data were indexed  in  an  orthorhombic  unit  cell, a =  10.953(3), b =  12.140(2), c =  7.546(2)  Å, V = 1003.41(0.4) Å 3 (final values from Rietveld refinement at (RT) on the basis of 12 Bragg reflections). The B11H4 -units were treated as rigid bodies and the structure of  -NaB11H14 was solved in space group Pna 21. The structure was then refined with the Rietveld method using FullProf. 6 The backgrounds were defined by linear interpolation between selected points. Unit cell parameters,  scale  factors,  zero  point,  peak  shape  mixing  parameters  (pseudo-Voigt  function),  and three profile parameters (U, V, W) were refined together with occupancies of Na atoms. The powder diffraction patterns of the mixed anion samples were indexed on a cubic unit cell with a ~7.9 Å unit cell, either I -centered (mixtures 1:1 and 2:1) or primitive (mixture 1:2). In analogy with the anion mixed compound Na2x (CB11H12) x (B12H12)1x , the anion packing was modeled as bcc, and the structures have been solved by Fox either in space group Im3 m or Pm3 n . 1 The Rietveld refinement was then performed with TOPAS, 7 modeling the anionic mixture as one

rigid  icosahedral  object  B12-xH12  with  one  boron  atom  partially  occupied  in  the  fraction  of NaB11H14:Na2B12H12 ratio. In the mixture compounds, the B11H14 -and the B12H12 2anions were treated as one element due to the similar size and number of electrons.

## Fourier-transform infrared spectroscopy

Fourier-transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) spectra were obtained in the range of 400 to 4000 cm -1 with a Bruker Alpha spectrophotometer inside the glovebox.

## Thermal analysis

The phase transitions and the decomposition reactions of the samples were studied by combined thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) and differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) using Netzsch STA449 F3 Jupiter. The samples (  5-10 mg) were packed in sealed Al crucible. A small hole was punched into the crucibles immediately prior to measurements allowing the release of the evolved gases. All the measurements were carried out using a temperature ramp of 5 K/min under argon flow of 20 ml/min. Two programs of (RT  -30  C  110  C  -30  C  500  C) and (RT  550  C) were used for the combined TGA-DSC measurements.

## Scanning electron microscopy

The scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images of the samples were collected with a FEI NanoSEM 230 at 5 kV. The samples were carbon coated with a Q150T, Quorum technologies evaporator/sputter machine prior to the measurement. Few nanometers of carbon was coated using a carbon rod (with a tip diameter of d = 1.4 mm) and applying a pulsed current of 60 mA for six cycles of 10 seconds.

## Density functional theory calculations

Density  functional  theory  (DFT)  calculations  were  performed  within  the  plane-wave  DFT method implemented in the Vienna ab initio Simulation Package (VASP). 8 The atoms were replaced with projected augmented wave potentials 9 with the valence electron configurations (1s 1 ) for H, (2s 2 2p 1 ) for B, (2p 6 3s 1 ) for Na. The gradient-corrected (GGA) exchange-correlation

functional and the non-local correction accounting for a weak dispersive interaction were applied. 9,10 The experimental crystal structure was optimized with the conjugated gradient method with respect to the internal atomic positions, symmetry constraints were imposed in the optimization procedure. Once the ground state atomic configuration was established 12 new configurations were generated by shifting Na atom form 4 a ( x, y, z ) site to ( x ± δ, y ± δ, z ± δ) with δ = 0.3  Å.  The  same  optimization  procedure  was  applied.  Auxiliary  calculations  for  separated B11H14 -anion and NaB11H14 molecule were performed with an orthorhombic cell with at least 8  Å  of  vacuum  in  each  direction.  The  charge  density  analysis  was  performed  with  Bader method 11 on the charge density grid with spacing 0.05 Å or smaller.

## Electrochemical Characterization

The Na-ion conductivities were measured by electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) using a Bio-Logic VMP3 multi-channel potentiostat in the frequency range from 1 MHz to 1 Hz and voltage amplitude of 50 mV. Powder samples were compressed in pellets with thicknesses between 0.8 and 1.4 mm and diameters of 10 or 12 mm using an axial hydraulic press with a pressure of 260 to 375 MPa. The relative densities of the pellets were in the range of 0.67 to 0.72 (Tab. S6). The pellets were sandwiched between indium foils to improve the contact between the sample and the molybdenum electrodes and were mounted in airtight stainless steel cells. The samples were measured in two cycles from either -20 or -40  C to 100  C in a climatic chamber and at each temperature the cell was equilibrated for 2 h.

At Low temperatures, R1/Q2+R2/Q2+Q3 model was used to fit the conductivity data in which R1 and R2 correspond to bulk and interphase contributions and Q correspond to the electrode contribution. At higher temperatures, only one spike was observed in the Nyquist plot, which is ascribed to the electrode contribution and the pellet resistance R was extracted from the intercept of low-frequency tail in the Nyquist plots.

Cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements were carried out at 60 °C after thermal equilibration for 12 h using a low-current channel of the VMP3 electrochemical workstation. Two separate cells were prepared for studying the reductive and oxidative stabilities. For the reductive stability, an asymmetric cell was prepared by sandwiching the electrolyte's pellet between Na and Al electrodes (Na/electrolyte/Al). Oxidative stability measurements were performed according to ref 12 . A small amount of carbon composite (C65) was used on one side of the electrolyte pellet which was in contact with the Pt working electrode. For this purpose, electrolyte and conductive carbon were hand-mixed using a mortar and pestle in the weight ratio of 85 to 15% for 15 minutes. Then a two-layer pellet was formed with 5 mg of the mixture and  100 mg of electrolyte with a pressure of 173 MPa. 12 Finally an asymmetric cell was prepared by sandwiching the electrolyte's pellet between Na and Pt electrodes, Al electrode was also used to improve  the  contact  of  Pt  electrode  with  stainless  steel  electrodes  of  the  cell  (Na/electrolyte/electrolyte+C/Pt). In both measurements, E onset  was determined from the intersection of two linear lines ( R 2 &gt; 0.99). A line fitted to the current associated with the double-layer capacitance and a line fitted to oxidative/reductive current.

Galvanostatic  cycling  of  symmetric  Na/NaB11H14/Na  and  Na/Na(B11H14)(B12H12)2/Na  cells were performed using the same setup as described above. For the first 12 h, only the OCV of the cell was measured without applying any current. Then for the next 24 h, the current of 25  A/cm 2 was applied (one h on each direction). Finally, the current was increased to 50  A/cm 2 and the measurement was continued. The measurements were carried out at 60  C. However, for the Na5(B11H14)(B12H12)2 sample, the galvanostatic cycling was continued at 25  C after 410 h of stripping and plating at 60  C.

Figure S2 PXRD patterns (  Cu = 1.5406 Å) of the commercial NaB11H14 in comparison with NaB11H14  synthesized  by  different  methods.  Symbols:   -NaB11H14,   -NaB11H14,   -NaB11H14,  Nax+2y(B11H14)x(B12H12)y ( Im -3 m ).

Different degrees of broadening in the PXRD of the synthesized NaB11H14 samples is attributed to different crystallite sizes for each batch.

Figure S4 a) Solid-state  23 Na MAS NMR spectra of NaB11H14, Na2B12H12 and Nax+2y(B11H14) x(B12H12)y  compounds with x:y ratios of 2:1, 1:1 and 1:2. Spinning sidebands observed for Na2B12H12 are indicated by *. b) The central transition of I) simulated and II) experimental 23 Na MAS NMR spectrum of Na2B12H12. The simulation was performed using the software DMFIT 13 and convergence was obtained applying the quadrupolar parameters d( 23 Na)iso = -7.8 pm, CQ = 1.31 MHz and h = 0.50.

Figure S5 PXRD pattern and Rietveld refinement of NaB11H14 obtained based on method 1 at RT (  = 0.6868 Å, Full prof). Blue line: experimental data; red line: calculated pattern, grey line:  difference  pattern.  Rwp = 2.34% (not corrected for background),  2 = 33.7. The unexplained peaks correspond to  and  phases of NaB11H14.

Superposition of the DFT calculated structures for NaB11H14 suggested the partial occupancy of Na atoms for both octahedral and tetrahedral sites (Fig. S6a). Additionally, the independent configurations with Na distributed over tetrahedral or octahedral sites resulted in the calculated ground state energy difference of &lt; 25 meV per formula unit (Fig. S6b). Therefore the structure was solved by using two Na atoms in tetrahedral and one in octahedral sites. The partial occupancy of Na in both octahedral and tetrahedral sites has been also observed previously in similar systems such as Na2B12H12-xIx. 14 DFT calculations also implies anion disorder which is reflected in high displacement factor for B and H (Fig S6, Tab. S1). Anion disorder for NaB11H14 has been suggested in the previous work 15 as well and our model shows the coexistence of anion and cation disorder for this structure. This is consistent with the polarity of the B11H14 -cage (Fig. S8) and preference of sodium atoms to stay away from the open side of the cage.

Figure S6 a) Overlap of the calculated and experimental structures at room temperature (yellow, red for sodium atoms and blue for B atoms in the experimental structure and magneta for Na and olive for B atoms in the calculated structures. b) Energy differences for five configurations from the calculations with different initial combinations of Na in tetrahedral and octahedral sites.

Figure S7 The electron localization function of B11H14 -anions. a) Structure with two bridging H atoms and one endo-H atom. B) Structure with three bridging H atoms.

The charge distribution at the vertex with missing B is much flatter and the structure with two bridging H atoms is by 30 mV more stable. Moreover, Na + has a strong preference to be located at the vertex opposite to the open side.

Figure S8 a) The Bader charges on atoms in the B11H14 -anion in a vacuum; b,c) in a vacuum with sodium atom in the vicinity and d) in the Pna 21 structure. Boron is represented with green balls, sodium in yellow and hydrogen in light pink. The bold number on the right indicates an integral charge on each side of the anion. The total charge on anion depends on the charge transfer between Na + and B11H14 -, thus it is lower than -1e, except anion in a vacuum.

The charge density of anion can be characterized by electron localization function (ELF) and it is presented in Fig S8. The charge distribution analyzed with the Bader method indicates a small asymmetry of charge on the anion in a vacuum (Fig S8a). The integral charge is -0.54e and 0.46e on the open and bottom vertex. Interaction of anion with Na leads to charge redistribution such that charge density depends on the position of the cation. In vacuum the asymmetry of charges range from -0.78e/-0.12e to -0.35e/-0.55e as presented in Fig S8b,c. In the crystalline structure, charge asymmetry is -0.78e/-0.20e with lower electron density at the open apex (Figure S8d). In general charge on boron atoms at the open apex is larger (+0.8e) than on the bottom side (+0.5e) and this is not strongly influenced by the presence of the surrounding cations. The

higher electron density at the bottom of the cage attracts the Na + atom and leads to the preferred cation location.

Figure S9 a) PXRD pattern of NaB11H14 synthesized by method 1 measured at room temperature and -110  C. b) Comparisons of the PXRD patterns of commercial NaB11H14 and NaB11H14 synthesized by methods 1 and 2 at RT (  = 0.7095 Å).

In Figure S9.b the different intensity ratios of Bragg reflections of (011), (020), (111), (200) in samples synthesized by different methods also suggest various states of Na disorder in the structures.

Figure S10 PXRD pattern and Rietveld refinement NaB11H14 synthesized by method 1 and measured at -110  C (  = 0.7095 Å, Full prof). Blue line: experimental data; red line: calculated pattern, grey line: difference pattern. Rwp = 3.89% (not corrected for background),  2 = 7.2. The unexplained peaks correspond to  and  phases of NaB11H14.

Figure S11 PXRD pattern and Rietveld refinement of Na4(B11H14)2(B12H12) (  = 0.7095 Å, TOPAS). Blue line: experimental data; red line: calculated pattern, grey line: difference pattern. Rwp = 2.14 %,  2 = 3.11.

Figure S12 PXRD pattern and Rietveld refinement of Na3(B11H14)(B12H12) (  = 0.7095 Å, TOPAS). Blue line: experimental data; Red line: calculated pattern, grey line: difference pattern. Rwp = 2.57%,  2 = 3.92.

Figure S13 PXRD pattern and Rietveld refinement of Na5(B11H14)(B12H12)2 (  = 0.7095 Å, TOPAS). Blue line: experimental data; Red line: calculated pattern, grey line: difference pattern. Rwp = 1.63 %,  2 = 2.42.

In order to understand the effect of heat treatment on the observed phases, the same batch of commercial NaB11H14 was selected and heat-treated to 100  C for 2 and 16 h. PXRD measurements (Fig. S14a) show that the sample annealed for 2 h contains  ,  and  phases while the sample annealed for 16 h shows only  and  phases. This is also in agreement with what has been observed in ref 15 . NMR measurements (Fig. S14b) confirm that heat treatment at 100  C for 16 h does not decompose the sample and no additional B-containing compound is formed compared to the sample heat-treated at 100  C for 2 h.

In the next step, IR and DSC measurements combined with MS were performed to understand whether the  -phase has the same stoichiometry (NaB11H14) or is a solvate phase. In the IR measurements (Fig S14c), no C-H and O-H bands are observed in the region of 2800 to 3500 cm -1 indicating that no solvent is remaining for both annealed samples. DSC-MS measurements (Fig S15a,b), also did not show a release of any gas at the transition temperature of  to  phase (75-85  C). Therefore, we believe that  -NaB11H14 is a metastable phase and disappears when long annealing is applied.

Figure S14 a) PXRD of the synthesized and commercial NaB11H14 annealed at 100  C for 2 and 16 h.  ,  ,  symbols mark the Bragg reflections of the room-temperature  and  phase and the high-temperature  phase  of  NaB11H14,  respectively  (λCu  =  1.5406  Å).  b) 11 B{ 1 H} NMR spectra of the samples in DMSO-d6 solutions. c) FT-IR-absorption spectra of the samples.

Lower part: Corresponding MS signals shown for H2, H2O and THF by green, blue and red curves respectively. M/z = 42 is selected for THF due to the formation of cyclopropane (C3H6 + ) as a major fragmentation product with the highest intensity in the MS spectrum.

The refinement of B11H14 -and B12H12 2anionic mixture was performed with one rigid body description using the Z-matrix of B12H12 2with one boron position occupancy decreased according to the ratio of B11H14 -:B12H12 2. The difference in hydrogen positions between B11H14 -and B12H12 2was neglected due to the low X-ray scattering contribution of hydrogen. All atoms of the rigid body are listed in the tables of atomic coordinates, which explains the low occupancy values.

## Chapter 8

## Li nido -Hydroborate-Based Solid Electrolyte for All-Solid-State Lithium Batteries

The sodium salt of a nido -hydroborate [B11H14] -shows high Na + conductivity by anion mixing with a closo -hydroborate, as discussed in the previous chapter. To examine the analogous lithium salt as a candidate for a cost-effective building block for Li + hydroborate solid electrolytes, the nido -hydroborate is transferred to the lithium systems by modifying the last step of the NaB 11 H14 synthesis.

In this study, we first synthesize the lithium salt of [B 11H14] -with high purity by careful  analysis  of  impurities  done  by  NMR  and  MS.  In  analogy  to  NaB 11 H14,  the synthesized,  solvent-free  LiB 11 H14  exhibits  phase  transition  at  a  lower  temperature  of around 112 °C than that of Li2B12H12. The anion mixing of the Li nido -hydroborate with Li-1-CB9H10 marks high room-temperature ionic conductivity of 1 × 10 -3  S cm -1 in the hcp anion lattice, while the anion mixing with LiCB11 H12 lowers the ionic conductivity by one order of magnitude with the fcc anion lattice. Because the [B 11H14] -and [CB9H10] -have similar sizes, this result suggests the use of small hydroborate anions to enable favorable cation conduction channels for the small size of Li + ions. However, similarly to NaB11 H14, the  instability  of  the  open-cage  structure  leads  to  a  low  onset  temperature  of  thermal decomposition at 180 °C and a narrow electrochemical stability window. The incompatibility with a lithium metal anode is shown to be mitigated kinetically by anion mixing  and  by  possible  formation  of  [B 11H13] 2 -at  the  interface,  whereas  the  limited electrochemical oxidative stability up to 2.6 V vs Li + /Li needs additional considerations on the integration of cathode active materials. To match the electrochemical stability range of the electrolyte, TiS 2 is selected as a cathode active material for the fabrication of a proofof-concept TiS2|Li2(B11H14)(CB11H12)|Li cell. With a minimal electrolyte decomposition, this cell exhibits stable capacity retention of 82 % after 150 cycles at C/5 at 60 °C under 3.2 MPa.  This  demonstrates  that  the  structural  stability  of  hydroborate  anions  directly influences  their  electrochemical  stability.  Therefore,  designing  a  stable  hydroborate structure,  as  well  as  favorable  cation  conduction  channels,  is  crucial  for  possible applications in high-voltage all-solid-state batteries. This study provides a hint to discover another promising candidate for hydroborate solid electrolytes.

In this study, I mainly contributed to the design of the electrochemical experiments such as the electrochemical stability measurements and galvanostatic cycling experiments. Additionally,  I  advised  on  anion  mixing  with  Li-1-CB 9 H10  and  performed  long-term cycling  experiments  of  a  TiS 2 |Li2(B11 H14)(CB11H12)|Li  cell.  Dr.  Seyedhosein  Payandeh prepared most of the materials and performed most of the structural characterization and electrochemical  experiments.  Dr.  Daniel  Rentsch  conducted  NMR  measurements  and analyzed 2D NMR data. Dr. Zbigniew Ł odziana performed DFT calculations. Dr. Laurent Bigler  performed  electrospray  ionization  mass  spectrometry  (ESI-MS).  Prof.  Radovan Č erný contributed to the crystal structure solution. Dr. Seyedhosein Payandeh designed the experiments, performed the data analysis, and wrote the manuscript with input from all co-authors.

This chapter is published as S. Payandeh, D. Rentsch, Z. Ł odziana, R. Asakura, L. Bigler, R. Č erný, C. Battaglia, A. Remhof, Adv. Funct. Mater. 2021 , 2010046.

www.afm-journal.de

## Nido -Hydroborate-Based Electrolytes for All-Solid-State Lithium Batteries

SeyedHosein Payandeh,* Daniel Rentsch, Zbigniew Łodziana,* Ryo Asakura, Laurent Bigler, Radovan Černý, Corsin Battaglia, and Arndt Remhof*

Hydroborate-based solid electrolytes have recently been successfully employed in high voltage, room temperature all-solid-state sodium batteries. The transfer to analogous lithium systems has failed up to now due to the lower conductivity of the corresponding lithium compounds and their high cost. Here LiB 11 H 14 nido -hydroborate as a cost-e/uniFB00ective building block and its high-purity synthesis is introduced. The crystal structures of anhydrous LiB 11 H14 as well as of LiB 11 H14based mixed-anion solid electrolytes are solved and high ionic conductivities of 1.1 × 10 -4  S cm -1 for Li 2 (B 11 H14)(CB 11 H12) and 1.1 × 10 -3  S cm -1 for Li 3 (B 11 H14)(CB 9 H10) 2 are obtained, respectively. LiB 11 H14 exhibits an oxidative stability limit of 2.6 V versus Li + /Li and the proposed decomposition products are discussed based on density functional theory calculations. Strategies are discussed to improve the stability of these compounds by modifying the chemical structure of the nido -hydroborate cage. Galvanostatic cycling in symmetric cells with two lithium metal electrodes shows a small overpotential increase from 22.5 to 30 mV after 620 h (up to 0.5 mAh cm -2 ), demonstrating that the electrolyte is compatible with metallic anodes. Finally, the Li 2 (B11 H14)(CB11 H 12 ) electrolyte is employed in a proof-of-concept half cell with a TiS 2  cathode with a capacity retention of 82% after 150 cycles at C/5.

## 1. Introduction

Solid-state electrolytes combining  liquid-like ionic  conductivity, stability against metallic anodes, compatibility with highvoltage cathodes, processability, and low environmental impact and cost are essential for next-generation all-solid-state batteries

Dr. S. Payandeh, R. Asakura, Dr. C. Battaglia, Dr. A. Remhof Laboratory of Materials for Energy Conversion

Empa Swiss Federal Laboratories for Materials Science and Technology Ueberlandstrasse 129, Dübendorf 8600, Switzerland E-mail: Seyedhosein.payandeh@empa.ch; arndt.remhof@empa.ch Dr. D. Rentsch Laboratory of Functional Polymers Empa Swiss Federal Laboratories for Materials Science and Technology Ueberlandstrasse 129, Dübendorf 8600, Switzerland

The ORCID identification number(s) for the author(s) of this article can be found under https://doi.org/10.1002/adfm.202010046.

© 2021 The Authors. Advanced Functional Materials published by WileyVCH GmbH. This is an open access article under the terms of the Creative Commons Attribution License, which permits use, distribution and reproduction in any medium, provided the original work is properly cited.

DOI: 10.1002/adfm.202010046

Adv. Funct. Mater. 2021 , 2010046

(ASSBs).  They  promise  to  overcome  the limitations of the current lithium-ion technology with higher power and energy density while improving operational safety. [1,2]

Currently, a few material classes exhibiting su/uniFB03ciently high  ionic conductivities  are  under  consideration  as  potential candidates  for  the  solid  electrolyte  of  allsolid-state Li batteries: Ion-conducting oxides show moderate to high Li-ion conductivities on the order of  10 -6 to 10 -3 S  cm -1 at  room  temperature. [3] They are hard, brittle materials that require sintering to minimize interface resistance  and  are  vulnerable  to  crack  formation  and  contact  loss  caused  by  volume changes during cycling. [4]   Thiophosphates such as Li 10GeP2S12 ( σ = 12 mS cm -1 ) and Li 2 S-P2S5 ( σ = 17  mS cm -1 )  exhibit liquidlike ionic conductivity at room temperature. Their  limited  chemical  and  electrochemical interface stability,  however,  require the  use  of  protective  coatings  to  form stable  interfaces,  hindering  their  integra- tion into competitive batteries. [5,6]   Halides such as Li 3YCl6 and Li 3 YBr6, have recently shown high ionic conductivity &gt; 1 mS cm -1 at  25 ° C  and  high  oxidative  stability  up  to  4  V  versus  Li + /Li. However,  they  are  unstable  against  Li  metal  and  are  typically  used  with  a  Li-In  alloy  or  in  two-layer  combination  with other  electrolytes  to  stabilize  the  interface  with  Li  metal. [7-9]

Prof. Z. Łodziana Institute of Nuclear Physics Polish Academy of Sciences ul. Radzikowskiego 152, Kraków 31-342, Poland E-mail: zbigniew.lodziana@ifj.edu.pl R. Asakura Department of Physical Chemistry University of Geneva 24 rue du Général-Dufour, Geneva 4 1211, Switzerland Prof. L. Bigler Department of Chemistry University of Zurich Winterthurerstrasse 190, Zurich 8057, Switzerland Prof. R. Černý Laboratory of Crystallography Department of Quantum Matter Physics University of Geneva

Quai Ernest-Ansermet 24, Geneva 4 1211, Switzerland

www.advancedsciencenews.com

Solid polymer electrolytes composed of a Li salt dissolved into a  polymer  matrix  are  another  class  of  solid-state  Li-ion  conductors.  Due  to  their  flexibility,  low  interfacial  resistance,  and low cost, they are still in the focus of many research activities, regardless of their low ionic conductivity, limited thermal range of operation and narrow electrochemical stability window. [10]

Recently,  complex  metal  hydroborates,  in  particular,  metal nido -/ closo -hydroborates and carbacloso -hydroborates have emerged  as  promising  alternative  solid  electrolytes, [11-14] providing  liquid-like  ionic  conductivity, [15] stability  against  alkali metal anodes, and oxidative stability of up to 4 V versus Na + / Na. [16-23]   Furthermore, they show favorable mechanical properties [24] and are solution-processable, facilitating their integration in ASSB using slurry processes. [21]

Due  to  the  proper  matching  size  of  the  hydroborate  anions with Na + cation and formation of discorded structures, the ionic conductivity of the Na-hydroborates usually surpasses the ones of their Li equivalents. [15,23,25,26] An ionic conductivity of ≈ 70 mS cm -1 at 27 ° C has been obtained for Na 2 (CB 9 H10)(CB11 H 12 ) in this class of  materials. [15] Stable  3  V  cycling  was  demonstrated  in  Na-Sn/ NaCrO2 half  cells  with  high  cathode  mass  loadings  of ≈ 7 .7  mg cm -2 . [27]   Moreover, some of us recently demonstrated stable 4 V room-temperature  cycling  of  Na/Na3 (VOPO4)2 F  all-solid-state cells for &gt; 800 cycles without any protective coatings. [28]

Currently, the most promising Li-ion solid electrolyte in the hydroborate  family  is  (1 -x )LiCB9H10 -x LiCB11H12  ( x = 0.1-0.9) which is stable against Li metal and shows high ionic conductivity  of  6.9 × 10 -5 -6.7 × 10 -3 S  cm -1 at  25 ° C. [15,18,29]   However, LiCB9H10  is  challenging  to  synthesize  and  therefore  rather expensive  with  a  price &gt; 120  000  €  kg -1 ,  hindering  a  broader application of this material. [30]

In  this  study,  we  propose  the  use  of  the nido -hydroborate (lithium  tetradecahydroundecaborate,  LiB 11 H 14 )  as  a  cost-e/uniFB00ective  replacement  of  LiCB 9 H10  as  the  B 11H14 -anion  exhibits  the same  charge  and  similar  size  as  the  CB9H10 -anion.  First,  we describe  the  synthesis  and  purification  of  solvent-free  LiB 11 H14 and we solve its crystal structure. Several impurity side products are thoroughly identified using  11 B nuclear magnetic resonance spectroscopy  (NMR)  and  electrospray  ionization  high-resolution mass  spectrometry  (ESI-MS).  Second, nido -hydroborate-based electrolytes are formed by mixing LiB 11 H14 with closo -hydroborates and carbacloso -hydroborates,  such  as  Li 2 B12 H 12 ,  LiCB 11 H12,  and LiCB9H10 and their crystal structures and electrochemical properties  are  investigated.  Finally, nido -hydroborate-based electrolytes are evaluated in a proof-of-concept battery with a Li metal anode and a TiS2  cathode. Our results show economic viability of hydroborate electrolytes for all-solid-state lithium batteries.

## 2. Results and Discussion

## 2.1. Synthesis and Impurity Identifications of Novel Nido -Hydroborates

## 2.1.1. Experimental Procedure to Synthesize Solvent-Free LiB 11H14

LiB11 H 14  was synthesized based on the reaction of n -butyllithium (LiC4 H 9 , BuLi) with (CH3)3NHB11H14 in tetrahydrofuran (THF)solution according to Equation (1)

Adv. Funct. Mater. 2021 , 2010046

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

After THF was removed, water was added to filter unreacted, insoluble  (CH3 ) 3 NHB11H14,  resulting  in  an  aqueous  solution of  LiB11H14(H2O) n (1 &lt; n &lt; 2).  The  complete  removal  of  the coordinated  water  without  decomposition  of  the  B 11H14 -cage is  challenging.  Our  initial  attempts  using  heat  treatment  led to the formation of LiB 11 H 13 R impurities (R = OH, OB11H13Li, O-Butyl(O-Bu))  and  LiB 11 H 12O  (Samples A -D ,  Table  S1,  Supporting Information). Figure 1 a shows the open-cage molecular structure  of  the nido -hydroborate  anion.  The  impurities  were identified  by  11 B{ 1 H} NMR (see Figure 1b; and Table S1 (Supporting Information); for discussion, see Section 2.2 below).

The synthesis of pure LiB11 H 14  was made possible by using an excess of n -butyllithium in the synthesis (Equation (1)). This leads to a partial reduction of the sample, resulting in a mixture of LiB 11 H14 and Li2B11 H 13 .  After heat treatment under aqueous conditions,  Li 2 B11 H 13 converted  back  to  LiB 11 H14  according  to Equation  (2),  allowing  the  synthesis  of  solvent-free  LiB 11 H14 (Figure 1c)

<!-- formula-not-decoded -->

It is important to mention that H 2  is released for the synthesis of  (CH3 ) 3 NHB11H14  as  a  precursor. [13][31] Therefore,  H 2 release during the synthesis should be considered for the large-scale production of these materials. However, similar compounds such as M2B12H12 (M = Li,  Na)  in  which  H 2 is  released  during  the  synthesis are commercialized and produced on a kg scale. [32]

Figure 1c displays the respective 11 B{ 1 H} NMR spectra. The complete  removal  of  water  was  evidenced  by  powder  X-ray powder  di/uniFB00raction (PXRD),  IR spectroscopy,  and  thermogravimetric analysis (TGA, see Figures S1 and S2, Supporting Information). In the last step, LiB 11 H 14 is  separated  by  adding THF  to  the  mixture  to  filter  THF-insoluble  LiOH  and  by subsequent  drying  at  100 ° C  for  16  h  to  remove  THF.  This procedure also allows the removal of coordinated water from commercial LiB11 H 14(H2O) n samples and is described in more detail in  the  Supporting  Information  (Samples E,  G ).  Finally,  anion mixing was used as a known strategy to improve ionic conductivity. [15,33] The  pure  LiB 11 H14  was  ball-milled  with  LiCB 9 H10, LiCB11H12,  or Li 2 B12 H 12 in di/uniFB00erent molar ratios (Table S1, Supporting  Information).  The  compositions  of  the  samples  were determined by peak shape analysis of the 1D  11 B NMR spectra. The results are summarized in Table S1 (Supporting Information). We found that LiB 11 H 14   partially decomposed during the ball-milling process. This leads to the formation of nonassignable resonances in the  11 B NMR data (Table S1 and Figure S3, Supporting Information).

## 2.1.2. Identification of LiB 11 H13R By-Products of LiB 11 H14 Synthesis

LiB 11 H 14 ( 1 ) : The molecular structure of 1 depicted in Figure 1a has  been  fully  determined  previously  in  ref.  [13]  It  can  be described as an oblate spheroid with the minor axis (the 'height') perpendicular to the opening of the nest of 4.6 Å and a major axis (the 'width' or the 'diameter') of 5.3 Å ( Figures 2 and 3 a).

www.advancedsciencenews.com

Figure 1. a) General chemical structure with the labeling of boron atoms of the nido -hydroborate anions LiB11H13R 1-4 (R in 1 = H, 2 = OH, 3 = O(B11H13)Li, 4 = O-Bu), 5 = LiB 11 H12O. b)  11 B{ 1 H} NMR spectra of LiB11H14(H2O) n and LiB11 H 13 R after heat treatment under di/uniFB00erent conditions. The boron resonances of species 2, 3, and 4 are assigned by red, blue, and green numbers. c)  11 B{ 1 H} NMR spectra of i) the precursors (CH3)3NHB11H14 or LiB 11H14(H2O) n , (bottom), ii) the mixture of LiB 11 H14 and Li 2 B11 H13 in basic solutions (middle), and (iii) of pure LiB 11 H14 after heat treatment (top). d) 11 B{ 1 H}, e)  1 H- 11 B HSQC, and f)  11 B- 11 B{ 1 H} COSY NMR spectra with the assignment of resonances to the chemical structure of 2 in Sample F. Please note that the signals of the starting material 1 account to ≈ 93 mol% of the total signal intensity. The NMR spectra shown in b,c) are recorded in THF and d-f) in CD3CN solutions. g) Expanded regions of interest of the (-)-ESI-MS with  11 B/ 10 B isotopic patterns assigned to 2-5 for Samples B, C, and F.

www.advancedsciencenews.com

LiB 11 H 13 OH  ( 2 ) :  The  chemical  structures  of  the  abovementioned  impurities,  which  were  formed  during  the  drying process (Samples, B , C ,  and F ;  see Table S1, Supporting Information),  were  unveiled  using  NMR  and  MS  (Figure  S4  and Table S2, Supporting Information). The 1D and 2D  1 H and  11 B NMR data of Sample F leads to the identification of LiB11H13OH ( 2) as  a  first  by-product  as  shown  in  Figure  1d-f.  Besides  the strong resonances of the starting material, six distinct signals with an intensity ratio of 1:2:2:2:2:1 are observed in the 1D 11 B NMR spectrum (Figure 1d) and in the  1 H- 11 B HSQC NMR spectrum, each of these resonances results in a distinct cross peak assigned to positions 2-7 of the chemical structure (Figure 1a). Note  that  the  cross  signal  of  position  1  in  the  spectrum  is hidden  beyond  the  strong  resonances  of  LiB 11 H 14   ( 1 ).  The bridging protons Hbr of LiB 11 H 13 OH at the open apex correlate to boron resonances at 16.9, -10.2, and -28.4 ppm (Figure 1e), which therefore can be assigned to positions 5-7 of the chemical structure (Figure 1a, bonds of protons H br  are shown with dashed  lines).  From 11 B  NMR chemical shift  reasons  and  the relative  signal  intensity,  the  resonance  at  16.9  ppm  can  be assigned to B7 .  Position  B2  was  assigned  from  the  cross  peak between  the  boron  resonances  at  16.9  and -22.6  ppm  in  the 11 B- 11 B  correlated  NMR  spectrum  (Figure  1f).  The  cross  peak at -14.3/ -22.6  ppm  observed  in  the 11 B- 11 B  correlated  NMR spectrum leads to the unambiguous identification of B1, which was hidden beyond strong resonances of the starting material in  the  other  1D  and  2D  NMR  data.  Subsequently,  all 1 H and 11 B  NMR  chemical  shifts  of  the  molecule  were  assigned  (for enlarged regions of  1 H1 H and  11 B- 11 B correlated NMR spectra see  Figure  S6,  Supporting  Information).  The  presence  of  an OH group in 2 (in Sample F ) was confirmed by the correlation observed at 4.00 ppm to the bridging protons Hbr  at -2.21 ppm in the  1 H- 1 H correlated NMR spectrum (Figure S6e, Supporting Information). All other observable  1 H- 1 H correlations originate from  2 J or 3 J couplings and support the signal assignments to the  chemical  structure  of  the  boron  cage  shown  in  Figure  1a. Please  note  that  the  boron  boron  bonds  of  B2  B2,  B5  B5, and  B6  B7  could  not  be  verified  to  fully  identify  the  boron cage.  For  reasons  of  symmetry,  the  boron  atoms  in  the  first two cases are homotopic and therefore have identical  11 B NMR chemical shifts, and relaxation reasons may be responsible for the missing B6  B7 cross peak (see, e.g., [34-36] ).

The  presence  of  LiB 11H13OH  ( 2 )  in  Samples B and F was further confirmed by their corresponding mass spectra (Figure 1g). [37] The  11 B/ 10 B isotopic patterns with the most abundant signals at m / z 149.215 are assigned to the molecular composition of B 11H13OH -. A calculated MS pattern is presented in Figure S7 (Supporting Information) to confirm the assignment to  B11H13OH -and  the  summary  of  all  MS  data  is  given  in Table S2 (Supporting Information).

Li 2 (B 11 H 13 -O-B 11 H 13 )  ( 3 ) :  For  each  of  the 11 B  NMR  resonances assigned to 2 , a 2nd signal at almost identical chemical shift  is  observed  in  Sample B (see  Figure  1b).  The  decisive deviation  in  the  NMR  data  of  this  new  species  ( 3 )  is  the complete absence of an OH resonance in the  1 H NMR spectrum. Nevertheless, the chemical structures of 2 and 3 must be  rather  similar,  since  only  small  deviations  of  the 1 H and 11 B NMR chemical shifts can be observed for the two species (Figure  1b;  and  Figure  S8,  Supporting  Information).  In  the

Adv. Funct. Mater. 2021 , 2010046

www.afm-journal.de spectral  region  showing  the  resonances  of  bridging  protons Hbr  of  the  di/uniFB00usion-edited 1 H  NMR  spectrum  (Figure  S9a, Supporting  Information),  the  signal  of 2 is  considerably reduced  with  increasing  gradient  strengths  compared  to  the resonance of 3 . This indicates that 3 di/uniFB00uses less rapidly and must  probably  have  a  higher  molecular  weight  than 2 .  In the  mass  spectrum  of  Sample B ,  the  most  abundant  signal of the 11 B/ 10 B isotopic pattern centered at m / z 287 .433 can be assigned to the sum formula B22H26LiO -(Li(B 11H13-O-B11H13) -) (Figure 1g; and Figure S10, Supporting Information). Additionally, the peak pattern observed for Samples B and C with only 0.5  mass  units  distances  of  the  isotopic  peaks  and  the  most abundant  mass  peak  centered  at m / z 140.209  (Figure  S11, Supporting  Information)  can  be  assigned  to  the  doublecharged anions of 3 ,  with  the  molecular  formula  B 22H26O 2 -. This finding confirms the hypothesis from the NMR data that 3 should  have  a  similar  molecular  composition  but  a  higher molecular  weight  than 2 .  The  formation  of  a  dimer  with  a bridging  oxygen  seems  not  to  be  uncommon  for closo -hydroborates as it has been previously observed for other species such as [B 10 H13-O-B10H13]. [38]

Li(B 11 H 13 -O-Bu)  ( 4 ) :  For  species 4 ,  the 1 H- 11 B  and 11 B- 11 B correlated 2D NMR data (Figure S12, Supporting Information) suggest  a  boron  boron network similar to the data  evaluated for 2 and 3 .  In the  1 H and  13 C NMR spectra (Figure S13, Supporting  Information)  resonances  assignable  to  an O -n -butyl (O-Bu)  group  were  identified.  For  chemical  shift  reasons,  the signal at 70.7 ppm in the  13 C NMR spectrum can be assigned to  an n -butyl  ether  residue,  but  NMR  data  further  suggest that 4 consists  of  a  B 11 H13  core  unit  with  an  attached  butoxy group  at  the  open  apex  boron.  This  was  confirmed  by  mass spectrometry  of  Sample C with  a  prominent 11 B/ 10 B  isotopic pattern  with  the  most  abundant  signal  at m/z 205.277,  corresponding to the molecular formula B11H13-O-Bu -(B11H22C4O -) (Figure 1g; and Figure S14, Supporting Information). As already observed in the case of Sample B (a mixture of 2 and 3 ), in the di/uniFB00usion-edited  1 H NMR spectrum of Sample C (Figure  S9b, Supporting Information) the signal of 4 is significantly reduced with increasing gradient strengths compared to the resonance of 3 ,  which is associated with the lower molecular weight of 4 compared to 3 . It is important to mention that the formation of compound 4 was  observed  only  when  LiB 11H14(H2O) n is  heattreated  in  THF.  Heat  treatment  of  LiB 11 H14  in  THF  did  not form compound 4 , suggesting that the coordinating water plays a role in C  O bond scission of tetrahydrofuran and its attachment to the boron cage. The formation of 4 was reinvestigated in presence of THF-d8 as a reactant. From the 1D and 2D  1 H, 2 H,  11 B,  and  13 C NMR data (see Figure S15 and discussion in the  Supporting  Information  part),  we  unequivocally  identified O-CD2-CD2-CD2-CD2H as the butoxy residue of 4 .

LiB 11 H 12 O ( 5 ) :  Besides  the  impurities 1-4 discussed  above, a tiny amount of a further impurity was observed in Sample F . In this sample, in addition to the resonances of 1 and 2 , crosspeaks of a minor by-product assignable to LiB11H12O ( 5 )  were observed  in  the 1 H- 11 B  HSQC  NMR  spectrum  of  Sample F when a very low threshold was applied (Figure S16, Supporting Information). All  1 H and  11 B NMR data of this species are very similar  to  the  data  described  in  ref.  [34,35]  This  assignment was  further  supported  by  the  mass  spectrum  of  Sample F

www.advancedsciencenews.com

(Figure  1g;  and  Figure  S7,  Supporting  Information),  with  the most  abundant  signal  of  the 11 B/ 10 B  isotopic  pattern  at m / z 147 .199, which is associated the sum formula of B 11 H 12O -( 5 ).

An overview of the  11 B NMR spectra with integrated regions of  selected  samples  is  presented  in  Figure  S17  (Supporting Information) and global assignments of  11 B NMR chemical shift regions of compounds 1 -5 are shown in Table S3 (Supporting Information). Besides, the ground state energy of di/uniFB00erent isomers of B11 H 13OH -( 2 ), (B 11 H13-O-B11H13) 2 -( 3 ), and B 11H12O -(5) are  calculated  and  shown  in  Figure  S18  (Supporting  Information). For B 11H13OH -and (B11 H 13 -O-B11 H 13 ) 2 -, the energy di/uniFB00erences between isomers are relatively small ( ≈ 0.1-0.2 eV) but the most stable isomers show the same configurations predicted by NMR. For B11H12O -,  the oxygen at the open vertex is far more stable  than  in  other  positions,  confirming  the  NMR  results. Note that the presence of oxygen at other positions might lead to a major change of anion structure.

Understanding  the  chemical  composition  of  the  impurities is the basis to establish a high purity synthesis method for LiB11 H 14 ,  as  discussed earlier. In the next sections, the e/uniFB00ect of anion mixing (including the impurities) on the crystal structures and  ionic  conductivities  are  discussed.  Moreover,  the  e/uniFB00ect  of impurities on the electrochemical stabilities of the compounds is investigated using density functional theory (DFT) calculations.

## 2.2. Crystal Structures of the Compounds

In the next step, the crystal structures of LiB11 H 14 and of the anion mixed compounds (LiB 11 H14:Li 2 B12 H 12 (1:1), LiB11 H 14 :LiCB11 H 12 (1:1,  1:2,  2:1),  and  LiB 11 H14:LiCB9H10  (1:2) were solved by means of PXRD.

The  crystal  structure  of  the  low  temperature  (LT)  LiB 11 H14 was  solved  in  an  orthorhombic  unit  cell  with  space  group Pbca and  cubic-close  packing  (ccp)  of  the  B 11H14 -anions  and was  optimized  by  DFT  (Figures  S19,  S20a,  and  S22,  Supporting Information). Unlike tetrahedral coordination of Na in NaB11H14, [13] Li + ions in this structure lie in trigonal planar sites formed by three B11 H 14 -anions and located between octahedral and tetrahedral sites (Figure 2b). Trigonal planar coordination of Li has been also observed for other Li salts of hydroborates, such as Li 2 B12 H 12 [39] and Li 2 B10 H 10 . [40]  In general, the preferred coordination  environment  of  the  cation  is  defined  based  on the  cation-to-anion  size  ratio  and  by  using  the  first  Pauling rule (Table S4, Supporting Information). [41,42] Taking  0.73  Å  as the  size  of  Li + (crystal  radius  in  tetrahedral  coordination [43] ) and  4.6  Å  as  the  estimated  size  of  the  B 11H14 -anion  (apical axis,  Figure  3a),  the  cation-to-anion  size  ratio  is ≈ 0.16,  suggesting  triangular  coordination. [41] However,  in  NaB 11 H14  with a larger cation (Na + ≈ 1.13 Å), crystal radius in tetrahedral coordination, [43] ), the cation/anion size ratio increases to ≈ 0.25, and tetrahedral coordination is preferred for Na + ions. Similarly to NaB11H14, the B11 H 14 -anion in LT-LiB 11 H14 is oriented with the open side of the cage away from the Li + cation. This preferred orientation is explained by the more negative closed side of the cage  that  interacts  with  the  cation  (Figure  3b).  A  comparable trend  has  been  observed  for  CB 11H12 -and  CB 9H10 -in  which the anions are oriented so that the considerably more positive

Adv. Funct. Mater. 2021 , 2010046

Figure 2. a) PXRD patterns of the LT and HT phases of LiB11 H 14  and mixtures of LiB 11 H14:LiB 11 H13R' (R' = OH and OB11H13Li), LiB11 H 14 :Li 2 B12 H 12 (1:1),  LiB 11 H14:LiCB11 H 12 (1:1),  LiB 11 H14:LiCB9H10  (1:2)  in  a  logarithmic scale  ( λ Cu ).  Crystal  structures  of  b)  LT  and  c)  HT  phases  of  LiB 11 H14, d) Li 3 (B11 H14)(B 12 H12), e) Li 2 (B11 H14)(CB11 H 12 ), and f) Li 3 (B11 H14)(CB9H10) 2 . # and * indicate the characteristic Bragg reflections of the Pca 21 phase and of the remaining LiB11 H 14 , respectively. B atoms in the crystal structures are represented by green and blue (for B11 H 14 -)  spheres, and H and Li atoms are represented by gray and red spheres, respectively.

www.advancedsciencenews.com

Figure 3. a) Approximate relative sizes of the hydroborates anions. The height and width of the hydroborate anions are shown by gray and red bars, respectively. b) Calculated Bader charges and Li configuration for the hydroborates in the ground and least stable states. B, H, and Li atoms are represented by green, gray, and red spheres, respectively. B atoms for the B11 H 14 -and B 11H13 2 -are  presented in a light and dark blue, respectively. Orange arrows indicate the dipole moments.

C-bonded H atom avoids proximity to the neighboring cations (Figure  3b). [44,26] Upon  heating,  LiB 11 H 14   undergoes  a  phase transition at ≈ 112 ° C to a cubic phase with the Fm 3 space group and ccp packing of the anions similar to high temperature (HT) phases of Li 2 B12 H 12   and LiCB 11 H12 (Figure 1c; and Figure S2b, Supporting Information). [44,45]

The coordination environment of Li remains trigonal planar and 3D conduction channels appear to form for the Li + cations in  ccp  anion  packing  (Figure  S20b,  Supporting  Information). The Li + jumps between tetrahedral and octahedral sites occur through the shared faces where Li + is localized in a disordered manner (Figure S21a, Supporting Information). [46]  Preliminary DFT calculations of the conduction mechanism show Li + hopping  near  the  vicinity  of  tetrahedral  sites,  and  large  Li + -  Li + correlations  in  electrolytes  containing  a  fraction  of  CB9H10 -. This points out to lower activation energy of mixed anion compounds (Figure S21b, Supporting Information) Ion conduction mechanism of related compounds is discussed in detail. [47]

The sizes and polarities of the hydroborate anions under discussion are summarized in Figure 3. The charges were calculated in the simplified systems that consider one isolated hydroborate anion and cations compensating the charge of the anion. This  model  neglects  the  periodic  crystal  structure;  however,  it allows  observation  of  the  charge  redistribution  on  the  anions related  to  the  motion  of  Li.  When  LiB 11 H14  is  mixed  with  the bigger anions of Li 2 B12H12 and LiCB11H12 (Samples H -L ), solid

Adv. Funct. Mater. 2021 , 2010046

solutions form, and a B 11H14 -anion integrates into the LT crystal lattice  of  Li 2 B12 H 12 and  LiCB11 H 12 . [39,44] Therefore,  Li 3 (B11 H14) (B12 H12)  crystallizes  in  the Pa 3 space  group  and  Li 2 (B11 H14) (CB11 H 12 ),  Li 3 (B11 H14)(CB11 H 12 ) 2 ,  and  Li 3 (B11 H14) 2 (CB11 H 12 )  crystallize  in  the Pca 21 space  group  (Figure  2d,e;  and  Figure  S23, Supporting Information). Both structures have the ccp packing of the anions and triangular coordination for Li.

In the case of mixing LiB 11 H 14 with LiCB9H10 as an example for a smaller anion (Samples M, N ), the HT phase of LiCB9H10 with a hexagonal unit cell and with the space group P 31c is stabilized.  This  is  confirmed  by  the  disappearance  of  the  Bragg reflection  at  2 θ = 8.6 ° for  the  LT  phase  and  appearance  of  a shoulder at 2 θ = 16.6 ° for the HT phase (red and green arrows in Figure S24, Supporting Information). [15,25]  In this structure, Li atoms occupy tetrahedral sites sharing a face with an empty octahedral site (Figure 2f). The higher coordination of lithium is based on the smaller size of anions used in this compound and is in favor of Li-ion conductivity (based on the Pauling rule, Table S4, Supporting Information). [42]

In  addition,  the  presence  of  small  amount  of  impurities LiB11 H 13 R' (R' = OH, B11 H 13 OLi) in LiB 11H14 formed upon drying of LiB 11H14(H2O) n stabilizes the HT cubic phase of LiB 11 H14 with space  group Fm 3 (Sample A ).  The  di/uniFB00raction  pattern  of  this phase  ( Fm 3 )  is  very  similar  to  the  orthorhombic Pca 21 phase. These two patterns are distinct by the absence of the shoulder at 2 θ = 18.6 ° and some smaller reflections at 2 θ = 9.2, 12.9 ° for the cubic phase (Figure 2a). Structural parameters of the new compounds  determined  by  Rietveld  refinements  are  summarized in Table 1 .  The  atomic  coordinates  of  all  solved  structures  are presented in Tables S8-S12 (Supporting Information).

## 2.3. Thermal Behavior

Figure 4 a  shows  the  thermal  behavior  of  LiB 11 H 14 and  the mixed  hydroborate  compounds  as  observed  by  di/uniFB00erential scanning  calorimetry.  The  reversible  phase  transition  from the orthorhombic to the cubic phase of LiB11 H 14  appears upon heating  as  an  endothermic  peak  at ≈ 112 ° C  (Figure  4a,b;  and Figure S26c, Supporting Information). Upon cooling, LiB11H14 transforms back to the orthorhombic phase with an exothermic event at ≈ 96 ° C. Thermo-gravimetric measurements of LiB11H14 show an ≈ 3% weight loss at ≈ 180 ° C (Figure S2b, Supporting Information),  indicative  of  sample  decomposition.  Moreover, with  prolonged  heat  treatment  at  120 ° C,  the  sample  color changes from white to yellow and extra resonances in the  11 B NMR spectra appear, indicating thermal instability of LiB 11 H 14 above  the  phase  transition  (Figure  S25,  Supporting  Information).  The  decreased  signal  intensity  in  the 11 B  NMR  spectra indicates the insolubility of the decomposition products, indicative of the formation of dimers or poly-hydroborates with higher molecular masses. [48,49] The phase-transition temperature of  LiB 11 H14  at  112 ° C  is  lower  than  the  ones  of  Li 2 B10 H 10 and Li 2 B12 H 12  (367 and 355 ° C, respectively), [40,50]  and is comparable to the phase-transition temperature of LiCB 11 H 12  at ≈ 122 ° C. [44]

For  the  LiB 11 H14:Li 2 B12 H 12 (1:1,  Sample L )  mixture,  an  irreversible  endothermic  event  is  observed  at T ≈ 120 ° C  for the  first  heating  cycle.  In  the  subsequent  cycle,  this  event shifts to a lower temperature  and  fades  (Figure 4a;  and

Figure S26c, Supporting Information). We attribute this to the phase  transition  of  the  remaining  unreacted  LiB 11 H14  in  the sample  that  could  not  be  distinguished  by  PXRD  and  is  dissolving  in  the  Li 2 B12 H 12 crystal  lattice  upon  heating.  For  the LiB11 H 14 :LiCB11 H 12 (1:1)  mixtures  (Sample H ),  the  phase  transition  to  the  HT  phase  is  observed  at ≈ 80 ° C (Figure  4a;  and Figures S26 and S27, Supporting Information). In this system, the phase-transition temperature is dependent on the LiB 11 H 14 / LiCB11H12 ratio. Higher content of LiB 11 H 14  decreases the phase transition to 70 ° C for LiB 11 H14:LiCB11 H 12  (2:1, Sample J ), while the higher content of LiCB11 H 12  increases the phase transition to ≈ 110 ° C  for  LiB 11 H14:LiCB11 H 12 (1:2,  Sample I )  respectively (Figure S26d, Supporting Information).

In the mixtures LiB 11 H14:LiB11 H 13 R' (R' = OH, OB11H13Li) and LiB11 H 14:LiCB9H10, the HT phases are stabilized at room temperature  and  the  phase  transitions  are  completely  suppressed (Figure  4;  and  Figure  S26b,e,  Supporting  Information)  which is similarly observed for other mixed anion systems. [13,15-17,33,51]
