## 2.4. Conductivity

Figure 5 displays  the  temperature-dependent  lithium-ion  conductivity  of  the  synthesized  compounds.  For  comparison,  the conductivities  of  Li 2 B12 H 12 ,  LiCB 11 H12,  and  LiCB 9 H10  are  also presented. LiB 11 H14 shows a conductivity of 1.5 × 10 -6  S cm -1 at 25 ° C which is similar to the reported conductivity of LiCB 10 H 13 , but  higher  than  the  published  data  for  LiCB 9 H10,  LiCB 11 H12, and  Li 2 B12 H 12 . [14,25,44,52,53] The  apparent  activation  energy  for LiB11 H 14  is 0.81 eV (Sample E ) at low temperatures and reduces to 0.16 eV above the phase transition at ≈ 112 ° C (Figures S28a and S29, Supporting Information).

Figure 5. Arrhenius  plots  of  the  lithium-ion  conductivity  of  LiB 11 H14, Li 2 B12 H 12 , LiCB11 H 12 , LiCB9H10, and mixtures of LiB11 H14:LiB 11 H13R', LiB11 H14:Li 2 B12 H 12 (1:1),  LiB11 H14:LiCB11 H 12 (1:1),  and  LiB11 H 14 :LiCB9 H 10 (1:2). The red dash lines indicate σ = 10 -4  and 10 -3  S cm -1 .

www.afm-journal.de

www.advancedsciencenews.com

For  the  LiB 11 H14:LiCB11 H 12 solid  solutions  (Samples H-K ), the conductivity depends on the LiB11 H 14 :LiCB11 H 12 ratio (Figure  S28,  Supporting  Information).  The  conductivity  of 1.1 × 10 -4 S  cm -1 at  25 ° C is observed for the 1:1 mixture. The jump in  the  conductivity  at ≈ 80 ° C  corresponds  to  the  phase transition to the cubic HT phase (Figure S27, Supporting Information).  The  LiB 11 H14:Li 2 B12 H 12 (1:1,  Sample L )  solid  solution shows lower conductivity of ≈ 2.3 × 10 -5  S cm -1 at 25 ° C, which could  be  ascribed  to  nonpolarity  of  B 12H12 2 -and  its  bivalent charge  that  increases  the  Li  content  in  the  Li 3 (B11 H14)(B12 H 12 ) structure.

The LiB11 H 14 :LiB 11 H13R' (R' = OH and OB11H13Li, Sample A ) in  which  the  presence  of  impurities  stabilizes  the  HT  cubic phase of LiB11 H 14 ( Fm 3 ), shows  an  ionic conductivity of 1.5 × 10 -4   S  cm -1 at  25 ° C.  The  highest  ionic  conductivity of  1.1 × 10 -3   S  cm -1 within  this  system  is  obtained  for  the LiB11 H 14 :LiCB9H10  (1:2,  Sample N )  mixture  where  the  disordered  HT  hexagonal  unit  cell  of  LiCB 9 H10  is  stabilized.  The PXRD pattern of this sample still shows the presence of unreacted LiB 11 H14 and suggests tuning the LiB11 H 14 :LiCB 9 H 10 ratio to  obtain  higher  conductivity  (Figure  2a;  and  Figures  S 2 4b and  S 2 7,  Supporting  Information).  A  non-Arrhenius  conductivity  behavior  is  observed  for  both  LiB 11 H14:LiB11 H 13 R'  and LiB11 H 14 :LiCB11 H 12   samples,  where  the  HT  disordered  phases are stabilized. This is the characteristic behavior of disordered structures  in  hydroborates  and  has  been  observed  in  former studies. [13,15,17]   A  transition  from  a  low-temperature  regime with a correlated cationic motion to a high-temperature regime with noncorrelated cationic di/uniFB00usion has been suggested to be responsible  for  this  behavior. [54] The  conductivity  trends  indicate  that  small,  single  charged  anions  with  high  polarity  are required  to  form  suitable  conduction  channels  for  Li + cations and to obtain high conductivities in Li-hydroborates. Although B11 H 14 -and CB9H10 -have similar sizes and polarities (Figure 3), the prolate (elongated) shapes of the CB 9 H 10 -anion appear to be  more  favorable  for  Li + conduction  pathways  by  stabilizing the  disordered  hexagonal  unit  cell.  However,  the  synthesis  of CB9H10 -is challenging and the use of expensive/toxic reactants cannot yet be avoided. [30]  Therefore, in the following, we assess LiB11 H 14 :LiCB11 H 12 (1:1, Sample H ) as a solid electrolyte (SE) for further electrochemical measurements and battery assembly.

In inorganic solid electrolytes, the ionic conductivity is predominately attributed to cation mobility and the anion mobility is  negligible. [55] The  electronic  conductivity  of  Sample E was determined to be ≈ 5 × 10 -9   S cm -1 at  60 ° C, by measuring the current decay in a Li/SE/Cu after applying constant voltages of 0.25 to 1 V (Figure S30, Supporting Information). The obtained electronic  conductivity  is  one  order  of  magnitude  lower  than 5 × 10 -8  and 2.5 × 10 -8  S cm -1  obtained at 60 ° C for the typical solid electrolytes, Li 7 La3Zr2O12,  and Li 3 PS4, respectively. [56]

## 2.5. Electrochemical Performance

To  evaluate  the  stability  of  the  LiB 11 H14:LiCB11 H 12 (1:1)  SE against Li metal, a symmetric Li/SE/Li cell was assembled and the time evolution of the impedance spectra was measured at the  open-circuit  voltage.  Impedance  spectra  were  measured at  intervals  of  2  to  80  h,  starting  directly  after  contacting  the

Adv. Funct. Mater. 2021 , 2010046

www.afm-journal.de

Li metal with the SE to evaluate the interphase formation rate and its impact on the cell resistance over time. As depicted in Figure 6 a, the evolution of the impedance was extracted using an  equivalent  circuit  consisting  of  a  constant  phase  element and two resistors representing the bulk and interphase resistances. Figure 6a also shows the Nyquist plots recorded at t = 6, 20, 40, 60, and 80 h. The extracted bulk and interphase resistances  are  presented  in  Figure  6b,c.  Similar  to  sulfide-based solid electrolytes such as Li 10 GeP2S12 and Li3PS4, our SE is not stable against Li metal and slowly decomposes over time. [57]  The growth rate of the interphase obeys a parabolic rate law with a rate constant of k = 14.6 Ω cm 2  h -0.5  obtained from the slope in Figure 6c, which is lower than the 45.1 and 1394.3 Ω cm 2   h -0.5 reported  for  Li 10 GeP2S12 and Li6PS5I, respectively. [58]   However, this  value  is  slightly  higher  than  2.9  and  3.8 Ω cm 2   h -0.5 published  for  Li 7 P3S11   and  Li 6 PS5Cl,  respectively. [58] The  initial interphase resistance immediately after contacting the SE with Li metal is also calculated as 29.6 Ω cm 2   from the intercept in Figure 6c.

As  previous  studies  have  confirmed  the  stability  of  the CB11H12 -anion  against Li/Na  metal, [18,19] we  attribute the decomposition  reaction  on  the  anode  side  to  the  reduction of  the  B 11H14 -anion  in  the  electrolyte.  To  get  a  better  insight into possible decomposition reactions at the anode and cathode,  we  performed  electrochemical  stability  calculations for  LiB 11H14 [59,60] (for  details  of  the  present  approach,  see  the DFT  section  in  the  Supporting  Information  and  Figure  S33 and Table S5, Supporting Information). DFT calculations show that LiB 11 H14 is unstable against Li metal, reducing to Li 2 B 11 H13 (dashed rectangle in Figure 6f; and Table S5, Supporting Information).  This  reduction  reaction  requires  the  detachment  of one  proton  and  was  also  observed  during  the  synthesis  steps and when (CH3)3NHB11H14 was reacted with an excess amount of BuLi as a Li source (Figure 1c).

Although LiB11H14  appears  to  be  unstable  against  Li  metal, the SE/Li interphase shows su/uniFB03cient ionic conductivity to support reversible electrodeposition as shown by the galvanostatic cycling of a symmetric Li/SE/Li cell. The galvanostatic cycling experiment (Figure 6d) reveals the excellent stability of &gt; 620 h at a current density of 25 or 50 µ A cm -2 , switching the current direction every 1 or 10 h (0.50 mA cm -2 ).  A moderate increase in the overpotential from 22.5 to 30 mV after 620 h might be attributed  to  continuous  and  slow  electrolyte  reduction  at  the Li/SE interphase due to cracking of the formed Li 2 B 11 H 13  layer during electrodeposition. The critical current density (CCD) at which dendrites start  to  propagate  through  the  SE  was  measured by increasing the current density in each cycle in a Li/SE/ Li cell while keeping the transferred capacity per half cycle constant  at  0.2  mAh  cm -2 (Figure  S31,  Supporting  Information). Using this method, a CCD of 0.16 mA cm -2 at  60 ° C is determined.  Similar  CCD  values  of  0.04-0.28  mA  cm -2 have  been recently  reported  for  single-crystalline  Li 6.4 Ga0.2 La 3 Zr2O12  at ambient temperature. [61]  Higher critical currents beyond 1 mA cm -2 typically  require  interface  engineering  to  lower  the  interfacial resistance and external pressure in the MPa range applied on the cell to counteract void formation at the interface between Li metal and the electrolyte. [62-64]

In  the  next  step,  we  experimentally  determined  the  oxidative  stability  of  the  SE  following  the  methodology  of  Asakura

www.advancedsciencenews.com

www.afm-journal.de

Figure 6. a) Nyquist plots and their equivalent circuit (fits) measured after 6-80 h of contacting the LiB 11 H 14 :LiCB 11 H12 (1:1) SE with Li metal. Solid electrolyte interface (SEI) and bulk resistances plotted versus b) time and c) square root of time to determine a parabolic rate constant. d) Galvanostatic cycling of a symmetric Li/SE/Li cell at current densities of 25 or 50 µ A cm -2  (for 1 or 10 h in each direction). e) Voltammogram of a Li/SE/SE + C/Pt cell from 1.5 to 4.0 V versus Li + /Li at a scan rate of 0.05 mV s -1 . f ) Calculated electrochemical stability of selected hydroborate anions. LiB 11 H14 only appears as a stable compound in DFT calculations under specific assumptions (dashed rectangular, see Table S5, Supporting Information). The electrochemical stability of the other compounds was calculated with and without consideration of LiB 21 H 18  as a final decomposition product in red and gray bars, respectively. g) Cycling performance and Coulombic e/uniFB03ciency of a Li/SE/TiS 2 cell cycled between 1.6 and 2.5   V versus Li + /Li at C/10 (1st to 5th cycle) and C/5 (from 6th cyle) (1C = 0.239 mA g -1 ) under 3.2 MPa. All measurements were performed at 60 ° C.

et  al.  by  linear  sweep  voltammetry. [65] The  voltage  of  a  SE/ carbon  composite  working  electrode  was  swept  against  a  Li metal reference/counter electrode from 2.0 to 4.0 V versus Li + / Li, as shown in Figure 6e. Similar to NaB 11 H 14 , [13] a clear onset of decomposition reactions is observed at ≈ 2.6 V versus Li + /Li followed by a second onset at 3.2 V versus Li + /Li. In the subsequent cycles, no significant current up to 4.0 V versus Li + /Li is observed during  both  forward  and  backward  scans  (see  Figure  S32, Supporting  Information),  indicating  the  irreversibility  of  the decomposition  products  and  the  formation  of  a  passivating, blocking layer during the first cycle.

DFT  calculations  were  used  to  predict  the  electrochemical stability  of  the  LiB 11 H14  together  with  other  impurity  compounds  ( 2 , 3 , 5 ).  It  should  be  noted  that  the  calculated  electrochemical  stability  windows  of  the  considered  compounds

Adv. Funct. Mater. 2021 , 2010046

depend  on  the  selected  decomposition  products.  Upon  oxidation,  electrolytes  are  typically  delithiated.  In  this  study,  therefore, several poly-hydroborates with lower lithium content such as LiB 22 H22, LiB21 H 18 , LiB 20 H18, and B18 H 22  are considered (see Table S5 and Figure S33 for the complete list, Supporting Information). [66] A B22 H 22 2 -compound was reported as the oxidation product of the B11 H 14 -anion in solution [67]   and trace amounts of B 21H18 -were observed in the MS data of Sample B (Table S2, Supporting  Information).  DFT  calculations  predict  a  narrow electrochemical window for LiB11H14 (dashed bar in Figure 6f) when  only  B18H22  as  a  completely  delithiated  and  neutral borane  is  considered  as  the  oxidation  products  (Figure  S33 and Table S5, Supporting Information). The calculated stability window of LiB11H14 is comparable to Li 3 PS 4 [68] and is still larger than  the  calculated  values  for  Li 10 GeP2S12. [59,69] The  list  of  the

www.advancedsciencenews.com electrochemical  stability  window  of  the  most  common  solid electrolytes and their phase equilibria at their stability limits are presented in Table S6 (Supporting Information).

The electrochemical stability windows of the rest of the compounds were calculated with and without considering LiB21H18 as  a  decomposition  product  (red  and  gray  bars  in  Figure  6f). DFT calculations suggest LiB21 H 18  as the most thermodynamically stable compound among the hydroborates considered here and can be perceived as the thermodynamic sink. It means that this  compound tends to form as the oxidation product and is stable up to ≈ 5 V versus Li + /Li before completely delithiating to B18 H 22 (red bars in Figure 6f).

A second series of calculations was carried out without considering the LiB 21 H18 as the decomposition product in order to stimulate  that  the  formation  of  this  compound  is  kinetically hindered.  Under  these  assumptions,  Li 2 B 20 H18  and  Li 2B22H22 are formed as the oxidation products and are stable up to ≈ 5 V versus Li + /Li (gray bars).

In general, the B 11H14 -cage is prone to be oxidized by losing protons at the open side of the cage and subsequently forming poly-hydroborates.  The  same  reaction  is  observed  in  aqueous media by replacing protons at the open side of the cage with an  oxygen  (compound 5 )  or  a  hydroxyl  group  (compound 2 ). Calculated  electrochemical  stability  of  these  compounds  ( 2 , 3 , 5 )  implies  a  wider  electrochemical  stability  window  compared to LiB 11 H14 (Figure 6f) and points toward a new strategy to synthesize  them as a pure phase for the next generation of solid electrolytes.

The  electronic  conductivity  of  the  decomposition  products could be estimated by the following measurement. The Li/SE/ Cu cell assembled for the electronic conductivity measurement shows an open-circuit voltage of ≈ 2.3 V. To measure the current response versus applied potential, constant voltages of 0.25, 0.5, 0.75, and 1 V were applied to the cell. When a constant voltage of ≥ 0.5 V is applied, the cell potential increases to &gt; 2.6 V versus Li + /Li, which is above the electrochemical stability, and the SE starts to decompose. In these cases, smaller current responses are  observed,  suggesting  the  lower  electronic  conductivities of  the  decomposition products as a preferred requirement for the formation of a stable interphase (Figure S30b, Supporting Information).

Finally, a proof-of-concept battery using a Li metal anode and TiS2 cathode was assembled in a home-built pressure cell. TiS 2 was selected as a cathode active material because of its lithium intercalation  potential  of ≤ 2.5  V  versus  Li + /Li  matches  the oxidative  stability  limit  of  the  SE.  The  chemical  compatibility between the SE and the active material was further confirmed with  PXRD.  Figure  S34  (Supporting  Information)  compares the  PXRD  patterns  of  the  TiS 2 ,  SE,  and  the  SE/TiS 2  composite mixture annealed at 60 ° C for 12 h (under vacuum, p = 10 -3  mbar). Unlike LiBH4 that is unstable against TiS 2 , leading to spontaneous lithiation upon heating with a shift of the PXRD patterns to lower 2 θ angles, [70]  no apparent reaction is observed between the SE and TiS2 by PXRD. No shift of the PXRD patterns  and  formation  of  crystalline  decomposition  products are  detected.  Galvanostatic  cycling  of  the  cell  at  C/10  and  C/5 (1C = 239 mA g -1 ) under 3.2 MPa in Figure 6g shows a reversible  discharge  capacity  of  190  mAh  g -1 in  the  first  cycle,  corresponding to a TiS2 utilization ratio of 80% compared to the

Adv. Funct. Mater. 2021 , 2010046

www.afm-journal.de theoretical capacity of 239 mAh g -1 . [71]  Charge/discharge voltage profiles are presented in Figure S35 (Supporting Information). The Coulombic e/uniFB03ciency  is  as  high  as  98%  in  the  first  cycle, which stays at &gt; 99.5% in the later cycles. Switching C-rates to C/5  in  the  sixth  cycle  exhibits  a  minor  decrease  in  discharge capacity (188 mAh g -1 ). The reversible discharge capacity at C/5 retained 82% after 150 cycles (from 6th cycle), indicating stable battery cycling with a negligible oxidative decomposition of the SE. The slow cell degradation over cycling is attributed to the contact  loss  between  the  SE  and  the  cathode  active  material together with the slow reductive decomposition of the electrolyte by the Li metal anode.

Improvements in battery performance, as well as the use of high voltage cathodes, could be made by using coated cathodes. This is a common approach for using sulfide-based solid electrolytes beyond their stability window and in combination with high-voltage  cathodes.  Summary  lists  of  the  electrochemical stability  window  of  the  most  common  solid  electrolytes  and their  performances  in  all-solid-state  lithium  batteries  are  presented in Tables S6 and S7 (Supporting Information). [68,72]

## 3. Conclusion

In this work, we present for the first time, the synthesis of anhydrous LiB11 H 14 as a cost-e/uniFB00ective building block for mixed-anion hydroborate lithium solid electrolytes. The impurity side products  such  as  LiB 11 H13OH, Li2(B11H13-O-B11H13), Li(B 11 H 13 -O-Bu), and LiB11 H 12O were thoroughly characterized by MS and NMR, and  high  purity  LiB 11 H14  was  obtained  by  excess  use  of  Li source in the synthesis.

Disordered  structures  were  obtained  by  partially  replacing LiB11 H 14   with  carbacloso -hydroborates.  Ionic  conductivity  of 1.1 × 10 -4 S  cm -1 at  25 ° C is  achieved  for  Li 2 (B11 H14)(CB11 H 12 ) and liquid-like ionic conductivity of up to 1.1 × 10 -3 S  cm -1 at 25 ° C is achieved in a mixture with slightly smaller LiCB 9 H 10 in the Li 3 (B11H14)(CB9H10)2. Therefore, contrary to the common trend that focuses on increasing the anion size and widening the ion conduction path, we propose focusing on small hydroborate anions which are more compatible with the small size of the Li + cation and enable suitable conduction channels.

LiB11 H 14 is  not  stable  against  Li  metal;  however,  the  formation  of  the  Li-rich  Li 2 B11 H 13 allows  electrodeposition  in  a symmetric cell  with  Li  metal  electrodes  at  50 µ A cm -2 (up  to 0.5 mAh cm -2 ) with a low overpotential increase which is stable over &gt; 620 cycles. The oxidative stability of 2.6 V versus Li + /Li is obtained for this electrolyte is similar to Li 3 PS4  [68] but  is  narrower  than  that  for closo -hydroborates  due  to  the  delocalized hydrogen atoms. [16,17]

In order to achieve high gravimetric energy densities, cathode materials such as Li 2 C 6 O 6 [73] or  sulfur [18] with a theoretical capacity of 590 and 1675 mAh g -1 ,  respectively, are suggested,  which  lie  within  the  stability  window.  In  this  regard, we  assembled  a  proof-of-concept  battery  using  TiS 2   cathode which  matches  the  oxidative  stability  of  the  electrolyte.  The other  common  strategy  is  to  use  protective  coatings  together with  high-voltage  cathodes  as  a  common  method  for  sulfidebased ASSBs. [68,72] Finally,  the  intrinsic  stability  of  the  electrolyte  could  be  improved  by  replacing  hydrogen  atoms  with  an

www.advancedsciencenews.com

O atom or an OH group as suggested by DFT calculations or closing the cage with Sn and P form SnB11H11 2 -and PB11 H 11 -compounds. [74,75] Therefore, LiB 11 H14 as a cost-e/uniFB00ective building block  opens  a  new  chapter  for  designing  novel  hydroboratebased solid electrolytes for all-solid-state lithium batteries.

[CCDC  2 043 896,  20 438 972 043 898,  2 043 899,  2 043 900 contain the supplementary crystallographic data for this paper. These  data  can  be  obtained  free  of  charge  from  The  Cambridge  Crystallographic  Data  Centre  via  www.ccdc.cam.ac.uk/ data\_request/cif.]

Nido -Hydroborate-Based Electrolytes for All-Solid-State Lithium Batteries

SeyedHosein Payandeh,* Daniel Rentsch, Zbigniew Łodziana,* Ryo Asakura, Laurent Bigler, Radovan Černý, Corsin Battaglia, and Arndt Remhof*

## Nido -Hydroborate-based Electrolytes for All-Solid-State Lithium Batteries

SeyedHosein Payandeh*, Daniel Rentsch, Zbigniew Łodziana*, Ryo Asakura, Laurent Bigler, Radovan Černý, Corsin Battaglia, and Arndt Remhof*

Keywords: nido -hydroborate, lithium tetradecahydroundecaborate, LiB11H14, solid-state electrolyte, hydroborate, solid-state battery, electrolyte stability

## Materials and Methods Chemicals

Indium foil with a thickness of 0.1 mm (≥99.995%), n -butyllithium (BuLi, LiC4H9, 1.6 M in Hexane), lithium hydroxide (LiOH, 98%) tetrahydrofuran (THF) anhydrous (99.9%), titanium (IV) sulfide (TiS2, 200 mesh, 99.9%) were purchased from Sigma-Aldrich (St. Luis, USA). Acetronrile-d3,  THF-d8  were  obtained  from  ReseaChem  (Burgdorf,  Switzerland),  carbon black  (Super  C65)  from  Imerys  Graphite  &amp;  Carbon  Company  (Bironico,  Switzerland). LiB11H14(H2O)n (1 &lt; n &lt; 2, ~97%), Li2B12H12(H2O)4 (&gt;98%), LiCB11H12(H2O)5.0  (&gt;98%),, LiCB9H10(H2O) (&gt;98%), and (CH3)3NHB11H14 (~97%) from Katchem spol. s r. o. (Kralupy and Vltavou, Czech Republic). Preparation and manipulation of the chemicals and samples were performed in an argon-filled glovebox ( p (H2O and O2) &lt; 1 ppm) (MBraun, Garching, Germany).

## Sample preparation Sample A

The  as purchased LiB11H14(H2O)n  (1 &lt; n &lt; 2, based on weight loss observed by thermogravimetric analysis, see Figure.S2) (100-500 mg), was transferred to a glass vial and heated for 2h at 100  C under vacuum on a Schlenk line ( p = 10 -3 -10 -2 mbar). After cooling the molten LiB11H14(H2O)n the solid was ground in a glove box by mortar and pestle and the heating process repeated for a second time.

## Sample B

15 mL of Milli-Q water was added to 150 mg of the as purchased LiB11H14(H2O)n (1 &lt; n &lt; 2). The solvent was initially removed using a rotary evaporator at 90  C ( p = 50-100 mbar) and then by heat treatment for 2 h at 90  C under vacuum on a Schlenk line ( p = 10 -3 -10 -2 mbar).

## Samples C, D

For Sample C , 5 mL of THF was added to 50 mg of the as purchased LiB11H14(H2O)n (1 &lt; n &lt; 2). The solvent initially was removed using a rotary evaporator at 90  C  for 1 h ( p = 50-100 mbar) and then by heat treatment for 1h at 100  C under vacuum using a Schlenk line ( p = 10 -3 -10 -2 mbar).

For Sample D, 3 mL of THF-d8 was added to 100 mg of LiB11H14(H2O)n (1 &lt; n &lt; 2) and the sample directly dried under vacuum using a Schlenk line at 100  C for 4 h.

## Sample E

500 mg of the as purchased LiB11H14(H2O)n (for 1 &lt; n &lt; 2: 3.16 mmol to 2.18 mmol of 1 ), 18.9 mg of LiOH (0.79 mmol) and 50 mL of Milli-Q water were transferred to a round bottom glass flask and stirred for 30 min at room temperature (RT) until everything was dissolved. To separate LiB11H14 from LiOH, 20-40 mL of THF were added to the powder in the glovebox, dissolving the desired product only. The insoluble LiOH was separated by centrifugation at 4000 rpm for 10 minutes using falcon tubes loaded in the glovebox.

The  clear  solution  containing  clean  LiB11H14  was  transferred  to  a  glass  flask  under  inert atmosphere and THF was removed using a rotary evaporator at 80  C ( p =  50  -  10  mbar).

Finally, the coordinating THF was removed by annealing the powder at 100  C for 16 h under vacuum using a Schlenk line ( p = 10 -3 -10 -2 mbar).

## LiB11H14 from (CH3)3NHB11H14 (Samples F, G)

(CH3)3NHB11H14 can be synthesized based on cheap reactants as was described in Ref. [1,2] and in this  study  was  purchased  from  Katchem. For Sample G ,  (CH3)3NHB11H14 (400 mg, 2.07  mmol) and 15 mL of anhydrous THF were transferred to a round bottom glass flask, sealed with a rubber septum in the glovebox and moved to a Schlenk line.

The reaction flask was connected to argon flow via a Schlenk line and BuLi (2.3 mL, 3.72 mmol) was added under inert atmosphere with a syringe through the septum. The suspension was stirred at room temperature for 1-2 h under argon forming a mixture of Li2B11H13 and LiB11H14. Subsequently, the reaction mixture was transferred to a rotary evaporator and the solvent was distilled off at 50-60 °C under reduced pressure of 250-350 mbar. 20 mL of MilliQ water was added to the remaining solid and the solution was centrifuged at 4000 rpm for 10 minutes  to  separate  the  solids  (possibly  unreacted  (CH3)3NHB11H14)  from  the  liquid.  The water was then removed at 80  C at a reduced pressure of 50 mbar on a rotary evaporator followed by a two h heat treatment at 90  C under vacuum using a Schlenk line ( p = 10 -3 -10 -2 mbar) resulting in a white solid when cooled to RT. In this step, Li2B11H13 transformed into LiB11H14 and LiOH. LiOH was removed using the same procedure as described for Sample E . Sample F was  synthesized  in  the  same  way  as  Sample G but  with  a  different  ratio  of  the reactants (Table.S1).

## Mixtures (Samples H-N)

LiCB11H12(H2O)5.0  and  LiCB9H10(H2O)  were  dried  for  12h  at  180  C  and  Li2B12H12(H2O)4 was dried under vacuum ( p = 10 -3 -10 -2 mbar) for 14h at 240  C before use. Powder mixtures of  LiB11H14,  Li2B12H12,  LiCB11H12  were  ball-milled  with  a  high  energy  ball  mill  (8000M Mixer/Miller, SPEX SamplePrep, LLC, Metuchen, USA) in the molar ratios of 1:1, 2:1, and 1:2 for 15 minutes in 5 repetitions using stainless steel vials and balls (5 mm) with balls to sample ratio of 15-17:1. For Sample K , a ball to powder ratio of 40:1 was used to investigate the harsher ball milling condition on the conductivity of the samples. Preparation methods and compositions of all samples are listed in Table.S1).

## Analytical Methods Mass spectrometry

The  electrospray  ionization  mass  spectra  (ESI-MS)  were  recorded  on  a  timsTOF  Pro quadrupole time-of-flight mass spectrometer (Bruker Daltonics Inc., Bremen, Germany). The NMR samples were diluted in methanol or acetonitrile at concentrations of 10-20 µL mL -1 and transferred  to  the  ESI  source  with  a  syringe  pump  at  a  flow  rate  of  3  µL  min -1 .  Mass spectra were acquired in the (-)-ESI mode, with the TIMS unit switched off, and over a mass range of m/z 20-1300. The ion source parameters were: 2.2 bar nebulizer pressure, 220 °C dry heater temperature, 10.0 L min -1 dry gas, 3600 V capillary voltage, and 500 V capillary offset. Calibration was achieved by measuring the Agilent ESI-L low concentration tuning mix. The mass  accuracy  was  better  than  2  ppm  and  the  resolution  above  20'000  at m / z of  302.  The Bruker Compass DataAnalysis vers. 5.2 software was used for data processing and calculation of theoretical isotopic distributions.

## Nuclear Magnetic Resonance (NMR)

The 1 H, 2 H, 11 B, and 13 C NMR data were recorded at 298 K at 400.1, 61.4, 128.4, and 100.6 MHz,  respectively  on  a  Bruker  Avance  III  400  NMR  spectrometer  (Bruker  Biospin  AG, Fällanden, Switzerland). The 1D NMR spectra of acetonitrile solutions, as well as the 2D 1 H11 B HSQC (gradient version with 80.0:25.7% gradient strengths and selection of 140 Hz 1 H11 B coupling) and the 11 B11 B COSY (with 1 H decoupling during the whole pulse sequence) NMR experiments, were recorded using the Bruker standard pulse programs and parameter sets  on  a  5  mm  CryoProbe™  Prodigy  probe  with  z-gradient  applying  90°  pulse  lengths  of 11.4  s  ( 1 H), 300  s  ( 2 H, data recorded on shim coil), 14.9  s  ( 11 B) and 10.0  s  ( 13 C). An adapted version of the Bruker DQF-COSY experiment with gradient selection applying 11 B decoupling during the whole  pulse sequence was  applied for the 1 H- 1 H  correlation experiments. The 1 H and 13 C NMR chemical shifts (δ) in ppm are calibrated to the resonance of acetonitrile-d3 at 1.93 ppm and 1.3 ppm, respectively; δ 11 B was calibrated to an external sample of 35% BF3 diethyl etherate in CDCl3 solution at 0.0 ppm. Please note that for the following assignments of 1 H, 11 B, and 13 C NMR data, the information from Samples B , C , D, and F is combined. Several minor deviations in chemical shifts up to  1 H ≈ ± 0.1 and  11 B ≈ ± 0.3 ppm were observed depending on sample concentration and composition, respectively. The numbering of positions used for the NMR assignments of 1 -5 is shown in Figure.1a (see the main text) and in all figures showing NMR data.

LiB11H14 (1) : 1 H NMR (CD3CN, 400.1 MHz):  1.87 (1H, H-7); 1.67 (5H, H-2 to 4); 1.23 (5H, H-5 to 7), -3.64 (3H, Hbr). 11 B NMR (CD3CN, 128.4 MHz):  -14.3 (1B, B-7); -16.1 (5B, B-2 to B-4); -16.8 (5B, B-5 to B-7).

LiB11H13-OH (2) : 1 H NMR (CD3CN, 400.1 MHz):  4.00 (1H, OH, corr. to Hbr); 2.05 (2H, H-5, corr. to H-3, 4 &amp; Hbr); 1.87 (1H, H-1, corr. to H-2, 3, 4); 1.53 (2H, H-3, corr. to H-1, 2, 4, 5, 6 &amp; Hbr); 1.12 (2H, H-2, corr. to H-1, 3, 6); 0.92 (2H, H-6, corr. to H-2, 3 &amp; Hbr); -0.09 (1H, H-4, corr. to 1, 3, 5); -2.21 (3H, Hbr, corr. to H- 3, 5, 6 &amp; OH). In the 1 H- 11 B HSQC NMR

experiment correlations of protons to directly attached borons were observed and, additionally, correlations  from  the  bridging  protons  Hbr  to  borons  B-5,  6,  7  were  detected. 11 B  NMR (CD3CN, 128.4 MHz):  16.8 (1B, B-7, corr. to B-2); -10.2 (2B, B-5, corr. to B-3, 4, 6); -11.3 (2B, B-3, corr. to B-1, 2, 4, 5, 6); -14.3 (1B, B-1, corr. to B-2, 3, 4); -22.6 (2B, B-2, corr. to B-1, 3, 6, 7); -28.4 (2B, B-6, corr. to B-2, 3, 5); -38.5 (1B, B-4, corr. to B-1, 3, 5).

6

## Powder X-ray diffraction

Powder  X-ray  diffraction  (PXRD)  data  were  measured  ( T =  20-25  C)  on  a  PANalytical Empyrean  powder  diffractometer  with  a  X'celerator  detector  (Malvern  Panalytical  Ltd, Malvern, UK). Temperature dependent powder  X-ray diffraction measurements were performed  on  PANalytical  X'Pert  Powder  diffractometer  with  a  pixel  detector  ((Malvern Panalytical Ltd, Malvern, UK) and equipped with a MRI furnace (MRI Physikalische Geräte GmbH,  Tübingen,  Germany).  All  the  samples  were  mounted  in  boron  silicate  capillaries (Outer  diameter  of  0.5  -  0.7  mm)  and  sealed  with  grease  prior  to  the  measurement.  All measurements were carried out in the 2θ range of 5-90° using transmission geometry and a focusing mirror was used to remove K  .

## Structure solution and refinement

The crystal structures were solved with Fox program and using global optimization methods. The B11H14 -, B12H12 2and CB11H12 -anions were treated as rigid bodies. In the next step, the structures were refined using TOPAS V6.

## FT-IR

Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FT-IR) analysis of the samples were performed in an argon-filled  glovebox  using  a  Bruker  Alpha  spectrometer  (Bruker  Daltonics  Inc.,  Bremen, Germany) averaging 24 scans for each spectrum within 400-4000 cm -1 at  a  resolution  of  4 cm -1

## Density functional theory

Density  functional  theory  (DFT)  calculations  were  performed  within  the  plane-wave  DFT method implemented in the Vienna ab initio Simulation Package (VASP). [5] The atoms were represented with projected  augmented  wave  potentials [6,7] with the valence electron configurations (1s 1 )  for  H,  (2s 2 2p 1 )  for  B,  (2s 2 2p 2 )  for  C,  and  (1s 2 2s 1 )  for  Li.  The  gradientcorrected (GGA) exchange-correlation functional and the non-local correction accounting for weak dispersive interactions were applied, [7,8] the cutoff energy for the plane wave expansion was 700 eV. [9]

The  experimental  crystal  structure  of  LiB11H14  was  optimized  with  the  conjugate  gradient method  with  respect  to  the  internal  atomic  positions,  unit  cell  volume  and  shape.  The symmetry constraints were imposed in the optimization procedure. Removing the symmetry constraints for the optimized system did not result in any space group, symmetry or structural changes.  The  calculations  for  isolated  anions  and  complexes  were  performed  within  an orthorhombic cell ( a ≠ b ≠ c ) with at least 8 Å of vacuum separating periodic images of the molecules.  For  charged  anions,  the  background  charge  was  added  to  maintain  charge neutrality  of  the  supercell.  All  configurations  of  isolated  molecules  optimized  the  atomic positions  with  convergence  criteria  10 -4 eV/atom,  single  k-point  sampling  was  used  for molecular systems.

The charge density analysis was performed using the Bader method [10,11] on a charge density grid  calculated  with  spacing  0.05  Å  or  smaller  for  all  relevant  crystalline  and  molecular species. In order to grasp effects related to the charge distribution on the anions and possible charge redistribution due to Li diffusion, we used a simplified model with an isolated anion

surrounded by lithium cations to maintain charge neutrality. For each anion/cation combination, at least four different configurations were created and geometry optimization of each configuration was performed. This procedure provides potential energies related to the local  minima  of  the  anion/cation  arrangements.  For  the  charge  distribution  analysis,  the configurations with the lowest and the highest total energy were considered for each anion. The  charge  distribution  calculated  for  the  low-temperature  structure  of  LiB11H14  gives  an average  dipole  moment  of  0.75e  for  B11H14 -which  is  close  to  the  one  calculated  for  the isolated system of 0.85e. The difference in charge distribution between periodic and isolated systems is considerably lower than the differences related to anion polarization triggered by cation proximity.

The  electrochemical  stability  windows  were  calculated  from  the  grand  chemical  potential phase diagrams. [12,13] The stability window is given by the range of chemical potentials over which a given electrolyte  is  stable  against  decomposition  to  compounds  considered  for  the construction of grand potential. For the crystalline phases, we have considered the structure of LiB11H14  determined  in  this  work  together  with  the  elements  B,  Li,  and  H2,  binary compounds:  LiH,  LiB,  and  B2H6,  and  other  lithium  hydroborates  LiB3H8,  LiBH4,  and Li2B11H13. All crystal structures were based on the ICSD database. [14] The grand potential is related to thermodynamic stability, and in principle any kinetic limitations are not considered. This is a fairly good assumption for oxides and sulfides [12,15] but shall be applied with caution for hydroborates. In previous studies of the electrochemical stability windows for hydroborate-based  solid  electrolytes, [15,16] only  a  few  neutral  polyhedral  boranes  were considered: that include neutral molecules like  B13H19 (#301, ICSD record), B20H26 (#9781), B10H14  (#9152,  #31566),  B16H20  (#26238),  or  B18H22  (#15802,  15823,  43255.  The  latter compound was found as decomposition product for Li2/Na2B12H12 oxidation at the cathode and is often denoted as B9H11, [15,16] hiding its macropolyhedral nature).

In the present analysis, we extend the list of possible decomposition products of nido / closo -hydroborates  toward  anionic  systems.  The  general  idea  of  our  approach  is  illustrated  in Figure.S32  and  can  be  summarized  as  (i)  conversion  between nidoor closo -hydroborates (B10H10 2↔  B12H12 2;  B11H14 -↔  B12H12 2;  etc.)  is  not  allowed  due  to  kinetic  limitation otherwise  only  B12H12 2anion  will  be  observed.  (ii)  The  oxidation  of  anions  leads  to  their agglomeration and the formation of neutral or charged molecules is possible. (iii) Reduction of anions at the anode leads to the formation of simpler hydroborate molecules (BH4 -, etc.) or might  drive  (reversible)  detachment/attachment  of  protons  altering  the  charge  state  of  the anion. Details of the method will be presented elsewhere. [17] For the purpose of the present analysis,  we  have  considered  the  following  macropolyhedral  anions:  Li2B20H18,  LiB21H18, Li2B22H22, and LiB19H22 [18-21] as  well  as  molecules  with  incorporated oxygen:  B20H26O [22] LiB11H12O,  LiB11H12OH,  Li2B22H26O,  and  LiB21H18O. [22] The  structure  of  LiB11H12O, LiB11H12OH, Li2B22H26O was determined by replacing H atom with OH group, two H atoms with one oxygen atom. For each stoichiometry, 5 different substitution sites were considered and  the  lowest  energy  structure  was  used  for  the  stability  calculations  (Figure.S18).  For anion/cation combinations 4 different initial configurations were considered.

For agglomerate polyhydroborates, several isomers, denoted as cis -, trans -, iso -, usually exist [19] and  all  polymorphs  were  considered  for  each  molecule  in  combination  with  counter-

cations, where applicable. The lowest energy structure was taken into consideration for the electrochemical stability calculations.

Since the crystalline phases for the majority of macro-polyhedral hydroborates are unknown (with exception of neutral molecules and CsB21H18, [23] KB21H18 [19] ), we applied the following approximation:  instead  of  searching  for  the  crystalline  structures,  the  anion-cation  (A-C) combinations were considered as isolated molecular species. For all systems, we calculated the ground state energy for A-C and (A-C)2. Combination of two molecular units for these ionic systems lowers the energy by 0.6 - 0.9 eV/molecule. As the reference for elemental and binary compounds, the following structures were used: Li12, [24] B12, [25] (H2O)4, [26] (LiBH4)4, [27] (LiH)4 [25] (Reference  systems  consisting  of  small  clusters  are  more  realistic  than  isolated atoms/ small molecules. For example, formation enthalpy of B12 cluster is only 20% lower than the hexagonal structure of α - boron).

Using  our  approach  and  considering  B18H22  as  the  decomposition  product,  the  oxidative stability  limit  of  +4.58 V  vs  Li + /Li  is  obtained  for  Li2B12H12. Under these assumptions, the oxidative  stability  limit  for  the  crystalline  structure  (bulk)  of  Li2B12H12  is  calculated  as +4.64 V vs. Li + /Li is obtained for the crystalline structure (bulk). However, once the anionic polyboranes are allowed as decomposition products the oxidative stability limit for Li2B12H12 drops  to  +3.08  V.  The  oxidative  stabilities  calculated  based  on  this  method  lie  within  the observed experimental results confirming the reliable accuracy of our method. [28-30]

## Thermal analysis

The phase transitions and the decomposition of the samples were analyzed by using Netzsch STA449 F3 Jupiter thermal gravimetric  analyzer coupled  with  mass  spectroscopy  allowing combined  differential  scanning  calorimetry  (DSC),  thermogravimetric  analysis  (TGA),  and Mass spectroscopy (MS) (Erich Netzsch GmbH &amp; Co. Holding KG, Selb, Germany) Five to ten mg of the samples were mounted in sealed Al crucibles, and a small hole was punched in the  crucible  immediately  before  measurement  allowing  the  release  of  evolved  gas.  Two programs of (RT  -30  C  150  C  -30  C  3 times) and (RT  550  C) were used for investigating the reversible phase transitions and decomposition reactions. All the measurements were carried out under helium flow of 20 mL min -1 and with a heating/cooling rate of 5  C min -1 .

## Li-ion conductivity

The  Li-ion  conductivities  were  measured  using  a  Concept  40  Novocontrol  impedance analyzer  equipped  with  Quatro  Cryosystem  (Novocontrol  Technologies  GmbH  &amp;  Co.  KG, Montabauer, Germany). The samples were measured in the frequency range from 10 MHz to 1 Hz and voltage amplitude of 50 mV.

Powder samples (~100 mg) were compressed into pellets with thicknesses of ~0.6-1.0 mm and diameters of 6-12 mm using an axial hydraulic press applying a pressure of ~150-200 MPa. The samples' pellets were sandwiched between indium foil (4-12 mm) to improve the contact with the electrodes and mounted in an airtight cell with brass electrodes (Novocontrol BDS1308).  Impedance  measurement  were  performed  in  the  range  of  -20  to  120  C  with intervals of 10  C. At low temperatures, (R1Q1)(R2Q2)Q3 equivalent circuits was implemented  to  fit  the  conductivity  data  in  which  R1  and  R2  correspond  to  bulk  and

interphase  contributions  and  Q  corresponds  to  the  electrode  contribution.  For  the  LiB11H14 sample,  R2  corresponds  to  bulk  conductivity  as  well.  At  elevated  temperatures,  the  semicircles were not clearly discernible and the pellet resistance R, was extracted at the intercept of linear spikes (tail) with the x-axis in the Nyquist plots.

## Electronic conductivity

For measuring the electronic conductivity of the solid electrolyte (LiB11H14:LiCB11H12 (1:1), Sample H ,  denoted as SE), a  Li/SE/Cu cell was assembled. DC voltages of 0.25 V to 1 V were applied for 24 h to the cell and the current response was measured. We assume that the equilibrium current after 24 h is purely electronic. In between the measurements, the cell was rested for 24 h to reestablish the open-circuit voltage (OCV). All electrochemical measurements were conducted using the same  BioLogic VMP3 multi-channel potentiostats (BioLogic, Seyssinet-Pariset, France).

## Stability versus Li metal

For  estimating  the  stability  of  the  SE  against  Li  metal,  a  symmetric  Li/SE/Li  cell  was assembled in a Swagelock cell and the impedance of the cell was measured every 2 h in the frequency range of 1 MHz to 1 Hz with a voltage amplitude of 50 mV, while the cell was kept at OCV.

## Oxidative stability

Oxidative stability of the SE was determined using the method described in Ref. [29] . Initially SE and carbon black (Super C65) were hand-mixed in a weight ratio of 75:25 for 15 min, using  mortar  and  pestle  to  form  a  carbon  composite  serving  as  a  working  electrode. Subsequently, a two-layer pellet was formed by uniaxially pressing ~5 mg of the composite and  100  mg  of  the  SE  under  a  pressure  of  170  MPa.  Finally,  an  asymmetric  cell (Li/SE/SE+C/Pt)  was  assembled  by  placing  the  12  mm-diameter  pellet  between  a  12  mmdiameter Li metal foil (as a reference/counter electrode) and Pt current collector. A piece of Al foil was used to improve the contact of the Pt current collector with the stainless-steel rods of  the  Swagelok  cell.  Cyclic  voltammetry  was  performed  for  three  reversible  cycles  in  the range  of  1.5  or  2.0  V  to  4.0  V  vs.  Li + /Li  with  a  scan  rate  of  0.05  mV  s -1 .  The  oxidative stability was  determined  as  the  intersection  of  two  linear  regression  lines  of  (i)  the background current associated with the double-layer capacitance and (ii) the faradaic anodic (= oxidative) current. In other words, the oxidative stability is determined by the onset of the anodic current.

## Galvanostatic measurements

Symmetric Li/SE/Li  (Sample H )  and  Li/LiB11H14:LiB11H13R'/Li  (Sample A )  cells  were assembled and left at OCV for the first 12 h. For the next 24 h, a constant current of 25 µA cm -2 was applied for 1 h in each direction. In the next step, the current was increased to 50 µA cm -2 while  the  measurement  continued.  After  500  h  of  cycling,  the  applied  time  for  the constant current of 50 µA cm -2 was extended from 1 h to 10 h in each direction. For critical current density (CCD) measurements, a symmetric Li/SE/Li cell was assembled and current density  was  increased  in  each  cycle  while  transferring  a  constant  charge  of  0.2  mAh  cm -2 .

CCD was defined based on the current on the last cycle before a sudden drop in potential response. The sudden drop in potential indicates a short circuit in the cell.

## Battery assembly

All-solid-state Li/SE/TiS2 half cells were prepared by the following procedure: For a cathode composite, TiS2 and SE powders were hand-mixed in a weight ratio of 40:60 for 15 min using mortar and pestle. First, 2.5 mg of the cathode composite (corresponding to mass loading of 0.82 mg cm -2 and areal capacity of 0.2 mAh cm -2 ) was cold-pressed at 40 MPa in a pressing die with a diameter of 12.5 mm using an axial hydraulic press. Subsequently, 90 mg of the SE was  transferred  onto  the  cathode  composite  and  uniaxially  pressed  at  240  MPa.  A  formed single pellet comprised of two layers of the cathode composite and the electrolyte. Finally, a 12 mm Li foil as a reference/counter electrode was placed on the pellet in contact with a SE layer. An Al foil was used as a current collector on the opposite side of the pellet in contact with  the  cathode  composite  layer.  All  the  components  were  assembled  in  a  home-built pressure cell in a glovebox.

The galvanostatic charge-discharge tests for the half cells were carried out under 3.2 MPa at 60  C  in  the  voltage  ranges  of  1.6-2.5  V  vs.  Li + /Li  at  C/10  and  C/5  (1C  =  239  mA  g -1 ), corresponding to 19.5 and 38.9 µA cm -2 ,  respectively. Electrochemical characterization was performed using a Biologic VSP-300 galvanostat/potentiostat.

Figure  S1 (a)  FT-IR-absorption  spectra  and  (b)  PXRD  patterns  ( λ Cu)  of  the  as-received LiB11H14(H2O)n and anhydrous LiB11H14 (Sample E ).

The  H2O  peak  at  ~3500  cm -1 is  completely  removed  after  purification  of  the  LiB11H14 (Figure.S1a, Sample E ). Small peaks at ~2800 cm -1 suggest the remaining of a minor amount of THF solvent used in the synthesis. However, the PXRD pattern of this sample (Figure.S1b, S21a) shows a pure LiB11H14 phase, suggesting a very low content of solvent that does not affect the crystal structure and is below the detection limit of PXRD.

Figure S2 TGA, DSC, and MS data for (a) LiB11H14(H2O)n, (b) purified and dried LiB11H14 (Sample E )  T /  t = 5 °C min -1 and in helium flow.

The  weight  loss  observed  for  the  as-received  LiB11H14(H2O)n  compound  suggests  n  to  be between 1-2. The weight loss upon H2O release is ~18% and the weight loss of ~11 and 20 wt% is expected for n = 1, 2 respectively.

Figure S3 11 B{ 1 H} NMR spectra of mixtures of LiB11H14 with (a) LiCB11H12, (b) Li2B12H12 and (c) LiCB9H10 in CH3CN solution.

Figure S4 Overview of ESI mass spectra of Samples B , C ,  and F dissolved in methanol or acetonitrile. Expanded and enlarged regions of these data sets were used for MS data analysis and identification of anions present in the individual samples (see the main text and below).

In the mass spectrum of Sample F , several 11 B/ 10 B isotopic patterns assignable to the starting material 1 are observed (Table.S2). The most abundant signal at m/z 133.22 in the negative mode ESI mass spectrum corresponds to the B11H14(-) anion, and the most abundant signals at m/z  273.45  and  289.43  to  single  charged  dimeric  clusters  of 1 with  molecular  formulae B22H28Li(-) and B22H28Na(-) (Figure.S5 and S10). It is known that borane salts tend to form clusters [32] and since sodium is ubiquitously present in ESI-MS systems, the formation of the Na + salt is comprehensible.

Figure S5 (-)-ESI-MS of Samples B , C and F zoomed in the region of m/z of 266-282 and calculated isotopic distributions of B22H28Li(-) = {Li[ 1 ] 2 }(-).

## Identification of LiB11H13-OH (2) in Sample F

Figure S6 ( a) Chemical structure with the numbering of boron atoms of 2 (Sample F ), (b) 11 B with  J( 1 H, 11 B)  data,  (c) 1 H and expanded regions  of  (d) 11 B11 B{ 1 H} COSY and (e, f) 1 H1 H{ 11 B}  DQF-COSY  NMR  spectra  recorded  in  CD3CN  solution  with  the  assignment  of resonances to the chemical structure of the B11H13OH anion. Please note that the signals of the starting material LiB11H14 ( 1 ) account to ca. 93.5 mol% of the total signal intensity in the 1 H and 11 B NMR data; amounts of by-products of ca. 6.0 mol% of 2 and 0.5 mol% of 5 (Table.S1, Figure.S17).

Figure S7 (-)-ESI-MS of Samples B and F zoomed into the region m/z of 142-154 and calculated isotopic distributions of the B11H12O(-) ( 5 ) and B11H14O(-) ( 2 ) anions.

## Identification of Li2B11H13-O-B11H13 (3) in Sample B

Figure S8 a) General chemical structure with the numbering of positions of species 2 and 3 , b) 11 B{ 1 H},  c)  and  d) 11 B11 B{ 1 H} COSY NMR spectra of Sample B recorded  in  CD3CN solution with the assignment of resonances. Please note that the starting material 1 is present to ~ 57 mol% and the two by-products 2 and 3 to  25 and 18 mol%, respectively (Table.S1, Figure.S17).

Figure S9 Chemical shift region of bridging protons Hbr of diffusion-edited 1 H NMR spectra recorded with different gradient strengths of (a) Sample B containing species 2 and 3 , and (b) Sample C containing species 3 and 4 . The applied gradient strength is indicated in the graphs and the individual spectra were magnified so that the signals of species 3 remained constant.

Figure S10 (-)-ESI-MS of Samples B and C zoomed in the region of m/z 279-300 and calculated isotopic distributions of Li(B11H13-O-B11H13) -(B22H26LiO(-)) ( 3 ).

Figure S11 (-)-ESI-MS of Samples B and C zoomed in the region of m/z 136-145 and calculated isotopic distribution of B22H26O(2-) = [ 3 ](2-).

## Identification of LiB11H13-O-Bu (4) in Samples C, D

Figure S12 a) General chemical structure with the numbering of positions of species 3 and 4 , b) 11 B{ 1 H}, c) 1 H- 11 B HSQC and d) 11 B11 B{ 1 H} COSY NMR spectra of Sample C recorded in CD3CN solution with the assignment of resonances. Please note that the starting material 1 is present to 61 mol% and the two by-products 3 and 4 to ca. 6 and 33 mol%, respectively (see Figure.S17 and Table.S1). Please note that cross-peaks of 3 in parts c) and d) could only be observed at lower threshold values.

Figure  S13 a)  Chemical  structure  with  the  numbering  of  positions  of  species 4 ,  b) 1 H diffusion-edited, c) 1 H- 1 H{ 11 B} DQF-COSY, d) 1 H- 13 C HSQC, and e) 1 H- 13 C HMBC NMR spectra of Sample C recorded in CD3CN solution with the assignment of resonances. Please note that no cross peak is visible in the red encircled area of the HMBC NMR experiment (such a cross peak mandatory would be expected e.g. for dibutyl ether).

Figure S14 (-)-ESI-MS of (a) Sample C zoomed in the region of m/z 196-214 and calculated isotopic  distributions  of  B11H22C4O(-)  =  [ 4 ](-).  (b)  Sample D zoomed in  the  region  of m/z 204-224 and calculated isotopic distributions of [B11 1 H14 2 H8C4O] = [4](-).

Figure S15 ( a) Chemical structure with the numbering of positions of species 4 prepared by reaction with THF-d8, (b) 11 B{ 1 H} with assignments of positions of chemical species 1 , 3, and 4 in black, blue, and green. (c) 2 H and 2 H{ 1 H} (d) 1 H- 13 C HSQC and (e) 1 H- 13 C HMBC NMR spectra with assignments of positions of the butoxy residue of Sample D (NMR data recorded in CD3CN solution).

When the annealing reaction was carried out using fully deuterated THF (Sample D ), the 11 B NMR resonances are observed for species 1 , 3, and 4 in  Figure.S15b  appear  exactly  at  the same chemical shifts as shown for Sample C in Figure.S12b, meaning that most probably the same  chemical  compounds  must  be  present.  In  the 2 H  NMR  spectrum  (Figure.S15c)  the signals assigned to positions 3' and 4' of the butoxy group both show a splitting due to the spin  coupling  with  the  proton  at  the  CD2H  terminal  methyl  group  (position  4').  In  the 2 H NMR spectrum recorded with 1 H decoupling during acquisition, these two resonances both appear as slightly broadened singlets due to unresolved 2 H- 2 H couplings. Additionally, in the 2 H NMR spectrum the signal assigned to  position  1' of  the  butoxy  group  is  split  into  two nearby  resonances  of  ca.  20:80%  relative  intensities  (Figure.S15c,  ratio  determined  by  line shape analysis, data not shown). The two resonances, therefore, were assigned to a 10 B/ 11 B isotopic effect of the boron at position 7 of the attached B11H13 cage. Finally, in the 1 H- 13 C HSQC and HMBC NMR spectra (Figure.S15d and e, for comparison with the corresponding data of 4 formed with 'normal' THF see Figure.S13d and e) only correlations related to the proton attached to position 4' of the butoxy group were observed. These data prove that the butoxy  group  must  be  present  as  O-CD2-CD2-CD2-CD2H.  The  MS  data  of  this  sample  is presented in Figure.S14b and confirms the composition.

## Identification of LiB11H12O (5) in Sample F

In  Sample F the  presence  of  LiB11H12O  ( 5 )  as  a  minor  by-product  was  verified  by  the observed 1 H- 11 B  HSQC  correlations  on  a  very  low  threshold  (Figure.S16)  with  almost identical chemical shifts as reported in the literature.[1, 2]

Figure  S16  ( a)  Chemical  structure  with  the  numbering  of  positions  of  B11H12O(-)  ( 5 )  as described in Ref. [3,4] and (b) expanded region with a very low threshold of the 1 H- 11 B HSQC NMR spectrum of Sample F recorded in CD3CN solution. Please note that for comparisons reasons the numbering of positions was adapted to the general chemical structure shown in Figure.1a (see the main text).

Figure  S19 PXRD patterns of synthesized  LiB11H14 (Samples E or G )  in  comparison  with those obtained from the solved structure and the DFT optimized structures (  Cu).

Figure S20 (a) Cubic closed packing (CCP) of the anions in the LT-LiB11H14 structure. A-BC layers of the anions are represented by blue, red, and green B11H14 -units. (b) The proposed 3D conduction channels in the HT-LiB11H14 structure.

Figure  S21 (a)  Visualization  of  Li  diffusion  path  for  LiB11H14.  The  solved  structure  of LiB11H14 is presented on the left side for comparison. Calculated diffusion of Li in LiB11H14 structure  (middle),  and  with  partially  substituted  CB9H10 -(right,  the  octahedron  involved  in the conduction path and anion rotations are not shown for clarity.) (b) Calculated activation energies and barriers for Li hoping within edge-sharing tetrahedrons in the structure. For these calculations, the crystal  structure of  LT  LiB11H14 ( T = 25  C) is used. (c) Calculated mean square displacement (msd) of Li in LiB11H14 structure, and with partially substituted CB9H10 -.

Figure.S21b shows lower activation energy for the mixed anion system containing [CB9H10] -. All barriers are calculated for cations motion in the vicinity of the guest anion. Although the energy  barrier  differences  might  be  too  small  to  be  conclusive,  an  important  difference between pure LiB11H14 and partially CB9H10 -substituted is observed, and it is related to strong correlations of Li + - Li + motion in the system with [CB9H10] -. Moreover, molecular dynamic calculations show a higher displacement of Li when B11H14 -anion is partially replaced with CB9H10 -indicative of higher mobility of Li in this structure.

More thorough studies are required to clarify the conduction mechanisms in these compounds that appear to be more complex than in closo-hydroborates.

Figure  S33 The  possible  decomposition  pathways  of  the  hydroborate-based  electrolytes. Agglomeration  of  LiB11H14  is  allowed  and  can  lead  to  the  neutral  (upper  left)  or  charged (upper  right)  complexes  upon  oxidation.  For  these  cases,  the  Li/B  ratio  decreases.  On  the other  hand,  the  reduction  of  the  electrolyte  leads  to  the  increase  in  the  Li/B  ratio,  where  a simple alternation of the hydroborates by detachment/attachment of protons is possible.

## Chapter 9

## Conclusion and Perspectives

In this thesis, we studied the electrochemical and thermal stability of hydroborate solid electrolytes and their compatibility with cathode active materials for the implementation into high-voltage all-solid-state batteries.

We first established a robust methodology to determine electrochemical oxidative stability  of  hydroborates  with  voltammetric  methods.  In  the  model  case  of  LiBH 4 ,  the experimental  oxidative  stability  limit  agrees  with  computational  predictions  by  firstprinciples calculations. This methodology is found to be applicable to other hydroborate solid electrolytes, which can also be extended to other material classes such as sulfides and halides. Utilizing the applicability of this method, we further characterized the interface passivation functionality of a mixed-anion closo -hydroborate solid electrolyte Na4(CB11H12)2(B12H12) with a 4 V-class cathode Na3(VOPO4)2F. We discovered cathode/electrolyte interface passivation by the formation of an ion-conducting interphase derived from the partial electrochemical decomposition of the electrolyte. This enabled the first long-term cycling of 4 V hydroborate-based all-solid-state batteries at  room temperature  with  an  excellent  capacity  and  energy  retention  for  &gt;800  cycles.  The Na3(VOPO4)2F|Na4(CB11H12)2(B12H12)|Na cells record the highest average discharge cell voltage  of  3.8  V  and  cathode-based  specific  energy  among  all  reported  all-solid-state sodium batteries.

We  found  similar  electrochemical  oxidation  behaviors  in  another  mixed-anion closo -hydroborate Na4(B12H12)(B10H10). Moreover, due to its intrinsic thermal stability, this hydroborate  solid  electrolyte  is  shown  to  be  more  compatible  with  layered  sodium transition metal oxide cathodes NaTMO2 (TM = Cr, Mn, Fe) than sulfide and selenide solid electrolytes. Especially, the NaCrO2 cathode composite exhibits high decomposition temperatures and minimal heat generation at both discharged and charged states. This evidences high interface compatibility and a possible wide operating temperature range of closo -hydroborates, surpassing the previous thermodynamic calculation results. Additionally, the investigation of nido -hydroborates shows an alternative approach to high ionic conductivity, the potential for cost-effective mass production, and the importance of structural  stability  of  hydroborates  for  high  thermal  stability  and  wide  electrochemical stability windows.

From these studies above, we demonstrate that hydroborates combine attractive material properties suitable to solid electrolytes, and that their technological advantages enable high-voltage all-solid-state batteries that mark the highest cell voltage reported in the sodium-based systems. Of course, hydroborate-based all-solid-state batteries possess great potential for further development. Although our studies achieved high cathode-based specific  energy, cell-level specific  energy and  energy density remain  to be improved by minimizing  the  amount  of  solid  electrolyte  and  metal  anode  with  an  upgraded  cell fabrication procedure. Also, specific power and power density have room for improvement mainly  by  suppressing  dendrite  formation  and  propagation  on  the  anode.  Moreover, electrolyte interface protection with an artificial layer/coating may widen the possibility of stable battery cycling beyond the electrochemical stability window of hydroborate solid electrolytes, in analogy to sulfide cases. On the other hand, a dramatic increase in materiallevel specific energy in the sodium-based systems would require the development of high-

voltage and high-capacity cathode active materials and of "sodium-metal-free" anodes in the discharged state.

The development of novel hydroborate solid electrolytes based on certain design principles is another important research target. Adjusting the cation-to-anion radius ratio and  the  related  cation  diffusion  pathways  helps  to  enhance  the  ionic  conductivity  of hydroborates from the structural aspect. [1] For Li + conduction, it is proposed in Chapter 8 to reduce the cluster size of a hydroborate anion. One of the candidates in this strategy is to employ closo -monocarbaborates [CB n -1H n ] -with smaller cages ( n = 7-9), which have already been synthesized experimentally. [2-4] According to the theoretical calculations, this approach  may  sacrifice  the  stability  of  the  anion  cluster, [5] which  would  have  to  be mitigated  by  chemical  modifications,  anion  mixing,  or  artificial  interface  protection. Alternatively,  the  incorporation  of  two  carbon  (C)  atoms  or  another  heteroatom  in hydroborates can simultaneously break the symmetry of hydroborate cages and reduce the density  of  cations,  which  can  be  used  as  a  component  for  anion  mixing.  Potential candidates include C2B n -2H n , NB n -1H n , OB n -1H n -1, SB n -1H n -1, PB n -1H n , and [SiB n -1H n ] -( n = 10,  12).  Many  of  them  are  only  computationally  modelled, [6-8] but  1,2-,  1,6-  and  1,10C2B8H10, [9] 1,2-,  1,7-  and  1,12-C 2 B10 H12, [10] 1-NB9H10, [11] NB11 H12, [12] 1-SB 9 H9, [13] and  1SB11H11 [14,15] have already been synthesized experimentally. Mixing these neutral heteroboranes or negatively charged heteroborates with closo -hydroborates or monocarbaborates might lower the activation barrier of anionic reorientational motion, the phase-transition temperature, and the number of cation per formula unit to less than one, leading to enhanced ionic conductivity. Chemical modifications of exo -hydrogen (H) atoms  with  a  non-polar  functional  group  in closo -hydroborates  and  monocarbaborates would also be effective to increase anion asymmetry and dynamic frustration. Overall, a combination of (i) cluster size and shape of hydroborates, (ii) the number, species, and position of heteroatoms, (iii) the number, species, and position of chemical modifications of exo -H  atoms,  and  (iv)  binary  or  ternary  anion  mixing  produces  a  vast  number  of possibilities for hydroborate derivatives and their isomers. Computational studies offer a powerful tool to pre-screen such a large number of combinations to seek for better solid electrolytes; therefore, close collaboration and discussion between scientists in theory and experiments play a more and more important role in exploring an oceanic playground of hydroborate compounds for high ion conduction and structural stability.

In  summary,  the  continuous  research  on  hydroborate  solid  electrolytes  and hydroborate-based all-solid-state batteries creates a substantial impact on the development of next-generation energy storage devices, facilitating the sustainable energy transition in the long run. Furthermore, these efforts also contribute to pushing the boundary of boron chemistry and its applications to other fields e.g. in medicine. [16-20]
